The Red Page
Your Soul; II.
The theory that your soul is a separate
unity meets a serious obstacle in the recent
discovery of the mysterious phenomena of
multiple personality. There are authenti-
cated cases of two or more totally different
selves manifesting themselves in turn in the
same body. Of the most remarkable of these
cases Pearson provides extraordinary details.
This is the case of Miss Beauchamp, described
by Dr. Morton Prince in The Dissociation
of a Personality. The patient was highly
neurasthenic, and in course of time four
different personalities appeared in her. Her
personality would change from hour to hour,
and with each change her character became
transformed. The- most remarkable person-
ality that appeared called herself “Sally.”
She enjoyed rude health and was full of fun,
vitality and mischief. When the three other
personalities were off the stage—or as Sally
put it, "dead” —they did not know what was
happening ; .but Sally’s existence ran on con-
comitantly with them all. She was never
“dead.” Three of the four personalities into
which Miss Beauchamp split up the doctor
called the saint, the woman and the devil.
B 1 closely x’esembled the typical saint of
literature. B 4 had all the frailties of tem-
per and self-interest of the average woman.
Sally was the devil. .She used to torment
the other selves, into which she could change
whenever she liked. She used to write B 1
letters td. annoy her, made B 1 tell lies,
sewed up her clothes at night, and played
other tricks upon the saint. She used to
keep the doctor informed of all that went on
in “the family,” as she called it. Once B 4
“awoke to find herself perched upon a shaky
structure composed of a couch, two chairs
and a dress-suit case. She was naked, and
iff an attitude as if posing for a statue.”
She could not move, but was able to cry
out. Sally had evidently got into that posi-
tion, and then changed into the unfortunate
84. She even scratched her own arms,
rubbed' lemon-juice into the scratches, and
then let B 4 wake up and feel the pain. But
she was sometimes quite good-natured. When
B 1 was delirious, Sally popped up at fre-
quent intervals, quite sane, and offered to
act as nurse, and to “come” at intervals to
take the food that Miss Beauchamp in her
delirium refused. Sally even wrote her auto-
biography. But in order to cure Miss Beau-
champ, Sally had to be suppressed, or, as she
put it, “sent back to where she came from.”
Seems a pity. But one proof of her identity
with the other personalities is striking.
Whenever Sally thought deeply on intellec-
tual subjects, she couldn’t help changing into
B 1 or 84. Evidently Sally was of sublimi-
nal origin. She insisted that she never slept,
she had a marvellous memory, was never
tired, and had a very imperfect sense of
time, like a child. Pearson, obviously per-
plexed by these phenomena, asserts that
Sally does not disprove the essential unity
of the soul.
Jit
As Pearson is committed to his theory he
has to admit that the soul must possess
secondary sexual attributes. A very inter-
esting chapter sums and examines the latest
theories of the causation of sex. “Every
human being has a soul which corresponds to
and registers the experience of the body with
which it is associated; and consequently, in
the matter of sex, every human being will
have a male or a female soul. Many of the
qualities now distinctive of sex have a value
which is wholly independent of the physical
function of reproduction, and consequently
there is no reason why they should perish
merely because the soul becomes dissociated
from the body.” And if there is to be recog-
nition between discarnate souls, it is impera-
tive that their essential qualities should per-
sist, and sex is perhaps the most important.
But Pearson does not suggest any way out
of the difficulty that at once arises. A child
loses his mother when he is an infant; he
grows up, marries and has children; his
wife dies; finally he dies. If there is recog-
nition of souls after death, his mother will
look to find her infant child; his wife will
want her lover and husband; Lis children
will seek their father. Can that soul be all
these B,t odcg V
Pearson is on more stable ground when
he suggests that the idea of a peaceful eter-
nity for souls is opposed to the principle of
evolution. Why shouldn’t evolution keep on
evoluting? The whole tendency of evolution
is to diverge into the various, not to converge
into the one. “We may reasonably infer
that the aim of evolution is a host of varied
personalities rather than an all-absorbing
one-ness in which individuality would be lost,
and that the power of survival enjoyed by
the mind-structure of the hydrogen atom will
not be denied to the human soul.”
,S*
Given his .theory, Pearson is compelled to
believe in reincarnation. But here he is up
against the difficulty of mental heredity. He
meets that obstacle by his assertion that your
soul, after death, will be drawn to a brain
fitted with the special sort of machinery
that will suit your soul-needs. But then,
we may ask, where would Leonardo da Vinci,
or Butters, or Shakspeare, or William Morris
Hughes find a new brain-machinery that
would enable those souls to express them-
selves’ Where will you discover another
brain that will suit vour own personality?
How tiie diScaruate soul is fitted to a new
brain Pearson cannot say, though he sug-
gests the possibility of something akin to
chemical affinity, which is really electrical,
or to some sort of harmony of vibration
between the two.
But the reincarnation of your soul will be
no good to you, you say, unless you remem-
ber your past. Pearson warns you to beware.
For that memory of your past that you wish
to retain would not be confined to your
latest life, it would extend back to a million
lives, back to the ape, the cytode, the atom.
“Over some few of his latest existences a
man might possibly linger with pleasure and
interest; but as he tracked his soul down-
ward through the darkness he would be faced
with all the grim story of its early career.
He would see himself revelling in a welter
of brutality and bloodshed, barely human in
form, and purely bestial in appetite.” Merci-
fully that door is closed. We still have the
water of some old ocean in our blood; the
germ-plasms of lower forms of life are woven
into our bodies and our brains. But while
these ancient elements in cur being are,slowly
dying out, nothing in the soul can be simi-
larly eliminated The soul records every-
thing ever happened to it. Better not ask
for memory to stimulate it.
Another difficulty is the improbability that
one existence should follow immediately upon
another. Pearson falls back upon Theoso-
phy, which allows the soul a long interval of
repose between births.
A remarkable essay in metaphysics, full
of interesting fact and ingenious theory; but
though to this page the author leaves his
elaborate theory non-proven, it is the sort of
theory for which absolute scientific proof
may ever be found. Yet the author has cer-
tainly tackled the tremendous subject from a
new and highly promising standpoint, the
doctrine of the evolution of matter. Though
in some of its details the theory evokes
scepticism and even laughter, the argument,
in its broad aspect, is a serious and valuable
contribution to an extremely difficult subject.
War
“The present war is a contest between
psychological forces. Irreconcilable ideals
are grappling with each other,” declares
Gustave le Bon, in The Psychology of the
Great War (Unwin). “Material weapons
are being utilised in the struggle, but they
are wielded by psychological forces.... All
nations possess an aggregate of inherited
feelings, which occasion those inextinguish-
able race-hatreds that are among the chief
causes of the European War.” And the
author goes on to develop his theory of the
crowd, of which he is the foremost exponent.
Briefly this theory may be put thus: The
mentality of men in crowds is absolutely
unlike that which they possess when iso-
lated, for an assemblage of men is as differ-
ent from the individuals of whom it is made
up as is any living being from its component
cells. This is a crowd war ; it is the entire
German nation which is fighting to-day, not
a sovereign or a military caste. The col-
lective military spirit of the nation had
already completely destroyed the civio
spirit. “The individual mind has only an
ephemeral existence, but the mind of the
race is permanent and not subject to disin-
tegration .... The history of civilised nations
is the story of their efforts to acquire this
permanent mind.... It is no exaggeration to
say that the fiercest fighting upon our fields
of battle is due to the innumerable hosts of
the dead more than to the living.” Every-
body possesses both an individual mind and
an ancestral mind, the former for use in
our daily actions, the latter to control and
guide us" upon great national occasions.
The German mind, however, has been
carefully transformed from the individual
mind into a collective mind. The German
thinks collectively, not only on great occa-
sions when he is under the emotional sway
of the crowd, but on everyday occasions.
The mental unification of a nation has never
before been carried to such an extent. All
Germans are the same German. The psycho-
logical unification of the masses is complete,
A single brain thinks inside 70 million heads.
Le Bon’s summary of the remote causes
of the war shows the small influence which
was exerted by rational elements at the
beginning of the war. If Germany had
thought, and not felt, about the war, she
would never have precipitated it. Germany
had already won her war of industry and
commerce; already she was the peaceful
conqueror of Europe. From a rational point
of view Germany was interested in prevent-
ing war, but mystic influence dominated her
entirely; and everything yields_ to those
deeper forces within us. “There is no more
touching sight than the struggles of. the
peace-loving diplomats who were pitted
against the haughty forces which were driv-
ing them into a terrible war. Never in the
course of the ages has there, perhaps, been a
better opportunity of seeing how men’s con-
duct is sometimes dominated by unconscious
influences whose pressure is so great that no
will can withstand them.”
J*
Answering the question, “Who wanted the
war?” the author comes to the conclusion
that no one did. But since war was declared
by the Kaiser, the responsibility for the war
is his. Le Bon thinks that though Germany
had made war certain by her increasing’
armaments and the arrogance of her diplo-
macy, she didn’t want it just then. He men-
tions the curious fact that on July 26,
1914, the German fleet was manoeuvering at
a great distance from its base, and could
not have coaled or taken on its full crews
if the British fleet had interfered. The imme-
diate cause of the conflict le Bon puts down
to “the mutual distrust of the three Emper-
ors.” The Kaiser was the most suspicious
of all, and that is why he will be held re-
sponsible for the war, since it was he who
made up his mind to declare it. He is also
to be held accountable for the atrocities,
“because no general would have dared to
order them without his permission.”
Discussing the difficulties of a permanent
peace, the author concludes that when Ger-
many has been sufficiently ruined industrially
and financially, the German will perhaps
acquire a few useful concepts, the first two
of which are: “It is not quite certain that
the Germans have been chosen by God to
devastate the world and then to reign over
it,” and “To massacre neighbors whom one
detests and to burn their monuments and
their cities is no doubt a very great pleasure ;
but it is a pleasure which costs extremely
dear in both men and money.” These truths
are obvious to all but the Germans, and le
Bon thinks that many millions of men must
perish before such truths can become motives
of conduct for the Germans. “A nation
chosen by God to conquer and regenerate the
world does not readily abandon such a mis-
sion, and Germany will not relinquish it
until she has been defeated many times.”
And the author ends on a grave note:
“The civilised nations are now exposed to
far more formidable perils than the loss of
their wealth and the vanishing of the best
elements among their populations; for they
are really threatened with the disappear-
ance of certain virtues which have been
acquired gradually and which have hitherto
been the glory and delight of civilisation,
but which have now become a danger to
those who possess them, and hence seem
doomed to pass away. Loyalty, integrity,
tenderness, respect for a promise and a
treaty, or, in a word, all the various forms
of honor, have become a source of weakness
in a struggle against nations which do not
respect such things, and perhaps they will
be unable to hold their own in the future;
but on the day when all these virtues have
perished forever, and when blind forces shall
rule the world, mankind will be condemned
to the most barbarous retrogression, and the
centres of civilisation will inevitably shift.”
J*
Is it humanly impossible for Philip Gibbs
to write another book on the war as fine as
The Soul of the War. In The Battles of the
Somme (Heinemann, per Dymock’s Library)
he implicitly admits as much. For this
later work is no attempt to probe further
into the soul of the war, but is merely a
reprint of Ms articles sent from the battle-
field, “pages written on the evenings of
battle, hastily and sometimes feverishly,
after days of intense experience and tiring
sensation.” For The Soul of the War was
written when Gibbs was a free-lance, and
now he is an officially accredited correspondent
with the British armies. His writing is as
vigorous as ever, but the book lacks the
breadth of his first work. He has become
a war-correspondent, he is no longer a
thoughtful literary artist. He has a good
deal to say about “those bronzed, hatchet-
faced, handsome fellows who brought a new
character of splendid manhood into the
medley of British types,” and their work in
the taking of Poziferes.
Incidentally oibbs denies the allegation
that war-correspondents are “spoon-fed”
with documents and facts given to them by
General Headquarters. “All we get from the
General Staff are the brief bulletins of the
various army corps, a line or two of hard
news about the capture or loss of this or
that trench such as appears afterwards in
the official communiques. For all the details
of an action we have to rely upon our own
efforts in the actual theatre of operations
day by day, seeing as much of the battle as
it is possible to see talking to the walk-
ing wounded and the prisoners, the_ men
going in and the men coming out, going to
headquarters.... for exact information as to
the progress of the battle, and getting in
touch as soon as possible with the battalions
actually engaged. All this is not as easy
as it sounds. It is not done without fatigue
and mental as well as physical strain. But
we have full facilities for seeing things, and
see more and know more of the whole battle-
line than is possible even to Divisional Gen-
erals. For we have a pass enabling us to
go to any part of the Front at any time and
get the facts and points of view from every
class and rank, from the trenches to G.H.Q.
Because the correspondents • sometimes tell
the same stories it is because we tell them
to each other, not believing in professional
rivalry in a war of this greatness.” But
he confesses that his articles do not tell all
the truth. He has had to spare the feelings
of men and women who have sons and hus-
bands still lighting in France. “I have not
told all there is to tell about the agonies of
this war, nor given in full realism the hor-
rors that are inevitable in such fighting.”
Gibbs tells of the prisoners taken at
Pozieres that they regarded themselves as
sheep sent to the slaughter. “They knew
only that the Australians were in front of
them, and from what they have heard of
the Australians they did not have much
hope.” And he adds: "The Australians are
still fighting in a way which wins the admi-
ration of their Generals and Staff and of all
the army. These clean-cut men, so fine in
physique and appearance that one always
turns to look at them in any street of war,
are not stolid fellows who can stand the test
of shell-fire without suffering in spirit. They
are highly strung and sensitive, with a more
nervous temperament than many of our Eng-
lish soldiers, but they have a pride and an
heroic quality that keeps them steady, and
an intelligence in the individual as well as
in mass which makes them great soldiers.”
Jt
In John Buchan’s lucidly written The
Battle of the Somme: ,First Phase (Nelson,
per Sydney Book Club, Is. fid.) the author
has similar praise for Billjim. “The Aus-
tralian Corps was second to none in the new’
British Army.” After the capture ot
PoziSres, Buchan says: “Out of the dust
and glare would come Australian units
which had been relieved, long, lean men with
the shadows of a great fatigue around their
deep-set far-sighted eyes. They were per-
fectly cheerful and composed, and no Low-
land Scot was ever less inclined to expan-
sive speech. At the most they would admit
in their slow quiet voioes that what they
had been through had been ‘some battle.’
Buchan declares that the first battle of
the Somme ended in victory ; for his argu-
ment is that the success or defeat of a
strategic purpose is the sole test. Judging
by this, Tannenberg was a victory for Ger-
many, the Marne for France, and the first
battle of Ypres for Britain. The- battle of
the Somme was no less a victory, since it
achieved the Allied purpose and frustrated
that of the enemy.
J*
Leonid Andreyeff, the Russian dramatist,
adventures in a different genre in his Con-
fessions of a Little Man During Great Days
(Duckworth, per Dymock’s Library). This
is an intimate diary of a typical Petrograd
minor bank official during the war. The
soul of this insignificant atom, reacting to the
awfulness of the W'ar, is portrayed with an
exquisite tenderness. “I am 45 years old,”
he complacently writes, “and no matter what
happens, I will never under any circum-
stances be called to the colors. This is a
thing it would hardly be safe to say to others.
I hax’e to be somewhat of a humbug at times
and pretend, as all the rest do, that if I
were younger and stronger and so on, I
should most certainly join as a volunteer, but
at bottom I can’t help rejoicing that I can
stop at home and not have to expose myself
to some silly bullet.” You see, he is abso-
lutely truthful —in his diary. He is firmly
convinced that he doesn’t like this war at
all. “When I imagine myself standing in
some clear field at the Front, men aiming
at me with rifle and gun with intent to kill
-—-aiming, straining, bursting to hit me—-I
find it ridiculous; it seems like some silly
practical j0ke....1 have never harmed any-
one in my life, and I have a perfect right
to demand that no one shall harm me by
shooting me down like a sparrow. I didn’t
want the war. Wilhelm did not send his
ambassadors to me to find out if I wanted
to fight; he just said ‘fight,’ and that’s all.”
But the war declines to let him alone. His
wife joins a war hospital and neglects her
home and children. “The war has no right
to thrust itself among us like a brigand, and
lay waste our home.” His brother-in-law. is
killed; the bereaved mother comes to live
with them. “A huge pair of hands seem to
hold me in their grasp, moulding me into
some fantastic shape, hands that are too
strong for resistance.” His little daughter
dies; there are so few doctors left that she
is attended to too late. His bank fails and
he loses his job. He nearly goes out of his
mind. Awful details of the massacre of the
Armenians prostrate him —and Andreyeff
knows how to wring the last drop of horror
from, the gruesome. He determines to com-
mit, suicide. But at the last moment illumi-
nation comes to him ; he sees himself in all
his callous selfishness; and he volunteers for
the Front in an ambulance unit.
So simple and moving is this ordinary tale
that the reader hardly suspects the great art
of the author. For the. soul that Andreyeff
so startlingly illuminates is not the soul of
a Russian bank-clerk but the soul of you
and me. Or it ought to be.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pan-Folk.
Do you remember a radiant day,
With neither woman nor man to thank
For the purple shadows among the trees
And the filching hands of the perfumed
breeze
In the flowers on the bank,
When Pan came galloping through your life?
Swift and soft as an eyelid blinks,
Ilis hoofs and hair were real to you,
Ills bright face laughed the brown trunks
through,
And you heard the wild syrinx.
It might have been in the hush of reeds,
Where naiads hid by the river’s brim,
You saw the prick of a pointed ear
And knew at last that the gods drew near —
And turned.... to welcome him !
Do you remember the red-lipped sun
Lifting over the ragged crests,
The pigeon cooing somewhere unseen,
The dart of a swallow’s flight between,
And the mossy woven nests?
A broken leaf in a bed of clay,
The smell of resin and sap and dew,
And the crooked path in the timber set
And the fragile bluebell with petals wet,
By the way Pan came to you?
Do you remember the hot, still noon,
The gum-leaves crushed and the far off
smoke,
How close you lay to the friendly earth
And whispered her of the marvellous birth
On the day that Pan awoke?
Do you remember the yellow eve, *
The sun reluctant to go so soon,
The pine boughs singing a lullaby
And suddenly, in the empty sky,
The shadow of the moon ?
Do you remember the first white star
That like the light of a far ship shone,
The rushy fringes that seemed to sigh
The sob of a dying melody
Till you knew that Pan had gone?
Oh, not to everyone does he come !
The gods lift veils for a favored few ;
But I saw hinr once —yes, that I vow !
Though it may be a hundred years from
now!
I saw hipi close —did you?
Then you march to the call of a single pipe
Apart from the human caravan,
Keep pace to a rebel rhapsody—
For never the same can the boundaries be
When you follow the feet of Pan!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
April Laughter.
April is blowing her bubbles of blue
O’er coverts of daisies all dappled with dew,
And wily wind elves as they pelt her with
pla-y,
Sing, “Hi! hilly-ho ! It’s a very fine day !
So ho ! for the maiden,
With lily-love laden,
Who’s decking herself in a dimity gown,
With touches of pearl on her orange-wreath
crown,
That cuddles her tresses of wallflower brown !
The cream’s in the cup and the candies are
cut
In tiny pink piles in the honeymoon house,
Where Time has a tub of caresses to drink,
And every white minute a chuckle to clink.
And ho ! for the lover
She soon will discover;
When noon nodding over her goblet of sun
Wakes up with a wink at the sound of the
fun,
To drown in the dusk ere the frolic is done!
Oh, I am the maiden and you are the boy,
Our wedding-bells ring in the attic of Joy,.
Where Youth takes a kiss ’neath a lattice
of Love
From Beauty and Innocence meek as a dove.
Then ho! for the laughter
That rings to the rafter,
As over the ridge of our kisses we l'ide,
And tingling wfith mirth of the oncoming
tide,
We slip into Eden to sleep side by side.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
Glamor.
The high, immortal Powers
Have done me grievous wrong:
They have turned the heart in my breast
To a little fiery song.
They have played a magic upon me
Of old, immutable words,
Of the noon-mist, and the moon-mist,
And the crying of birds.
I have neither sorrow nor joy ;
All things are not as they were;
Because of your lips and your laughter,
And the dark fire of your hair.
N.S.W.
FRITZ S. BURNELL.
Lachrymal.
I saw a woman pleading with wet eyes,
Ere yet the pangs of labor stifled pain,
That he who bought her soul with specious
lies
Might give the child at least his worth-
less name.
Light laughter broke across his scornful lips
And careless words stung like a bitter
frost... .
They found her body ’mid the harbor ships,
And she was counted as a poor thing lost.
Time sped, and to the halls of final doom
A man came leprous with the sins of
years;
And 10, a woman crossed the hopeless gloom
To lave that awful soul with falling tears.
QUEENSLAND.

save money by buying “Plume” Motor
Spirit. When you drive on “Plume” you
drive mostly on air which costs you
nothing, the necessity of a large amount of
air for the perfect combustion of “Plume”
being one of its strong characteristics. No
other spirit takes such a large proportion of
air as
Plume Motor Spirit
Easij Starting—Sweet Running—More Miles
As your carburetter takes only a certain
volume of mixture, the greater the per-
centage of air in the mixture the less your
expense for Motor Spirit. This alone
would prove “Plume” to be the most
economical spirit to use.

How 8% Shortage of Air-Space, in Tyres,
may cost 25% of the CAR life!
SOLID Bubber Tyres would last
much longer on Pleasure Cars
than “Pneumatics.”
Yet nobody so uses them!
Because, what Solid-Bubber or
Steel might save on Tyres, would
represent only a fraction of the
additional Wear and Tear they
would cost on Motor, and Car-
Mechanism—through excessive Vi-
bration, Boad-Shock and “Cruelty”
to Passengers.
The shorter Mileage of Pneu-
matic Tyres must therefore be
balanced against the longer-life of
the CAB and the Luxury, or Com-
fort, PUBPOSE, for which that
CAB was primarily purchased.
* * * *
THE higher the Air-Pressure you
must carry, in Pneumatic
Tyres, the less pneumatic they
are—the nearer they come to the
Solid-Bubber Tyre result, on Car
Mechanism and Car Occupants.
But —per contra —when lowered
Air-Pressure is carried in Tyres
(admitting of more than 14% of
the Tyre Cross-Section diameter
being in-contact-with-the-road) you
not only handicap the power of your
Car, but you Wear out, and Burn
out, Tyres long before their proper
Mileage has been rendered.
So there you are—“between the
Devil and the Deep Sea”!
What to do ?
WHEN your Tyres are Under-
Sized for the Weight of
your Car and its Occu-
pants, you MUST use an exces-
sively-high Air-Pressure in order to
keep off-the-earth more than the
proper 14% of Tyre Cross-Section
Diameter.
That excessively-high Pressure is
then not only a cause of excessive
Vibration, but it also causes an
excessive strain on the Fabric walls
of the Tyre, tending toward prema-
ture “bursts.”
If you carry less Air-Pressure—
to wit, a normal , Comfortable, and
non-vibrating pressure—the exces-
sive load (in proportion to Tyre
Area) wears out your Tyres in short
order, while using up your Petrol
extravagantly.
0, you need the EIGHT SIZE
of Tyre—the FULL-SIZE
specified by the Car Maher —
for satisfactory results.
That is why you should be inter-
ested in knowing that all millimeter
Tyres except GOODKICH are
UNDER- SIZED.
Measure them, and see!
Then bear in mind that Goodrich
FULL-SIZE Millimeter Tyres cost
you no more than same quality of
“regular” (or Under-sized) Tyres.
GOODRICH
FULL-
SIZED
DUVAL & CO.
13 Castlereagh Street, Sydney
145 Collins Street, Melbourne
300 Adelaide Street, Brisbane.

The Bulletin
THE NATIONAL AUSTRALIAN NEWSPAPER.
“Australia for the White Man.”
Printed and published by William Macleod, of
• Musgrave and McLeod Streets, Mosman, for The
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the
Co., 214 George Street, Sydney, N.S.W., Australia.
PUBLISHED ONCE A WEEK.
TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, to any
ADDRESS IN AUSTRALASIA, post paid.
Post free to any address abroad, £1 10s. per
annum.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, .or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to _ be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
To Travellers Abroad.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs. Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.
South Africa. — The Central News Agency, Cape-
town, Durban and Johannesburg; Dawson and Soil,

THURSDAY, MAY 3, 1917.
ON THE RIGHT OF KINGS TO BE
HANGED FOR THEIR OWN GOOD.
The world’s kings grow scarce. With
Russia a Republic and the dynasties of Cen-
tral Europe quaking in the midst of hunger-
strikes and cannon smoke, the emblematic
person with the beard and the gold hat shows
signs of fading almost entirely away. Re-
publican sentiment is strong in many mon-
archical lands; and the near future may see
quiet abdications representing the scared
side-stepping of red and dangerous things
which can no longer be concealed. Even in
England H. G. Wells puts in a plea for
republicanism, or, at any rate, for the free
discussion of republican ideas. Exactly
what he means is not easy to gather. He
seems, however, to suggest that republican-
ism and kingship run very well in double-
harness; and, paradox though it appear, he
is quite right there. Republicanism does not
exclude the idea of kingship. The Ameri-
can President is for the three years of his
office much more a king in the old sense than
the limited monarchs who reign for a life-
time over other democracies and hold their
sceptres by Act of Parliament. The original
king, the Caning, was the person who could
do things; and if kingship consists in doing
things and having some responsible person at
the head of the State, every republican Presi-
dent perpetuates the kingly idea. The mon-
archy is a reality still, whatever name you
give to it and whatever form the government
of a country may take. Our own word
“Commonwealth” is the best possible transla-
tion of the original Latin “respublica” ; yet
the Commonwealth, in spite of the demo-
cratic suggestion in the title, supports seven
vice-regal establishments of a non-elective
character. So, too, the coins of the first
Napoleon bear the inscription, “Republique
Frangaise, Napoleon Empereur.” In getting
down to bedrock it is necessary to recognise
that names in the sphere of government
carry no sure and fixed meaning. Vital
democracy might exist under an alleged
despotism, just as there may be bitter oppres-
sion and the denial of all free citizen rights
under a so-called republic.
*
Very few people realise at the present day
all that we owe to the stern, hard-faced arbi-
ters who sent Charles I. of England to the
scaffold. Other kings had been murdered;
this king, in an age when many supersti-
tions combined to make the royal person
sacred, was tried on evidence, proved a
traitor and put to death. Anybody will tell
you how that shook up the idea of kingship
and weakened it forever. But that is only-
half the story. The execution of Charles,
in point of fact, gave a practical utility to
many of the kings who followed. A king in
his Divine right, sacred and stupid, was a
menace and a nuisance to his people; a king
with the shadow of an axe always over him
became quite a useful working cog in a piece
of more or less democratic machinery.
Turning back to the original kingship as it
was in England, and comparing systems
which have been tried through the ages, it
does not seem as if that early idea had been
bettered much at any time. The West Saxon
line of kings, descendants of Egbert, who
first ruled over a united England, were
elected to their thrones. That the succession
usually followed from father to son is not
difficult to understand when it is remembered
that the members of the Wessex royal family
—gracious-mannered men of big brain and
splendid presence—could be distinguished
anywhere in England and in any company
by their appearance alone. Kings like that
were sure of election before they went to the
poll; but the elective notion persisted, and,
in the time of stress and doubt that marked
the end of the exclusively Saxon rule,
Harold, the grandson of pig-feeders, was
chosen to be King of England. Rude as the
Saxon monarchy was —yet the thing was not
so rude that could keep a patrol fleet of 500
ships of war to prevent the importation of
murder and rapine—it had in it most of the
essentials of the Republic. The people all
had the right to vote if they could get to the
polling-booth; any citizen could elect himself
a member of Parliament and fire off a speech
when he had something to say. The exist-
ence of a king did not prevent these things.
It guaranteed them; and it was to the King
himself that the appeal was always made for
fair-play. Even to-day the need for such a
king exists, as is proved by the fact that
republics always elect a responsible head.
The monarchical idea has been dragged
through the mud of Divine right and sacred
prerogative, bolstered by churchmen, de-
nounced by patriots and generally pampered
and walloped until it hardly knows itself;
but essentially it is sound, and no better
human expedient has yet been evolved.
Under a limited monarchy the hereditary
notion has defeated itself and destroyed most
of the value of the kingship; but a strong,
plain king of the British republic might still
fill a vital part in the scheme of things. The
King still possesses the power of suggestion
and veto; and without being in politics, as
the American President is, he could influence
administration and add to national security.
In a word, the re-introduction of the old
English elective principle into the business
of the kingship would bring limited mon-
archy close up to ideal republicanism.
* * * *
But the most important reform necessary
in connection with the modern kingship is
the restoration of personal responsibility.
In the tremendous matters of peace and
war every monarch, whether called president,
emperor or king, has at least theoretically
the power of veto over the acts of his Min-
isters. That power which carries with it
the issue of life and death to millions of
people should be made a life-and-death mat-
ter to the person possessing it also. As
things are to-day the nation may suffer for
the rashness or ambition of its official head;
but the power really responsible meets no
punishment greater than a compulsory retire-
ment from his job—usually on a very liberal
allowance. The shadow of the axe which
hung over the successors of Charles I. has
almost faded away and kings can treat their
responsibilities as lightly as they will. In
the present terrific controversy of steel and
fire the Kaiser stands as the central figure.
Just what his personal part in the affairs
that led up to the war may have been it
is impossible to say with certainty ; but it
can be said without a shadow of doubt or
hesitation that Wilhelm could have pre-
vented the whole calamity. His crime lies
in that he did not prevent it, but whooped
loudly and continuously for the murder of
nations. For the good of the future and
the benefit of all kings, emperors, presidents
and potentates to follow, Wilhelm should be
hanged—hanged without ornament or cir-
cumstance in a common prison yard as a
common felon. Thereafter the right of
kings to be hanged if they cause or permit
crimes against humanity must be solemnly
established. The three-years king of a
republic or the life-president of a monarchi-
cal State must make his decisions with the
rope in full view, arid with a complete
understanding of the personal discomfort of
an untidy death if those decisions are on the
side of international crime. In this way
the king may be made a most potent factor
for peace. The Minister at the council
table of such a sovereign dare not urge war.
To do so would be to slip the potential noose
over his chief’s head. Arguments for peace
might be advanced; but if the situation
seemed to demand war, the most that any
statesman could do would be to put the
facts plainly before the person most con-
cerned and leave him to decide. When a
king or president, with the tremendous
weight of his responsibility forced home by
his own personal dread of consequences, did
turn down a thumb and order out the battle-
gong, it might be taken for granted that
the cause was of no common sort; but the
very fact that such was the case would
probably drive the rival potentate at the
head of the opposing nation to prayer and
dove-flying. Once the principle was firmly
established kings would rejoice in the new
arrangement and count the right to be hanged
as among their choicest privileges. Also the
king who wasn’t a king would side-step his
job and doff his gold hat with great enthusi-
asm in the light of a responsibility that
carried a rope in its outfit. Let us talk
about republicanism by all means, about
kingship, too, and the essential facts of
leadership. But. the shadow of the gallows
on the screen of international politics is the
thing to which the immediate future may
look for protection against royal fireworks
and ambitious throat-cutting.
THE PARTY THAT WAS BORN IN
THE GUTTER AND HAS NEVER
GOT OUT OF IT.
Convention is one of the strongest forces
in the world. We do all sorts of foolish
things because other people, whom we think
a little bit better socially or otherwise than
ourselves, do them. In every general elec-
tion tens of thousands of votes are thus cast
on one side or other for no better reason than
that it is regarded as the “proper thing” to
cast them that way. The cunning election
manager therefore strives to show that his
crowd is the “respectable” party, for whom
all the “best people” should vote. This
paper is not much concerned as to how the
“best people” will vote on Saturday. The
so-called best people are, as a rule, socially
and nationally the most worthless people;
and the Freetrade, anti-Australian party led
by Joe Cook seems fairly entitled to them.
But to those who think that the Phewsion
party is the “respectable” party in this elec-
tion, in the sense of being the party of
decency, this paper is interested in saying a
little.
When War struck Australia the electors
found themselves with a Liberal Govern-
ment. There was a general election. That
Government was thrown out and passed
into Opposition with the explanation that its
sole object in life would be to assist the in-
coming Ministry in prosecuting the war and
everything incidental thereto and in preserv-
ing unity of purpose among tlie citizens of
the Commonwealth. For a couple of years
it maintained this attitude, at times pomp-
ously drawing attention to the correctness
of its pose. “Let us not waste time quarrel-
ling about other things, but sustain one
another in the good work. We have no
fault to find with the Government. It is
doing fine. Every man and woman should
be behind the Government, trusting it in
this hour of great trial. We must be Aus-
tralians first and stand a united people and
Parliament against the common enemy.”
That was the daily sermon of Liberalism.
Then the earthquake happened, and a Min-
istry that was a jest even to itself emerged
from the disturbance signalling for assist-
ance. “Aha ! This looks like where we come
in,” mused the Liberal junta. And, after
hard bargaining, it came in. To assist the
Government? To unite the people? Nothing
of the sort! Liberalism forgot all about its
former tests of virtue. On all patriots
there now suddenly devolved the duty of
hating Labor like the Devil. There was
nothing now about having a “united front”
or “standing together.” The main thing
was to loathe everything but the Phewsiou,
and everybody who omitted to feel that way
became a sympathiser with the Germans, a
pariah, a scoundrel whose guilt clamored
for vengeance. Men who a few months ago
were “proud to co-operate” with the Minis-
try then in office; men who were personally-
identified with that Ministry and extollhd
the personal and collective patriotism of
their mates; newspapers which insisted that
the Ministry and Parliament were entitled
to the support and confidence of the citizens
-—all these men and most of these papers
are now howling themselves hoarse that the
Phewsionists are the only true-blue patriots
and that people who are not Libs, care
nothing for their country.
The Bulletin holds that this campaign
of vilification and perjury not only is not
respectable but is grossly indecent—that a
political party seeking to gain momentary
advantage by raising such an issue is morally
guilty of treason. Men who would make
party a test of loyalty cannot be patriots.
They are either too small and too narrow-
minded or they are plainly and bluntly dis-
honest men, prepared to use any 7 means at
all to gain their own ends. That they have
disgusted and alarmed the more intelligent
and serious section of the electors who
usually vote on that side is notorious. Even
among themselves there are men whose con-
ceptions of the fair deal have suffered daily
outrage during the progress of this con-
spiracy to win an election by stirring the
passions and prejudices of the people with
lies of the basest kind. They know, and know
well, that the individual who charges his
neighbor with being an enemy of Australia
because he rejects the gospel of Cook is
worse than a mere liar, for such an
individual is sowing seeds of which the har-
vest may be more perplexing than all the
prickly pear in Bananaland. This paper
has never had any doubts about the serious-
ness of this war to Australia, nor wavered
in its opinion that the people of the country
should fight with all their soul against the
enemy who assailed them. Everything lias
been at stake and is so still. It has been a
cause of the gravest regret to The Bulle-
tin that the people of Australia have for
the most part been treated as children in rela-
tion to the struggle, and that the State and
Commonwealth Parliaments have dismally
failed to discharge their obligations to the
nation, not only in respect to the enlistment
of men for service (on that point there is
less room for censure than on any other),
but over the whole field of administration
and finance and preparation for the problems
of peace created for Australia by the march
of external events. Criticism of Parliaments
on this score springs legitimately from con-
siderations of public policy. Ministries may
be mistaken ; they may take a wrong view;
they may be fat-headed, lazy, feeble. But
to charge them or any other body of public
men or political parties with being traitors,
anxious to lose the war, conspirators against
the public safety, is another thing altogether. It
is this that the Phewsion is doing. It has
gone up and down the country pouring out
poison-gas against its political opponents;
bawling that it is the Win-the-War party ;
asking the electors to choose between being-
followers of Cook and Hughes and adher-
ents of a criminal and disloyal and pro-
German organisation. The Phewsion with
this dishonest and unpatriotic and greatly
harmful pretence covers the history of its
own formation, the story of those weeks of
ignoble negotiation, the facts to winch the
men and women it defames can point in
answer to the deluge of slander poured upon
them —it covers up all these things behind
its cry of “Vote for Fusion and win the
war.” No dirtier, no more dishonest cry
has ever been raised in Australia. No more
sinister untruth has ever been suggested.
[To express the views of The Bulletin, by S. H.
Prior, F. J. Kirby and D. M. Wright, 214 George
Street, Sydney.]
MY UN-SELECTORATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Other men have Commonwealth electorates;
I have a Commonwealth rejectorate in
Bourke, that Victorian constituency which
includes the Germanically-named swell
suburbs of Brunswick, Coburg and Heidel-
berg. Another man holds that seat
because more people voted for him than for
me; and the same scurvy reason in a
ridiculously small way is to blame for the
fact that West Sydney is not my elector-
ate but my un-selectorate, which was deter-
mined on the manciple of preference to
industrial unionism and other things too
numerous to mention.
West Sydney begins at the' water-front
in Sydney Cove and strikes up to Chip-
pendale and a shockingly misnamed place
called Golden Grove (it was named for a
ship of the early days) into Darlington;
thence to the old Parramatta-road, taking
in Forest Lodge and a bit of Camperdown,
all of Glebe to the water at Glebe Point,
and thence by the water line to the point
of commencement. It includes the cradle
of settlement in Australia —the Rocks, before
tlie old officers’ quarters were built in Dalley-
street by the Tank Stream, and when the
Gaol was in Gloucester-street and seven men
were hanged in a row for stealing bread from
the public store.
The crazy rubble cottages of the poor
used to cluster on tlie steepest part of the
Rocks ; officialdom first, and later the mer-
chants of old Sydney, built on the heights
of Upper Fort-street or backing tlie water-
front of Miller’s Point. The old mer-
chant Campbell had a big garden on the
western side of Sydney Cove, and that
habit was repeated in the solid stone
houses on the western side of Fort Dawes.
You may see the last survivor of the old
gardens of the ’forties in some rusty-leaved
camellia tree, choked by dust and smoke,
in a little wilderness of thicket half hidden
by a rubbled wall built in the time of
Grandfather, and now falling stone by
stone.
The earth-hunger of these early people
with all Australia waiting for them was
enormous; or it may have been that fear
of the aborigines, whom the newcomers of
the time quaintly spoke of as “Indians,”
or of the thieves they had brought with
them to the new land, caused them to
huddle together as if they were living on
a crag in the sea and not on a continent.
This earth hunger is responsible for the
fact that, excepting the old Dawes Fort
grounds, a few square yards of tigtree-
grown reserve at the Main Terminus, the
Observatory grounds, and the triangle of
open space in old Charlotte-place and the
oblong of Wynyard Square, the northern
end of West Sydney has no playing spaces.
Darlington, Chippendale and Pyrmont are
even worse, and between Glebe and Pyr-
mont there is a Wentworth Park which
would not be there only that it was a
swamp and not worth the grabbing. The
effects of this land-hunger and land-mean-
ness are most apparent in Pyrmont:
hideous houses in Harris-street that would
make even St. Francis of Assisi snake-
headed and quarrelsome; houses huddled
together on breakneck rocks, and children
with only the streets for playground and
babies using tramcars as toys. There are
Mechanics’ Institutes and Technical Schools
and Geological Museums and other excellent
public institutions punctuating my un-
selectorate, but rarely a tree or a grass plot
to give children the close 'friendship with
the growing earth that every child longs for
and every child owns by nature; rarely any-
thing unknown to the builder to soften and
humanise that wilderness of bricks.
There was the best of Torres pilots who
was my friend, and his simile for good for-
tune was “as lucky as a Pyrmont billy
goat,” said simile being explainable by
these, among other, facts——that at Pyrmont
there are bill-hoardings and bill-stickers’
paste, and many high rocks , to play King of
the Castle on. But there is little luck in
being a Pyrmont child as compared with
the other children of Australia. It is the
sordid terrace repeated to infinity; houses
with the street-door opening into the rooms;
hideous cottages lime-washed in places until
the walls seem to have a new sort of eczema ;
architectural horrors excluding light and
air; half a street designed by a blind man
who should wear crape round his head in
memory of dead brains.
And the people of to-day are no better
than the dullards who permitted the earlier
horrors. In point of fact they are worse,
because the earlier people had no time for
sweetness and light, and were building only
for the moment. The Rocks Resumption
Board had a great chance and lost it. Its
new tenements in the Rocks area are all on
the one dull and thickheaded plan; all per-
petuating the disgraceful fear and idiotic
old land-hunger that huddled the early in-
habitants together to the exclusion of light
and air. Space could easily have been
arranged for flower plots and trees before
these new tenements that are the lineal heirs
of all the worst attributes of the old, but in
every case the new tenements are prisons
bringing their red brick up to the line of
the asphalt. There is a Building Act in
existence and presumably no administration.
Presumably also these new publicly-owned
tenements comply with the building regula-
tions to the letter. If they do it is thereby
proved that the building regulations want
alteration or development.
It is the falsest of economy this building
dwellings for people as if they had no more
aesthetic sense than dogs or horses. In the
best of the citizens affected it means that
the best is not encouraged in them : in the
inferior citizen affected it means that the
worst is encouraged in him. There can be
no argument against this statement: that
children whose playground is the dirty,


squalid street have not anything like the
same chance as children Who walk on grass
and play among flowers and under the shade
~f trees. There is the real Australian —the
Australian to be—starting off with a handi-
cap; beginning with the wrong foot first,
and therefore with a bigger chance of break-
ing Step all his life than he would it his
earliest and most impressionable years were
in a better environment.
These children playing in the streets were
the first impression I had of my un-selecto-
rate and the last impression too. One of
my regrets for losing West Sydney is that I
might have been able to speak for those
small Australians with a more certain hope
of holding attention long enough to ensure
reform.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Rod and the Serpent.
There is one honest man among the I’hew-
sionists anyway, and he is the one whose in-
stincts warned him against the unholy pact
that disgraced the national life of this
Commonwealth by making chicanery and
falsehood appear a virtue and lowering the
moral standard of Australian citizenship.
This is William Irvine, and The Bulle-
tin salutes him, though it dislikes his poli-
tics and mistrusts his vision. He stands out
to-day with the truth that is in him on his
lips, holding fast to what he believes to be
a big principle and challenging judgment
upon that and that alone. This is a rare
thing to see these times. Let Liberalism
compare this man with Hughes, who with
slanderous tongue goes shrieking up and
down the land, bartering this and that for
votes. Let Labor, too, compare the stand
he makes for his principles with the stand
some Labor men make for theirs, and then
say if the Democracy would suffer were
Labor men who seek the people’s votes as
honest to themselves and the country as
Irvine is. A man like Frank Anstey,
who knows his own mind and is not afraid
to follow what he believes to be the truth,
can have nothing but contempt for a wrig-
gler like Hughes. He may profoundly dislike
Irvine’s political doctrines; but lie can have
nothing but respect for the man, for
between them there is a strong bond. And
so it is among the people—they understand
Anstey, they understand Irvine, because
it is each man’s dearest wish that they
should. How many citizens can understand
the Win the War policy of Hughes and
Cook, twisting and turning this way to-day
and the other w r ay to-morrow, dropping dust
in the public eye and converting the patri-
otic impulse of the people into the sport of
party-mongers? Irvine leaves no doubt
about what his policy is and what the policy
of Phewsion ought to be if it would escape
the ridicule of having none except the one
it has declared to be the property of traitors.
Listen :—-
Having pledged my word to the people that
conscription was necessary for Australia then, and
circumstances having made it more necessary now, I
say to you to-night that, for me, conscription is the
issue at this election. I would sooner not get into
Parliament at all; I would sooner relinquish public
life —it does not possess such charms to me that I
would abandon any principle to remain in it —I
would sooner not go into Parliament at all than
have the vote of a single anti-conscriptionist given
in my favor. Let us remember that if we do not
now rise to the occasion which for the first time
presents itself to us as a community, we for ever
sink into a lower plane. Nothing can afterwards
save us. That fatal verdict on October 28 was a
declaration by this partner in the Empire—this
partner which has all its lifetime been protected
and nursed by the British navy—that it is a partner
with a limited liability. I say if that verdict is
left uncancelled we can never get away from it.
Not all the blood shed by those who have gone, not
all the tears and anguish of those left behind, can
save Australia’s good name unless we are prepared to
do our utmost.
Whatever criticism this may be open to,
Irvine cannot be charged with dishonesty.
He is entitled to the admiration of every
decent Australian who believes the people
have a right to claim a fair deal. Most of
what Irvine says this paper believes to be
true. That the refusal of Australia to make
military service compulsory has been a
shocking mistake is the unalterable opinion
of The Bulletin, held since the first Bill-
jim regiment was enlisted. But that
plebiscite of October 28 created a position
which it is mere futility to disregard, and,
no matter who was really responsible for
the result, Australians will have to stand
the reproach. The Bulletin is sorry that
Irvine misses the significance of the plebis-
cite. But it is glad to see him or any other
man standing upright to follow the light
instead of pretending to see it and crawling
through the gutter.
[To express the views of The Bulletin, by S. H.
Prior, F. J. Kirby and D. M. Wright, 214 George
Street, Sydney.]
The Final Hours of Boom and Bloat.
As other parts of the world can find more
important use for money than handing it
over to Australian Governments for the pur-
pose of providing Royal Commissions with
horrible revelations later on, many public
works in N. S. Wales are being closed down.
Large numbers of men are thus being thrown
out of employment, the expenditure of about
£140,000,000 not having sufficiently deve-
loped the great natural resources of the
State to permit a handful of people to make
sure of a job unless Cgiien takes a p.n.
Following on this there is much wailing that
the men out of work are in a bad way and that
labor is threatened with oppression. That the
stoppage of work must cause hardship to
many poor people is certain ; and this ought
to provide evidence for labor of how rotten a
foundation the public policy of the State
has been resting on. Borrow and bloat
are nothing but the outward and visible sign
of an organised shirk, by which Capital and
Labor conspire to dodge the responsibilities
of to-day by pawning the week after next,
and having a picnic with the proceeds. At
this outing nearly all the solid nourishment
is appropriated by the speculator and the
parasite. Labor gets little more than the
bones. The tragedy for the Labor party has
been that it refused to see this, and as a
borrower in N. S. Wales it went full speed
ahead. Now, when the inevitable occurs,
Labor howls dismally that the way to pre-
vent it happening is to borrow some more
and keep the jag moving. Well, the plain
fact about the borrowing dodge is that
it is nearly played out, for Australia has
just about taken enough borrowings on board
to submerge the Plimsoll mark. The labor
of the crowds of men who have hitherto
been toiling to help Parliaments build up
debt is wanted now to assist the country in
grappling with the cost of debt, and unless
it is diverted to this rational purpose Aus-
tralia’s job of holding her end up is not
going to be completed. Of course it is. no
use eternally moaning about the criminal
recklessness of the past. But there is an
urgent necessity for recognising that the
work Australia has to perform for national
salvation will not even be started until the
last vestige of the old policy has been flung
off the premises. For Australia to go on
borrowing, wasting and importing—each
folly being inseparable from the other —
would mean, even if such lunacy could be
indulged in. the certain destruction of nearly
every political principle the people have come
to regard as permanent in national policy.
This paper has suspicions that already some
of these are half-way to the dustbin. No
country in the world ever pawned its way
to greatness, nor did any people ever shirk
their way anywhere but to a place that was
mighty uncomfortable.
[To express the views of The Bulletin, by
S. H. Prior, F. J. Kirby and D. M. Wright, 214
George Street, Sydney.]
Playing with Matches in a Powder
Magazine.
The British Empire consists of, in round
figures, 60 million white citizens and 370
million colored subjects. The very existence
of the Empire in this world-crisis depends
on preserving the solidarity of the white
citizens. The National party is pledged by
its leader, Mr. Hughes, to “a policy for the
whole British Empire covering every phase
of our national, social and economic life.”
This policy must, of course, include citizens
and subjects alike in its domination. That
policy already dominates the 370 million
colored subjects. The National party fol-
lowing Mr. Hughes seeks to extend that
centralised Imperial sway to the 60 million
white citizens. Can South Africa, Canada
or Australia accept that proposition?
South Africa is engaged at the present time
with us in fighting against German Imperial-
ism. But would South Africa, after her
bitter experience of British Imperialism,
consent to allow a Parliament sitting in
London to control her “national, social and
economic life”? No one but a madman could
believe that she would do so. Canada, like
Australia, has her own settled policy of in-
dustrial and economic progress, social stan-
dards and race purity. Would Canada
tamely revert to the position which America
occupied before the War of Independence?
We know she would not. Would we, the
free citizens of the Australian Common-
wealth, consent to put our high ideals
of “social and economic life” and our
white Australian policy- —our religion of race
purity—to the hazard in an Imperial
gamble where we know that the dice are
already loaded against us, and where the
stakes are the bodies and souls of our chil-
dren’s children? Never! The mental status
of a politician who in this supreme hour,
when salvation lies in unity, seeks to let
loose these terrific forces of disruption, can
only be compared to that of an idiot child
playing with matches in a powder-magazine.
The only way for citizens alike of Australia
and the British Empire to stop such a mad
Mullah in his mission of destruction and
disruption is to vote down his followers by
whatever name they choose to call them-
selves.
[Frank Cotton, Hornsby, N.S.W.]
The Merchant Shipping Act badly wants re-
casting and the Immigration Act is a poor
thing if what happened recently in a Vic-
torian Court is allowed to "go.” A big cargo
steamer took in its usual crowd of mixed-
bred all-nation firemen and sundries at Barry,
Wales. One of the crowd, a sort of Greek,
raised Cain all through the voyage, winding
up with a hint of knife-work on the chief
engineer between Calcutta and Java. At
Java an attempt was made to unload the
undesirable, but Java wasn’t having any. It
recommended Australia, no doubt adding
something about a birth-stain. Arrived at
Melbourne the ship’s officers lost no time in
running the cross-bred nuisance up to the
nearest suburban court on an assault charge.
The case was easy. The prosecuting lawyer
mentioned that the maximum penalty was 12
weeks and asked for it, or (as he put it in
his naive innocence) as much of it as would
exceed 10 days, by which time the boat
would have cleared for another port. The
honorary Bench obliged with a month —not
without, it must be said to its credit, a pro-
test about the dumping of such human trash
upon Australia. And thus we gain another
citizen, God help us! But what sort of a
law is it that allows a shipping company to
unload its labor-mistakes, gathered up in the
slums of Goo-knows-where, upon Australia?

“D.A.S.II.”: Another little way the Federal
Commissioner of Taxation has of obtaining
money which I do not believe Parliament
ever intended him to get is to make assess-
ments on the income earned by soldiers prior
to enlistment. The Income Tax Assessment
Act 1915 states (Section 13) :—
This Act shall not apply to any person who is on
active service during the present war with the
military or naval forces of the Commonwealth....
so far as regards income from personal exertion and
earned prior to the commencement of this Act or
during the present state of war.
This surely releases Bilgjim from Federal
tax on what he earned by the sweat of his
brow; but the Commissioner takes the point
that this section applies only from the date
of Bill’s enlistment, and that Bill is liable
for tax on his earnings before that date.
Therefore if Bill enlists in July and sails,
say, in December, the probability is that the
following March Mrs. Billjim is called on
by the Commonwealth to pay up whatever
amount the Commissioner has taxed Bill.
Practically, Mrs. Bill has no redress. She
has to pay. She cannot fight in the courts,
because she mostly cannot afford it, and, any-
way, if she could she isn’t sure enough of the
facts. All she knows, as a rule, is that Bill
didn’t leave the money to pay the tax, not
expecting that he would be taxed ; and if she
pays she has to do it out of the “allot-
ment.” Thus the bigger the monetary sacri-
fice Bill made the harder it is for Bill’s
missus, because the bigger the tax she has to
produce from somewhere. Nine times out
of 10, it isn't a tax on Bill; it is a tax on
Bill’s missus while Bill himself is away
fighting.
An interesting sidelight on the “solution of
the metal problem” that Hughes talks so
much about is contained in a report just
made to the shareholders of Iodide (Mineral
Hill), Ltd., Sydney:—
The directors' efforts have been hampered (a) by
the abnormal times we are passing through due to
the war, (b) the reluctance of shareholders to pay
calls, and (c) the unprofitable results from concen-
tration of the milling ore and the heavily increased
charges made by the Smelting Company since the
War Regulations shut other outlets for disposal, a
position that has been brought under the notice of
the Federal Authorities, but so far without relief.
In this connection a favorable offer was received to
purchase the Company’s output of leady concen-
trates for the Japanese market, subject to the
approval of the Fedora 1 Authorities; but upon
application being made for permission to enter into
negotiations the same was peremptorily refused.
There being no prospect of relief from the local
Smelters, whose charges have been again increased,
the directors had to abandon concentration, and
could only recommend shareholders to wind up the
Company, but at the same time would impress upon
them that the possibilities of the property, of which
all the general evidences were distinctly favorable,
could not be determined until greater depth was
attained.
So a Win the War Government refused to
allow Australian lead concentrates to go
even to an Ally, but insisted upon the stuff
going to what a Victorian Supreme Court
Judge the other day declared was a mon-
opoly ; and as this monopoly has made cumu-
lative increases in the smelting charges
another mining co. has had to throw up the
sponge. And this is at a time when it is
urgently necessary that every possible source
of employment in Australia should be kept
open and every possible pound of lead pro-
vided for the needs of the Empire and its
friends.
Resolution passed by the recent Melbourne
conference of interstate farmers’ organisa-
tions :—
That in the interests of Australia and the Empire,
and with the object of encouraging primary produc-
tion throughout the Commonwealth, the Government
should recognise the absolute necessity of amending
the tariff to enable farmers to import their tools of
trade and bags free of duties.
A better thing would be to amend the intelli-
gence of some farmers. Can’t Cocky see
that every other producer logically has as
much right to claim free importation of
“tools of trade” as he has? And if such a
claim were admitted all round—seeing that
the tools of one producer are the finished
product of another—iow would the farmer
stand? To whom would he sell his finished
product if no other producer could afford to
buy it because every other producer was
importing the article which he should be
producing? The farmer would have a
mighty poor local market, anyhow —and the
war has shown wba(; is liable to happen to
the foreign markets. For mere sordid bust*
ness reasons the farmer ought to be barrack-
ing mightily for High Protection on every-
thing Australia can produce. High Protec-
tion means a busy population, and that
means a handy market for the farmer s
goods.
[P. T. Freeman, 214 George Street, Sydney.]
“J.”: The man who is there already and
scratching hard, grins, when he hears how
poor old Billjim, back from the wars, is
to be dumped on the land and turned into a
prosperous farmer overnight. Let me speak
of what I do know. Recently the Koppio
(S.A.) branch of the Farmers and Settlers’
Association presented the member for the
district with a list of 49 farms, comprising
over 100,900 acres, under the jurisdiction
of two S. Australian district councils,
Tumby and Lincoln. Said farms are abso-
lutely deserted, the holders having walked
off, and nobody can be got to take them on.
There are dozens of other farmers who
would throw up the sponge if they knew of
a decent job. These men have lived on rab-
bit and hope for the last 10 years, and now
jump at an offer of 10s. a day and eight
hours. Yet it is proposed to reward
Billjim by putting him where these hard-
ened specialists could not exist! It looks
now as if the cocky's harvester, which a few
years ago cost £6O, is to soar from the
present £llO to anything the makers like.
If his “super” and everything else is to get
dearer and dearer, more land of the poorer
class will be deserted presently. You can’t
keep the man on the land and bleed him to
support great cities mostly occupied by
parasites that make in a mouth far more
than the farmer can net in a year.
“Verdad” : I detect German intrigue in the
cable that Chile is sounding Peru about the
advisability of jointly making war on Bolivia
in order to take the latter country’s province
of Tarija. The reason given for making
this move now is that Argentine, if she
joins the Allies, will be too busy to be able
to protect Bolivia. I think German agents
have started the rumor to keep Argentine
from joining in against Germany. Argen-
tine and Chile are fairly friendly—as
friendly, that is, as any two neighboring-
nations are ever likely to be —but there is a
natural jealousy between them. Chile-fought
and conquered Peru and Bolivia combined,
and still retains portion of their territories.
Chile and Peru hate each other cordially.
Bolivia and Peru are not at all friendly—
they sort of blame each other for their
defeat. Chile and Bolivia are neither very
friendly nor yet very antagonistic. It is
most unlikely (I would have said quite im-
possible, but in these days prophecies are apt
to go wrong) that Peru and Chile would
fight on the same side. Peru is rather pro-
German. But no matter how improbable
the tale is, many votes in Argentine would
certainly be influenced by the fear of letting
Chile get one ahead.

About one Australian in 500, perhaps, has
taken serious note of the fact that British
forces in Mesopotamia last week got hold
of a section of the Bagdad railway—the
first big blow to German prestige that the
Kaiser and his Deutscher Bank have suffered
in that region. Australian indifference to
war details would seem almost splendid if
it were not wholly due to ignorance—the
ignorance that Cook and Hughes are ex-
ploiting when they howl for support of their
undiscover able Win-the-War policy, and
swear that under no circumstances will they
hurt the feelings of anti-conscriptionists.
Still, the sin of Australian indifference to
the relative importance of things is charge-
able more against the censorship than
against the people. In the early days of
the war ignorance was willing, if not eager,
to be educated ; but as a result of its per-
sistent befoolment it ceased to try to under-
stand the Allies’ scheme of extricating them-
selves from the mess and decided to wait
for something too definite to be misunder-
stood.

Another tale of muddle:—
If the recommendations of the conference upon
the rabbit industry are not greatly modified, there is
going to be trouble in various parts—especially of
N, S. Wales—where poisoning rabbits for their skins
is fairly remunerative at present prices. There are
multitudinous places where factories are too far
distant for trapping-for-the-market to be profitable,
but where bunny exists in millions. When Federal
interference with the industry was proposed it was
stated that, in consequence of the value of the rabbit
as a food to Britain, the Commonwealth Government
was going, in conjunction with the British Govern-
ment, to assume control of the whole of the indus-
try—slaughtering for skins included. Many carcase
trappers, considering that the prices they were paid
by the factories were not commensurate with the
prices obtained for the factory products in Britain,
hoped that there would be an adjustment, while skin-
trappers, who work tremendously long hours under
the roughest <sf conditions, hoped that any change
would lie in the direction of eliminating the middle-
man. Last year, when skins were cheaper in the open
market than they are now, the Federal Government
commandeered what it required for military pur-
poses at Is. Bd. per lb. for best, a price then con-
siderably lower than that obtainable by auction, but
removed the embargo when its own demand was
satisfied. Any return to this arrangement was not
expected, the anticipation being that Government
control would simply ensure fair treatment to both
producer and consumer. What has been decided is
that the Government shall commandeer, not only
skins required for its own requirements, but the whole
of the output, at the old price of 20d. per lb. for
best, and this at a time when skins of that quality
are at 30d. in open market. Local manufacturers
are to be supplied at prices to be determined, after
which the Government is to re-sell the balance in
any way it likes. Skin-trappers and poisoners—all
small men with no pull —can hardly be blamed if
they look upon the scheme as a plan for robbing
them. But other consequences are likely to follow
the action, and many people not directly interested
Civilisation.
From The Bulletin’s old contributor
“McNorth” :—
Sixteen years ago I gave civilisation the go-by
and sailed away north to the sunlit lands that lie
around the Equator. Ten years were spent in Papua
and adjacent isles trading with brown-skinned natives
for copra and rubber; sailing strange-odored lug-
gers to little-known villages in search of plantation
labor: fossicking amongst the coral reefs for the
slothful beche-de-mer in a tiny, heaving cutter that
at times was almost a submarine; making long
canoe journeys up mighty, nipa-fringed rivers
prospecting for sandalwood and cedar; marching at
the head of long strings of carriers into mountain-
ous inland country in pursuit of the golden penny-
weight—-and rarely finding it. The last six years I
spent at Cape York —Australia’s uttermost north —•
struggling with the jungle and the solitudes in the
endeavor to make cocoanuts grow where only
Binghis grew before. But all the time 1 lived close
to nature, breathing Gon’s pure air beneath sunny
skies ; and not once in all the years did I visit the
civilisation of the South till a week or two ago,
when I boarded a China steamer at Thursday Island
and came steaming down the wonderful Inner Route
of the Great Barrier and so on to Sydney.
And now that I am here I don’t like it. After
those years of quiet the Ways of the city and the
everlasting noise of alleged civilisation jar horribly.
People sit hunched up in noisy trams and trains in
rows one behind the other like so many puppets
instead of human beings possessing individuality.
Of all sizes, shapes and ages, and of every manner of
dress, they pour out of dingy doors in tall buildings
at “knock-off,” and make the traversing of the
narrow streets a feat of navigation. I eat a city
lunch in a crowded, voice-laden atmosphere and
strain my ears —so long used to silence—to catch
the conversation of my companion. All the time
people are chattering, and the hoof-beats on the
pavements add to the general din. Harshness is
the dominating note. Trams tear along with an
uproar like the Day of Judgment on the spree, and
motors, making unsavory noises that sound like
seasickness, honk incessantly. Even in the compara-
tive quiet of Suburbia butchers, bakers, ice-men and
the like call several times daily and attract the
attention of the householders with grating noises like
“Ye-rrp!” The nerve-shattering results of the
continual row and the necessity of being always on
the alert are reflected in the strained faces of the
people.
Artificiality is everywhere. There are women with
young skirts and old faces, and, at the surfing
resorts, females who paint their faces after donning
their bathing costume—shams that are foolish and
futile inasmuch as they deceive nobody. Even the
air is make-believe stuff containing petrol-fumes,
smoke, road-dust and sundries—especially sundries.
And the purposeless haste! On hot days even,
men dressed in three-garment suits and stiff collars
rush along as though the fate of the Empire
depended on their haste. Why should a man twirl
round a corner on one leg merely in order to
purchase a packet of cigarettes? They strain their
hearts chasing trams, although the lumbersome
things follow one another at intervals of a few
seconds. Life should not be the strenuous thing
city people make it.
Then there are the dreadfully high venereal statis-
tics, the slums, tales of families in distress and
other horrors.
It reads like an account of some over-populated
Chinese Province. Yet all this noise and crowding
and hustle and general chaos is in our own country
—a country that has millions of unpeopled, fertile
acres lying beneath sunny skies in a climate beyond
compare.
I am getting back to my North. Nature is better
than this horror that man has made.

moment, in many remote districts of N. S. Wales,
the destruction of the rabbits is of such value to the

The Great Struggle for Victory—and a
Crust.
The submarine represents the crux of the
Allies’ problem, and after everything has
been said about it this remains—that mastery
of the sea during the last few weeks has
been passing out of the hands of the British.
Yowling at the German for being a bar-
barian does not alter the facts which have
to be grappled with. It is possible that the
blockade may be beaten by sinking the enemy
craft. It is conceivable that the rate of
constructing new vessels may be so speeded
up as to make good the losses, though this
obviously involves a loss of freight horrible
to think about. There is, further, a proba-
bility that by concentrating on production of
foodstuffs on the premises and the regula-
tion of the national appetite, the necessity
for imports may be vastly reduced. But
these considerations merely emphasise the
critical situation created by the torpedo.
The elements of the case as they stand to-
day boil down to this: Can the army win
before the submarine isolates Britain, or
can Britain, on whom the weight of respon-
sibility falls, overcome the peril at her door
so that the army may take its time? The
details in relation to the German armies,
Russia, the campaign in Northern France,
and the blockade are so obscure as to permit
of little more than guesswork, but it is clear
from the tone of Lloyd George’s latest
speech,-the studied ambiguity of the figures
referring to ships, and the comments of the
London newspapers that the powers in this
scrap are fighting with their backs to the
wall, and the side that makes the best use
of its resources will win. Meanwhile the
battle east of Arras goes furiously on, the
German hitting back heavily and the British
troops making progress which may mean
much or little. The only certain thing is
that Von llindenb tt rg is being severely
mauled, and that the yarns about “the shat-
tered morale of the enemy” are the same old
rubbish —told to appease cold-footers and
cowards mostly. For Australia there ' has
lately been the abrupt revelation that
a man who writes copious notes in
order to avoid war may still set about
making war in a much more rational and
business-like way than an individual who
spouts a torrential deluge of fierce words.
Uncle Sam has made service compulsory
from the start, acting upon the only sane
conception of civic duty in an hour of the
most dire menace to every democracy in the
world. It is good to see the Yankee in this
fight and it will do the citizens of Australia
no harm to remember that Jonathan, is
helping to save this Commonwealth, that the
end of the war is not yet in sight, and that
mouths which are full of bombast are full of
danger. If the Allies win through to where
they want to get —and where every patriotic
Australian should pray for the future safety
of his country they will get—the inhabitants
of this Commonwealth will owe more to
the people of other countries than any
nation, perhaps, that ever breathed the air
of freedom.
