The solemn announcement that the London
Conference has decided to adopt the princi-
ple of preference as a post-war policy means
little more than nothing. Its decision in
this respect, if there has been one, has rather
less force than a decision to adopt a policy
of twins. The members of the Conference
are without even the shadow of authority to
bind Governments, and certainly have no
power to bind peoples. It has always been
within the competence of what are some-
times called “Colonial Governments” to make
preference part of their fiscal policy. They
have done so. British Governments have, on
the other hand, hitherto rejected preference.
This Conference cannot change that. The
question is one of domestic politics: and
while it may happen that the war will lead
to a sharp change in Britain’s fiscal system,
this will certainly not be as the result of
anything decided at a meeting of a few indi-
viduals without authority to decide anything.
If it is the intention of Lloyd George to be
an apostle of reformation in England, that is
the joint business of himself and the people
thereabouts. If any politician or political
parties elsewhere chose to bind themselves in
advance to co-operate with him, that in turn
is the business of other people. Everything
will hinge upon the details. There is no
magic in a mere word whether it be “prefer-
ence” or anything else. For the present
it looks to The Bulletin that something
like the first steps are being taken to give
this meeting in London a status entirely
new ; and it is ominous that a start should
be made at the very point where, so far as
Australia at any rate is concerned, the issue
raised impinges directly upon the powers of
the Commonwealth Parliament. This Con-
ference has been shrouded ip a great mystery
all along. It requires careful watching.
A quaint feature of the election cam-
paign is the discovery of the transcendent
virtues and unique talents which the Labor
party possessed until a few months back. In
an attempt to show that the party has
“blown out its brains,” the. Argus furnishes
a list including such intellectual giants as
Pearce, Laird Smith and Jawbone Webster,
and credits them with the mental qualities
of an Asquith or a Rosebery. The papers
dilate upon the “splendid, virile programme”
of Labor when Hughes and Archibald were
in its ranks, aaid compare it unfavorably
with the present menu. Meanwhile readers
are probably wondering why the same papers
always tried to defeat a party with such
an admirable programme and sought to ex-
clude the brilliant intellects from Parlia-
ment.

Thus the desperate Phews as he swings
on the farmer’s log : —
I appeal to you, the primary producers, therefore
to stand behind your fellows in Europe and leave no
stone unturned to see that their pressing food rer
quirements are forthcoming. Upon your answer hangs
our destiny and that of the civilised world.
And if that heart-sob doesn’t move you, con-
sider the sorrows of a poor Socialist-Labor-
ite-Freetrader-Friend of the People-Mono-
poly Destroyer-Monopoly Builder-Pet o’
Duchesses-Secretary of Wharflaborers-Lib-
eral-Tory out of work and down at heel!
At last a Phewsion candidate has been
pinned down to a definite answer to the
question: “How does your party intend to
carry out its promise to win the war?” At
a meeting at Maribyrnong, Jowett, the shep-
herd king who is opposing J. E. Fenton, was
asked: —■
As you state conscription is not an issue, how do
you propose to get men, failing voluntary enlistment ?
And here is Jowett’s illuminating reply:—
I believe with the driving out of public of the men
who are carrying on the campaign of hatred against
the Prime Minister, that recruiting will be stimu-
lated sufficiently for the needs of the army.
Doubtless if Jowett happens to beat Fenton
in Maribyrnong hordes in Brisbane will
rush to join the colors, and if Hughes can
only defeat Hainpson there is even a possi-
bility that Willie Watt and Willie Kelly
will enlist.

[B. J. H. Owen, 75 Glenhuntly Road, Elwood, Vic.]
It is costing N. S. Wales £360,000 to buy
itself out of the Norton Griffiths contract.
Now. is this Government going to appoint
a commission to ascertain how such a con-
tract came to be made, or is it going to lie
under the suspicion that it dare not take the
lid off?
Among all these stories about mice chew-
ing five-pound notes and women’s hair, not a
word has been published about the calami-
tous interference of the rodents with the
Phewsion’s Win the War policy. Probably
the Censor is bravely withholding details in
order that the enemy may not get the hang
of the situation. Well, this paper defies the
Censor and insists on publishing the facts.
The policy was left by Hughes with the
Liberal junta in Sydney to be approved and
then transmitted through the post to him in
Victoria. It was despatched by registered
letter, but Hughes happened to be careering
round the landscape pulling up upas trees, so
the Post Office locked the document up
while it went to have an hour’s rest. During
that fatal pause the policy was attacked by
mice, which nibbled all the corners off and
ate a hole in the middle. Subsequent efforts
by the Phewsion to remember what the
hiatus contained, and its most strenuous
attempts to capture the mice with a view to
recovering the plans and specifications, re-
sulted in failure. Australia is thus now re-
duced to the ignominious position of having
the national policy concealed in the innards
of what a cat pursues for breakfast.

“The House that Jack Built” is resisting
the rain splendidly at Her Majesty’s (Syd-
ney ), and the roar of counted coin in the
treasury drowns the drumming of torrents on
the theatre roof.
* * * *
Bruises are part of the acrobatic com-
edian’s make-up, but broken bones stop the
performance. Last Saturday while Barry
Lupino was trying to crack his neck in
Melbourne, Billy Rego of the Follies dumped
a piano on his toes at Sydney Tivoli and
smashed a bone or two. While he is off
Chris Wren will amble in his place. And
Wren himself has only just resumed grease-
paint after an accident of the same sort.
The other Follies remain unlamed by either
criticism or pianos, _ and basso Malcolm
McEachern is thundering in support.
Now that Winter is at hand, Wirths’
audiences at the New Hippodrome, Sydney,
have ceased to envy the performers who
have to dive into the sinking ring in pursuit
of their salaries. These chilly evenings even
the timid would rather take off their boots
and warm their stockinged feet on a flushed
lion. (P.S. —Flushed lion can be procured
this week at the Sydney Zoo. His missus
has just been guilty of triplets.)
“THE HOUSE THAT JACK BUILT."
AT SYDNEY MAJESTY’S.
Sammy (Harry Roxbury) and his panto,
cow defy the police.
Scenes from the islands of beauty and
legend are being filmed at Waddington’s
Grand (Sydney). Maoriland in all its
wonder and weirdness smiles at you in the
magic of . its greens and blues. The pro-
gramme is varied by scenes from other
lands; and the beautiful flower studies with
which the evening’s entertainment starts
were greeted with enthusiastic applause by
the first-night audience, most of it there as
the guest of the Maoriland Government.
Shadow drama in Sydney this week
Lyceum ; Charlie Chaplin in his latest eccentricities
as “The Vagabond” ; and “Aladdin from Broadway,”
a startling series of adventures that befall a ’Murkan
investigator into the mysteries of the Holy Land.
Hoyt’s: “The White Raven,” in which Ethel
Barrymore as a Wild West lady with an operatic
voice donates herself as a gambling stake so that
she may get to the glamor of New York and become
a prima-donna.
Crystal Palace : “The Dancer’s Peril,” a pleasing
Russian drama featuring Alexis Kosloff and Alice
Brady in an Imperial Russian ballet; Chaplin as
“The Vagabond.”
“VERY GOOD EDDIE” AT
MELBOURNE
KING’S.
George Whitehead as
Percy Darling, suffer-
ing from a song.
Barry Lupino as
Eddie Kettle
slightly upset.
“Very Good Eddie,” having been speeded-
up since the lirst night at Melbourne King’s,
now sends the audience unreluctantly off the
premises on the right side of 11 p.m. The
show would bear even a little more haste,
especially when Eddie and the other fellow’s
bride are singing their Babes in the Wood
duet. The vocalism and pained demeanor of
Barry Lupino in those mushy moments sug-
gest that any other boyish-looking comedian
might do the same thing much better. On
the other hand, it is evident that Lilian
Tucker and Higginson could do with a few
better opportunities than they get, for the
lady has a winsome style, and Andrew H.,
whose front name is strangely out of place,
retains all his old capacity for playing the
giddy goat in a gay and detached spirit, and
his singing has improved somewhat since the
time when he frisked around the Merry
Widow. Also the nippy little chorus might
be made more of, perhaps. True, there is
no logical excuse for the chorus having fol-
lowed Eddie off the boat, but where a bunch
of nice girls are concerned no excuse is
necessary. They are always welcome. The
only thing to be said against the irrelevant
girls is that they are usually attended by
able-bodied young men who ought to be
engaged in war work.
Barry Lupino fell out of “Very Good
Eddie” into a nice warm bed on Saturday
night. He was making his protracted step
downstairs during the acrobatic dance in the
second act, when he somehow missed his
footing, and, hitting the stage on an unsuit-
able portion of his anatomy, strained his
shoulder. The nimble comedian was too sore
to appear on Monday, but it was expected
that he would be right enough on the follow-
ing day to resume his all-night Sittings with
Fayette Perry and perform the old prodigies
of foolishness in the lolly-colored pyjamas.
Another phase of Marie Tempest is due
at Melbourne Royal next Saturday.
“Penelope,” a comedy by W. S. Maugham,
is the coming bill, and the cast will include
several additions to the co. Meanwhile Kitty
is making her final elopements with her law-
ful husband.
A glamor of naughty reputation still com-
mends “Canary Cottage” to the godly and
ungodly alike at Melbourne Majesty’s, and
the sad fact that the Williamson show is
no naughtier than it ought to be is not
apparent to everybody at a first visit. Some
people go more than once to make sure they
were not shocked the time before.
. T’lie “Bunyip” panto., which disposes of
its name, part in the first act, continues to
P«t in eight performances a week at Mel-
bourne Princess’s. The Manchurians and
the Bush Eire must be largely accountable
for this, unless the long, long comic dua-
logues are the cause. For the little bit of
plot is only a pleasant suggestion of what
might have been, and the management’s con-
ception of a Bunyip is a libel on legendary
animal life. Tt looks more like a demon
Turnip than the Bunyip of our crav-fish
dreams.

Whilst Melbourne Tivoli is lavishly
beflagged in honor of Ada Reeve the rush to
get there is tempered by a rise in prices.
Nevertheless the present entertainment seems
worth an extra shilling, compared with some
that went before. Ada’s humorous way of
leading the chorus for the sickly sentimental
doggrel ragtime ditty known as “Tennessee”
is a study in applied art. She gives a new
interest to hackneyed songs, and hits the
boys hard with her new ones. Nikola, the
man with the wonderful hands, also Little
Lucie, the versatile dancer, count for a
good deal more than Clarence Blakiston.
whose clever but out-of-date turn is alto-
gether quite too English, you know. Two of
the actors whom this actor imitates never
came to Australia before they died, and the
survivor is as yet unknown to this beastly
country. Besides, Blakiston introduces him-
self in the tone of a superior person con-
ferring a favor on the vaudeville stage.
* *
The programmes at the Melba and Britan-
nia picture-houses, Melbourne, this week are
keeping a lot of people off the streets. At
the Melba the current draw is Virginia Pear-
son as “Daredevil Kate,” a tanglefoot mer-
chant in a rough mining camp who keeps
the excitement simmering with tongue and
gun when the liquid fire runs .low. At the
Britannia “The Microscope Mystery” and
“Somewhere in France” are chasing their
tails as “continuous” spasms, along with a
Keystone comedy, “Maggie’s First False
Step.”

John Beck,

The opulent charms of the Dago prima-
lonna, Lina Cavalieri, are being screened at
Hoyt’s, Melbourne, this week in a. Tiber five-
acter, “Should a Man Forgive?” In this
attraction Lina is the flaming passion of an
artist and a duke, but it requires yards of
gesticulation and a well-managed duel to
decide which of the rivals can have her for
keeps. All the ingredients of a William Le
Queux story are contained in the Metro
offering, “In the Diplomatic Service” —a
delectable hash of mystery and espionage
decorated with handsome attaches, lovely
ladies, and ambassadorial uniforms.
Adelaide theatres are taking turns at get-
ting their heads in front in the dramatic
race. The King’s is shut again, and the
Royal has heard the last tremolo of Italian
opera—a long and popular and successful
season —and gone contentedly back to pic-
tures. This leaves the Tivoli to keep drama
going with the old “Robbery Under Arms,”
which, with Cross as Starlight and
Fitzherbert as the Inspector, goes as well
as ever. Joseph Brennan is the outstanding
feature of the Dandies at the Queen's. Le
Blanc and Mack are doing well at the
Majestic; but this bill needs strengthening
up on the athletic and spectacular side.
“C.R.8.”: Frank Anstey will have to
share with John Thomson the prize for
frankness allotted to him by Harrison
Owen (B. 19/4/T7). John, who was re-
turned unopposed for Cowper (N.S.W.), is
also a politician of “no occupation.” And
he has more right to regard himself as “un-
occupied” than the energetic Anstey. Thom-
son plays at politics as he plays at Life—-like
a gentleman-amateur, never considering
either of sufficient importance to lose any
sleep over.

If the Phewsion is as “sporting” as the
Argus says it is. Laborite Hampson, whose
seat Hughes is trying to collar, ought to be
given the use of a pair of motor-cars for
travelling through the Bendigo electorate.
On being tackled at Maldon for using two
Commonwealth cars on electioneering busi-
ness, Hampson’s opponent declared that one
was an old car and “the other was for the
Prime Minister and he meant to use it.”
The use of a Government car to advance a
Party’s interests may look fair to the party
behind Hughes; but that only goes to show
what sort of party it is.
AT POVERTY POINT.
"Ancient Prompter”: The Williamson Co.
has lost another of its old managers. Harold
Ashton (born Jordan and christened Bill),
who died the other day in Melbourne at
bo, has been connected with the Firm for
over 30 years, first as secretary to J.C.W.,
then as “advance” and business manager, and
latterly as director in S. Africa. Ashton
was hard to beat as a showman, but he was
“had” once. In a M.L. town, when all the
cheap parts of the house had filled, a diffi-
dent youth came to the manager to know
what the entertainment was like before risk-
ing 3s. for a front seat. “The best piece you've
ever had here,” asseverated Ashton. “Tell
you what I’ll do. If you don’t like it when
you’ve seen it, I'll give you your money
back.” The show was over and Ashton was
busy in the office, when the patron reap-
peared. “I thought it was rotten,” he
announced, “so I’ve come for my money.”
The boss recalled his stupid promise, put a
good face on the situation, and handed up
3s. The youth turned to go, then wheeled
round with: “And what about my tram-
fare ?”
The late Harold Ashton, associate-director
in the Williamson management, was a son
of the farm. John Jordan, of Burwood, near
Melbourne, was his dad, and Harold’s front
name was Bill. The Jordan place, still
dairy-farmed by a brother of the dead
manager, is an ancient, well-known land-
mark in a district dotted With elderly re-
minders of a bygone day—a picturesque
white house in a very green home-paddock,
approached by a bushy drive across a bushy
creek. Ashton was drawing £lOOO p.a. at
the time of his death—better than most
farmers do. But there’s more sleep in the
farming business, and longer life.
"M. 8.” : The late Harold Ashton was the
kindliest man you’d find In a thousand. One
of the quiet fellows who never quarrel. Like
all such, once started lue was hard to stop.
When he was looking after the front of the
Gaiety (Sydney) for Fred Hiscocks there
was a vocalist who uaed to unload his song
and then rush round to the door to hear the
criticisms of the patrons. One night he tried
to rush into the auditorium, probably with
the object of finding if there lingered an echo
of his last note. This butting in among the
paying portion of the audience is prohibited,
and Harold stopped the artist, who made a
very rude remark. Harold stopped him
again, this time with a punch which landed
with a dull but suitable thud. The party
who took delivery was amazed, for the young
operator was not within two stone of his
poundage. His amazement was enhanced
when Ashton swung a brace of beauties for
good luck. Thenceforth the singer decided
that, he was not popular on the door.
Wilson Forbes, an ancient pro. with a
marked affection for Sfihakspeare, died in the
Melbourne Hospital last week. Forbes had
been an efficient minor mummer in many
plays in Australia until Age practically re-
duced him to the role of a quavering subur-
ban lecturer on the one subject that could
bring a littlr fire to his eye and some of the
old fruitinees back to his voice. Forbes was
reasonably “mad" on the Bard, and had all
the quaint prejudices of the conventional
commentator. He leaves behind a promising
daughter, Lorna, who was recently spoiling
her pleasant features with grease-paint in
the Allan Wilkie co. in Melbourne.

’79, one company at the Academy of. Music,

were in the cast. As to Mabel in “The
Pirates,” Elsa May originated that heroine
at the Royal in May, ’Bl. I think Alice
Rees’s first essay in G. and S. parts was in
“Patience” in the following year.

But why not call Cook and Hughes on

This is told of one of our most beloved
male warblers. He was engaged to sing at
one of the Very Best houses and' allowed the
use of a dressing-room in which to adjust his
beautiful moustache between songs. A par-
ticularly luxurious bathroom opened off the
room, and it captured the minstrel’s fancy.
“Dear lady,” he observed to the hostess as
he stooped to kiss her hand on leaving, “I
am in love with your beautiful bathroom. I
must come some day and have a bath in it.”
And two days later he carried out the threat.
Bertie Ballenger, the sporting young
daughter of Colonel Ballenger, took to the
stage some time ago and has since got right
into the limelight in the U.S. She joined a
company under Morosco’s management and
understudied a lead who obligingly fell ill.
Bertha stepped into her shoes with complete
success. Her last Melbourne appearance
was with the Allan Wilkies in “As You Like
It,” on Government House lawn.

Lillah McCarthy, the good looking Irish

“Exile”: Melba gave a concert here in
’Frisco on March 29 in aid of the Allies’
War Funds, and raised £4200 from an audi-
ence of 15,000 people. Melba looked
queenly—or portly* as you please—in a
gown of sky-blue velvet, made in long,
straight lines with a square train. She
wore a chaplet of diamonds holding a single
paradise feather in front. The great Aus-
tralian is greater in weight and less in
voice, though maybe that was the bad
acoustic properties of the huge barn which
the ’Friscans blow about so much. In “Lo!
Here the Gentle Lark,” Melba sadly needed
John Lemmone and his flute to till out
her cadenzas. One Puyans, a Cuban, blew
the obligato, and it irresistibly reminded one
of old Martin Simonsen playing the violin
divinely, while his eyes ranged oyer the
audience trying to cheek the box-office tak-
ings. (That’s a memory of a Simonsen
concert in Bendgo 40 years back.) Tina
Lerner played the piano, and Una Bourne,
the Victorian, has played the same things
better for me. Melba wagged an Austra-
lian flag, while we exiles coo-eed and shrieked
“Good old Lilydale,” and “Good old Aus-
tralia”; in fact, there was just a little too
much of the “old” to be polite. As to the
audience—well, the chivalry and beauty of
’Frisco is rather dowdy. The women wear
expensive, ready-made clothes and vast
quantities of imitation diamonds, and the
men seem to keep their evening clothes in
cold storage, and release them for just such
occasions as this. But the crowd should
have given £42,000.
“J.E.” : I disagree with “E.F.’s” disparag-
ing criticism of operatic tenor Dani while
praising Cappelli (B. 12/4/’l7). I’ve heard
Cappelli, Balboni and Lois often, but I’ve lis-
tened in vain for Dani’s wonderful high G’s,
A’s, B's and C’s that used to thrill you and
then fade away to a long-drawn-out diminu-
endo. That is one of the great arts of vocal
production, which Cappelli and his brother
artists never employed; their high notes
seemed to be chopped off as with a meat-
axe. A Bulletin writer summed up Dani
in 1904 as “a singer with a voice like
honeyed milk” ; and that was very near it.
“A.T.S.”: I know nothing of the row said
to have occurred in Melbourne in “Madame
Angot” between Clara Thompson and Mrs.
Lambert as Lange and Clairette, but I was
present at the Royal, Adelaide, on March 9,
1877, at a performance of “Madame Angot”
when the disturbance occurred between the
Lambert and Catherine Lewis. The opera
was not finished that night. Catherine sub-
sequently apologised.

In Victoria the preferential-voting system
has been responsible for another striking
election result. There were four candidates
for Benambra, the Assembly seat vacated by
John Leckie. All four called themselves
Liberals. On the first count Leslie Sambell
led by 364 from David Langlands, of Glen-
maggie, while Henry Beardmore missed the
bottom place (George Jephcott’s) by 11
votes. Nearly half .Teph.’s second-prefs. went
to Beardmore, though on the second count
Sambell still led by 239, with Langlands
third. Langlands’ prefs., however, went 3to
1 in favor of Beardmore, who won by 273
votes. Insectarians did their best to -cloud
the main issue, which was that of national
economy. The winner in the scramble is a
strong advocate of killing the goose that lays
the golden loans.
Barrister Foster, who is opposing Watt
for Balaclava (Vic.), states from the public
platform that one manifestation of the
Prussian censorship which has been imposed
on Australia is that the authorities have
removed from public libraries copies of
Hansard containing a speech delivered by
Pearce at the time of the S’Afriean
unpleasantness, wherein Pearce expressed
doubt whether Australia would be justified in
sending troops to aid in that or any other
war in which Britain might be engaged. If
Pearce wishes to swallow his words he is
at liberty to do so; but that anyone should
use an official position to have Parliamentary
records removed from public institutions
(and Foster’s statement has not been con-
tradicted, although reported in the press)
is quite another matter.
[B. J. H. Owen, 75 Glenhuntly Road, Elwood, Vic.]
Though not a candidate, ex-Rep. Sam
Mauger is conducting a cross-country cam-
paign of his own in support of his political
principles, notably in the matters of tariff
and industrial reform ; and he is the holder
of a vast sheaf of correspondence, from all
over the State, urging him to take part in
the Senate campaign. But there is no room
for Samuel. There is, in fact, no room for
any body who isn’t machine-made. If
Samuel doesn’t please one of two small
juntas elected by nobody, Samuel has no
hope in Australian politics. The people
may choose between Smith and Jones. But
the junta reserves to itself the sole right to
choose between Smith and Thomson and
Robinson and Mulvaney and everybody else
in the directory. That is Party. It cer-
tainly isn’t Democracy.

“PROFESSIONAL” TERMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In chesty tones he asked of her
Her terms for board and bed.
The hash-house ma'am seemed filled with gloom;
“It all depends upon the room—
A guinea up,” she said.
“But I’m an aetor-r, ma’am,” declared
Hugh Montmorency Browne.
Suspicion showed upon her face.
“My terms,” she mentioned, "in that case
Will be a guinea down!”

MINCHINBURY
“Red Ned”: xl few weeks ago we hap-
pened to be stationed in the sand alongside
an English infantry regiment which owned a
dandy little captain as well-groomed as a
toy-terrier. His most important job seemed
to be to strut up and down between the two
camps until it was time to go and change
into a new uniform; and we marvelled at
his easy life until we heard he was the son
of one of the big noses in Lombard-street. To
prevent the men of his regiment deteriorat-
ing from contact with us brigands, a lecture
by the commanding officer was thought’advis-
able. When he got thoroughly warmed up he
Avorked this off: “Men, it is not all misfor-
tune to he camped near these undisciplined
Australians. For Ave have to consider the
force of our example; and I am pleased to
add they have already begun to copy us.
Only yesterday I heard one of their officers
blow a whistle,” Thank Heaven, there’s
hope for us! IVe may help to Avin this war
yet.

HE: “Bound to —common gratitude! Since

a cut out of it when he dies. ’

“Peg-Leg” : I met the jackals of the army
when malaria put me into a field hospital in
Mesopotamia, where the wards and the
kitchen were manned by coolies. When the
doctor ordered an egg each for the half-dozen
patients in the ward, the faithful nigger
cracked three only, divided them into six
equal parts, and Sold the other three at a
couple of shillings apiece. It was profit-
able when the ward had eggs three times a
day for a month or two. Incidentally, Chris-
tianity isn’t what it is cracked up to be.
The white troops in the field existed on bully
beef and condensed milk, but a flock of goats
and sheep were supplied for the colored regi-
ments, whose religion demanded that they
have only fresh meat and milk. How r ever,
we did not mind that so much as the fact
that a strict and impenetrable guard was
posted around the flock at night. That was
“dirt.”
* * * *
The enduring Australian. It happened in
Port Melbourne. Some children were lark-
ing in a stable, and after it was all over and
a couple had gone in to tea, a small daughter
next door casually informed her mother that
Artie, the youngster from over the way, was
under the floor, and couldn’t get out. Inves-
tigation disclosed that a. door had fallen back
on the boy, and pinned him to the stone
floor-. Artie, aged seven, had a shinbone and
two ribs broken. Asked by his mother, “But
why didn’t you cry out?” he answered
gravely: “I couldn’t cry. You see, we were
playing soldiers.”
# * * *
“S.E.” : An illustration of the way to win
a war. A major, who has done his share of
home service,, and was reckoned good enough
to command a battalion for many months,
has been trying ever since the war broke out
to get to the Front. A couple of weeks ago
he again wrote and begged to be allowed to
go and fight. “Are you willing to go as a
second lieutenant?” asked Victoria Barracks.
“Yes, go as anything,” declared the desper-
ate soldier. But the brains at Headquarters
devised a counter even for that. They wrote
back pointing out that there were no vacan-
cies, and actually added that, even if there
were a vacancy, the appliccant would have to
qualify at Duntroon for a second lieutenant!
This to a man who has been examined and
passed by a board of General Staff Officers
for the rank of major within the past 12
months, and who was subsequently promoted
to wear the crown ! [And this is the offi-
cial “explanation” : “Officers of the rank of
major are not being sent from Australia at
present. They are only sent when new
units are formed, and lately only reinforce-
ments have been leaving Australia.”]
“F.”: There was truth, seemingly, in the
first weird story of how China came to break
off friendly relations with Germany. The cable
stated that the Hun ambassador at Pekin,
with a view to convincing the Government
that vonderful Sherman resourcefulness
must vin in der long
r-run, bragged to a pious
mandarin of the vonder-
ful Sherman methods of
treating human corpses
for utility purposes.
Whereupon the Chinese
Minister raised his hands
in horror, and recom-
mended a diplomatic rup-
ture right away. It had
never occurred to the
clumsy Hun braggart
that reverence for the
dead is a central plank in
China’s platform, and
that the vond erf u 1
methods of turning dead
soldiers to account were
too loathsome for Chinese
words. At the time the
story was pooh-poohed as
too absurd a parody on
educated German charac-
ter. It was “an impu-
dent newspaper yarn.”
But now conformation
has come along through
the grace of the Berlin
correspondent of the
Lokal Anzeiger —a pat-
riotic journalist who re-
ferred to the “great
corpse-exploitation estab-
lishment of the Cham-
pagne army group” as a triumph of Kultur,
since that nation is most kultured which can
most completely bend science to its own use.
“Costs” : Re soldiers’ wills. The briefest
I know, and as good as any, was scribbled
by an Adelaide Bllljim in his pay-book:
"Everything to mother.” And very likely he
was.
“Opip”: The other night we went over
and took 300 yards of trench and some
prisoners. Next morning 05 of them were
being marched to the rear, when a cheerful
Australian lad called out: “Well, who’s win-
ning now, Fritzy?” But Fritzy wasn’t in a
mood for jokes. “Look at der plauky map!"
was the straight left that staggered most of
us. This stung the escort a bit. Further
back we passed a couple of Sin. howitzers.
Alongside one 10 shells stood ready for
immediate use. “Not too many shells for
your big pieces,” sneered Fritz. “Yairs,”
drawled Billjim, the conqueror, "when
we’ve fired that lot we’re licked!” In the
roar that followed it was Fritzy’s turn to
feel uncomfortable.
NOTHING MUCH REQUIRED.
STAGE MANAGES: “I wish you would take the part — it’s in aid of the Red Cross, you
know.”
LADY : “I’m sorry, hut I couldn’t afford to dress it.”
STAGE MANAGER: “Oh, that would he nothing! It’s all in the country and outside, you

“Peru”: The 7000-ton Lanfranc, recently
submarined while acting as hospital ship,
was, except for her sister craft the Antony,
the biggest steamer that traded regularly
from Liverpool to Manaos, 1000 miles up the
Amazon river. If the fine Australian coastal
boat Katoomba be excepted, she was also
probably the most comfortable passenger
boat afloat for her size. She was built in
1906, and owned with some 30 other steam-
ers by A. A. Booth and his family. Booth
at the age of 36 became chairman of the
Cunard Line, and was made a baronet last
year for managing the transport work of
the British Government when the war broke
out. The Booth Line problem was to have
quick-carriers, fitted for passengers, to bring
the valuable rubber freights from the Ama-
zon. A single cargo was occasionally worth
half a million sterling. The writer once
remarked to the juvenile Cunard chairman
on the Liverpool wharf, as the Lanfranc was
moving away from her berth: “She’s a
pretty ship.” “And she does as pretty as
she is,” said her proud owner. The line
paid a uniform 10 p.c. I can imagine how
he felt when the Huns murdered the beauty
of the fleet.
Prom our country contemporaries:—
Dogleg Gully, Monday.—The plague of mice has
been responsible for a tragedy which has cast a
gloom over the whole district. Brought to the verge
of ruin by the devouring element in the shape of the
voracious rodent, Mr. Fergus McHaggis, a respected
fellow-townsman, was endeavoring to wile away an
hour by the perusal of the works of Scotia’s bard.
Unfortunately he came across a eulogistic reference
to the furry denizen of the wheat-stacks in a poem
where Burns described the mouse as a “wee, sleekit,
cow’rin, timorous beastie.” Mr. McHaggis fell a
victim to the apoplectic seizure which followed.
Ants’ Nest, Tuesday.—The mouse plague is
assuming alarming proportions. The local ladies are
living on tables, with their feet in dishes full of
water, having been reduced to a state of nervous
prostration by the invading animals. The school-
mistress yesterday reached the top of the ■ tank in
one bound in her effort to avoid a mouse which
forced her to cry peccavi. A perpendicular dis-
tance of Bft. was cleared, and nothing like it has
been accomplished within the memory of the oldest
inhabitant, who was a witness of Gordon’s Leap
at Mount Gambier.
Wattle Flat, Friday.— A picture-show which
screened selections from yEsop’s Fables has met with
a strange fate. Just as the display had been com-
pleted of the pictures accompanying the story of the
mouse freeing the lion by gnawing through the net
which had caught him, the performance came to an
abrupt conclusion owing to mice invading the hall
and eating the bioscope, the operator, and the
films. Mr. Hagelthorn is to be asked to provide free
cats and mousetraps for farmers.
“Wheatstone” : The one article harder to
procure than kangaroo feathers here in Eng-
land is an Australian slouch hat. He is a
very wide awake Billjim who can snare one,
when his own is missing from the rack. The
Hun may blow his tunic off or shrapnel his
trousers to ribbons, but it’s a dead Billjim
that parts with his hat. That hat is the
Anzac trade-mark ; and don’t these fellows
love their country—mine too !

REFLECTED GLORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dave Scougal, pruning roses, turned
To snarl as Parsons passed.
Ilis eye with vengeful anger burned,
He breathed an evil blast;
And Purves, walking on a crutch,
Straight opened out, and he
Made brief reply that covered much
Of Scougal’s pedigree.
“Cold footer!” David Scougal cried,
And Purves with a chain
Of choice profanity replied.
Then David came again.
“Just look,” said he to half a score
Of grinning local imps.
“His brother’s wounded at the war—
He stays at home and limps!”

“Three Stripes”: Private Billjim will
have to put his roving eye in a refrigerator.
The staff at A.I.F. Headquarters, Horse-
terry-road, London, has the pip because
Brig.-Gen. “Bob” Anderson has insisted that
no unofficial communications may take
dace between the 1500 uniforms and the
1000 petticoats on the premises. The build-
ing, formerly a Methody teaching institute,
is a perfect warren of cubby holes and
offices, and, says an official, soldiers and
?irls “are thrown together under circum-
stances that almost invite gossiping and
lirting.” He goes on to say that “several
ncidents” resulting from the association of
the sexes have been brought under notice;
lence the new regulation, which will remain
n force until new quarters are found for the
Oris and women.
A story of these “undisciplined” Aus-
tralians :—-
A company of the A.I.F. were told to hang on to
m outpost till reserves came up and were doing so
when the O.C. sent in word that he was almost out
of ammunition. A staff-officer of the Imperial A.S.C.
sent for a sergeant of the A.I.F. and ordered him and
lis platoon to guard a convoy of five motor-waggons
loaded with ammunition, and take them along the
main road to the outpost. “I can never get through,
dr. But I could get through across country behind
the hills,” he said. “Your orders are to go by the
main road, and no other route,” replied the staff-
officer, and the jarred sergeant went off to get his
olatoon ready. lie mounted his men on the motors,
started, and soon ran into a hail of bullets, but
lushed on. Half-way through the leading motor
Topped ; a bullet had penetrated the water tank and
disabled it. The sergeant backed his motor, and
getting a towline out, pulled it along and fought
through to the outpost to the great joy of the O.G.
Then they started to unload the motors, only to find
every box empty ! The sergeant was giving his
opinion of Imperial staff-officers when a second con-
voy of motors was seen approaching from another
direction. They roared up, and were found to have
;heir boxes full. All hands set to to hold the outpost,
and did so till reserves arrived and the Huns dis-
persed. Then the sergeant reloaded his motors with
his empty boxes, returned to headquarters and re-
ported—oh, so sweetly—to the staff-officer, who
looked at him with a grim smile. Then lie unbent

From a wounded Billjim officer in Blighty :
If the Australian at home wants to do something
’or the Australian abroad let him knock off the
persistent comparison between the earnestness of
England and the indifference of Australia. Nothing
jould be less true, nothing more painful to the
feelings of the Australian in England. There
would be much more appropriateness in Australia
isking Young England to take this business seriously.
It doesn’t. With these people it is football, foot-
ball, football. They think football, they talk foot-
ball. They would have regarded Kitchener as a big-
man if he had played football. Certainly hundreds
of thousands of them would then have felt the
tragedy of his death as a more personal loss.
MOTLEY NOT THE ONLY WEAR.
“We are right in it —stripes seem to he all
the go.”
“Oh, no! The men who ‘go’ are in khaki.”
“Caroline”: I am a teacher in one of the
North Sydney schools. On Anzac Day I
asked the children to tell me anything they
knew of the Gallipoli campaign. One little
chap told me he had two brothers, twins,
who had gone to Gallipoli at the same time,
had both been wounded in the legs, and had
returned together. “Wasn’t it grand.” I
said, “to get them both back again! What
did mother say then?” “She didn’t say any-
thing. but father did.” “What did he say,
Jack?” “‘Serves yer right fer goin’!’ ”
They are telling of an “official” candidate
for a Vic. provincial district who had rather
a bad time at the hands of one energetic
interjector. She was an aggressive female
who attacked him at practically all his
meetings, and often got home on him with
personal' reflections and revelations. The
candidate bore it with almost pitiful meek-
ness, declining to have the lady
ejected or otherwise disposed of.
He said he hoped he could hold
his own against a woman in
any circumstances, but he said
it with such dejection that the
assertion did not carry convic-
tion half a yard. At a small
Mechanics’ Hall meeting in an
outskirt township the other
night the dame (who came to be
known as “the woman in the
red hat”) bobbed up again, cut-
ting into the most effective part
of the candidate’s address, and
started what threatened to be a
protracted oration on the quali-
fications of women as politi-
cians, judges, etc. Here the
poor man on the platform lost
his last shred of patience. “Now, for
Heaven’s sake, sit down, Marie!” he
wailed. “You know you gave me all
that in bed last night.” (It was all
right. The electorate now knows them
better. The lady is the candidate’s lawful
wedded wife.)
The Ballarat has just been torpedoed in
the English Channel with Australian rein-
forcements aboard; and they behaved as
splendidly as the other Australians did on
the Southland. It was this P. and O. boat
that The Bulletin told a story about a
while ago to illustrate the administration
of the Naval Department, though, of course,
her name couldn’t be mentioned at the time.
On being employed to bring Tommies from
England to Mesopotamia, she was torn to
pieces; among other things a smoke-room
for the officers was built on deck. Coming
on to Australia the smoke-room (which had
been in use just 23 days) was torn down in
Sydney and a hospital was put up in its
place. The hospital was practically unused
during the voyage back from Australia. Then
it was pulled to pieces. How many times
this pretty little game has been played Tiie
Bulletin doesn’t know. All it knows is
that, for lack of co-ordination between Brit-
ish and Australian authorities, thousands
of pounds were simply thrown away on this
ship alone.
“Polyphemus”: Industrial Magistrate
Morris (Townsville) had to listen recently
to a long discussion on female clerks and
their rate of pay. Certain local employers
protested against giving women equal pay
with men, on the
ground that, as these
employers had proved
in their own employ-
ment, girls were not
as strong physically
and had not the same
energy, concentration
or initiative as men.
It may be heresy to
say so nowadays, but
I believe all this is
perfectly true. Years
ago, as the head of an
important State De-
partment, I was con-
tinually worried by the
heads of sub-depart-
ments growling at
being compelled to in-
troduce female clerks;
wit li out exception
these heads declared
that they found female
clerks comparatively
inefficient. There ought
by this time to be a
mass of trustworthy
evidence on the point;
and it is of great im-
portance that it should
be collated. For, obvi-
ously, if the general
experience is as I have
found it in my own
State, the female worker
will be the sufferer in
the long run by a rule
which declares that, if
employed at all, she
must receive the same pay as the male.
Melbourne Uni., having turned out one
complete-course farmer in 10 years, has
lately been considering the position of its
Agricultural branch. A committee, told off
by the Uni. Council to make a report, recom-
mends, among other things, that six State
jobs per annum should be guaranteed by the
Government to graduates of the school.
There is something in this. In the U.S.A.,
for instance, the trained agricultural expert
is being widely used as a travelling educator
for the already-established farmer, showing
him how to stop waste and produce more,
and this has been responsible for a big agri-
cultural uplift. So a sound, largely-practi-
cal, special course in agriculture, with its
hoofs pared and its tail combed out, may yet:
be an excellent thing for Australian produc-
tion, even though its immediate results are
only a crop of teachers and special-job men.
“Tenbob”: This is how the Civil Service
does it. I was employed at a Common-
wealth Department in Sydney,
when the boss (salary £750 per
annum) found two Id. mouse-
traps were needed. Now :i>pri-
vate employer would have given
the office-boy tuppence and 10
minutes to complete the job—
including the baiting and set-
ting. But that isn’t the Gov-
ernment way. The usual “for-
mal order” was typewritten, and
given to the buyer (salary £2lO
p.a.). A long foolscap receipt
note was also punched out, and
the buyer went forth and ran-
sacked the city to find where
penny traps were cheapest. The
winning firm duly delivered and
invoiced the goods on the usual
Government form, which was
signed by aforesaid boss (£750
p.a.) in one place and initialled
by him in another. The account
was then “certified” and signed
by another oflicah in two places
£3BO p.a.), and duly
entered in an accounts
register by another man
(£l5O p.a.). A cheque
was then drawn for the
assassinating apparatus
by a clerk (£2BO p.a.).
The cheque was posted
to the firm bv another
clerk (£ISO p.a.). The
account was then entered
in the monthly list of ex-
penditure by a third clerk
(£l9O p.a.), and sent to
Melbourne, where they will have another
circus with it. Now run along and pay
your Federal Income Tax.
The usual charge was brought against a
country youth at St. Kilda (Vic.) the other
day. The boy was alleged to have enticed
the girl to the beach and ravished her,
despite her strenuous resistance. The yarn
looked convincing until counsel for the
defendant got going. He said that his client
had on several previous occasions been giddy
with the lady, with her consent; and finally
the little dear acknowledged that she had
been a consenting party on the occasion
before the court. Also she admitted that
another man had been sentenced to four
years’ imprisonment for a similar offence
alleged against him by her. When first ques-
tioned about this case she professed not to
remember it, and subsequently explained her
forgetfulness by remarking: “Oh, that was
three or four years ago !” At this stage the
Bench told the accused youth that he might
go home, and the damsel also tripped from
the court without even being assured that
she was a bold girl.

“T. 0.1.” : Apropos the Read baronetcy (B.
26/4/T7). The following appeared in
“Whittaker’s Peerage,” 1909 :
Read Baronetcy (England 1641 in the name of
Reade), extinct 1711, but was assumed in Australia
as 9th baronet by the late John Cecil Read, and is
now persisted in by his son, William Vero (of Arawa,
Waverley, N. S. Wales). As usual in such cases,
the claim is based upon some late discovered unreg-
istered relative; but no authority whatever makes
the slightest mention of such line, and our only
information is derived from an Australian newspaper,
which gravely charges us all with resting in gross
ignorance. Other untraceable titles make their
appearance at the Antipodes from time to time;
thus in January, 1904, there was a report of the
death of “Sir James Hamilton, Bt” ; in the following
April appeared that of a soi-disant Knight, Sir
11. D. C. Ohapelle,” and only a little later that of
a “Lady Diy,” widow of some supposed Knight of
Tasmania.
But “Whittaker” is undoubtedly in error
about the Drys, as the Speck had Sir Richard
Dry, a Speaker and Premier of Tasmania, a
native of that State, distinguished in every
walk of life.
* * * *
At South Melbourne, an Industrial suburb
which swarms with children, Not ley Moore,
P.M., recently had before him what he
described as “the worst case [of adulterated-
milk selling] ever before the court.” The
evidence showed that defendant and his
brother had gone for a ride on the old milk-
cart, taking with them four cans —one filled
with water, another with skim-milk, a third
with milk-up-to-standard and a fourth con-
taining milk plus 27 per cent, of added
water. The PAL sniffed and remarked that
the combination of cans was significant:
then he lifted up his official feather and
smote defendant with a fine of £B. If a
citizen is found killing babies with arsenic,
or by tying tape around their necks, there
is liable to be a hanging soon. But when a
milk-seller peddles slow murder in a suburb
packed with children, the Law, after roar-
ing at the murderer, whacks the profits with
him and lets him go. It couldn’t give him
“the office” more plainly if it. winked at him
as it saw him off.
A HARD CASE.
In England there is a common impression that
two-thirds of the Anzacs are the sons of wealthy men.
LUCINDA: “Is it true that your father cut
you off with a shilling?"
JIMBTLL: “Yes. And I had to lend him the
shilling.”
THE WAY OF IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
K chill, slow voice and a steel-cold glance,
And a white face turned away
And the sun on the waters forgot to dance,
And the gold world turned to grey.
Twin hearts that ache with a leaden beat,
And a mouth made pale with pain....
And two that meet in a crowded street,
And draw together again.
A pressure of hands, a look in the eyes,
And a voice no longer c01d....
A light leaps out of the scowling skies,
And a grey world turns to gold !

It was one of the Smiths who saved the
day at Lagnicourt, turned an Australian
repulse into a German rout, and gave Bill-
jim such a chance to nse his rifle as he had
not had since May, 1915, when tile Turks
were laid out in mown swaths on Gallipoli.
The_ counter-attack which recovered the Aus-
tralian guns and slew 3000 Germans was
organised by Colonel Bob Smith, D. 5.0.. in
charge of the Fifth Brigade. Smith, a fine,
upstanding soldier of 36, left Australia as
second, in command to Crouch, of the 22nd
Battalion. In December last, after acting
as colonel of another brigade, he was given

Died at Parramatta (N.S.W.) last week
a stalwart native of the sleepy town, W. J.
Ferris, ruddy-visaged and amiable, with a
big heart and generous pocket; M.L.A. for a
while, when he talked little and worked
overtime; more than 30 years a municipal
magnate ; pioneer of the volunteer soldiering
system. He was a sturdy Protectionist;
and on the only occasion when Parramatta
electors threw away their old fetish it was
Ferris who persuaded them to do it. He
had had 59 years of sunshine when he
passed out after a short illness.
The exploit of a Victorian youngster
makes the fabled phoenix look like a stuffed
Plymouth Rock. Flight Lieut. Frank
Hubert McNamara, who was 22 when he
left his job as State-school teacher and
joined the Flying Corps as a lieutenant last
year, was despatched 60 miles with another
aviator to destroy the railway along which
Abdul was sending reinforcements to Gaza.
He dropped two bombs on the line, and, at
a height of 100 ft,, flew up and down a
troop train, outing the passengers with his
machine-gun. Wounded, he took flight, but
swooped to the rescue of his mate, whose
aeroplane had come to grief. Owing to his
wounds he smashed his own machine on
landing. The pilot and observer of the
other ’plane were out of action, but in the
face of the oncoming Turks McNamara burnt
his own machine-and rose from its ashes in
the other, bringing the pilot and the observer
off. He is now in hospital at Alexandria.
The lad, who has a brother in the artillery
in France, is a son of W. F. McNamara,
one time president of the A.N.A. He has
been recommended for the V.C.
Henry Carey Dangar, N. S. Wales M.L.C.,
who died last week, aged 86 years, was son of
that Henry Dangar who came from Corn-
wall to Australia in the ’2o’s, and obtained
employment as a Government surveyor at
Newcastle, which city he laid out in part.
In 1826 he - obtained a grant of 700 acres
near Singleton, and named it after his birth-
place, Neotsfield. Later on, he went further
out and had a station known as Myall Creek,
which in IS3S became notorious over the
holocaust of a tribe of blacks, for which a
number of men were, executed in the gaol
yard in Lower George-street. Henry Carey
Dangar was called to the English Bar in
1854. had chambers in King-street, but never
sought briefs. In his young days he was
enthusiastic in rifle shooting and a patron
of cricket. His wife, a daughter of Captain
Lamb, died a year ago.
Now that the Czar lias been thrown out
of his job the wonder grows that he should
have been taken seriously
for so long. An interesting
yarn entitled “Frightening
the Czar” was published in
Uondon five years ago. A
Russian countess, formerly
billeted at the Court, told
how an apparition of the
Czar’s late unlameuted
father, lying in a coffin, was
fixed up in a room adjoining
his study and introduced to
his notice by sepulchral
noises. The whole ghostly
bus’ness was worked from
below by means of trap-
doors. ITol,v-man Rasputin,
lately assassinated. was
pulling the strings. The
countess got. a graphic
account of the appalling
show from the Czar himself
in the first place, and then
she consulted the doctor
who had been called in to
view the corpse of a soldier
supposed to have died of
fright at the apparition, but
which had probably been
dragged into the imposture
before the conspirators
started to scare the Czar.
The royal palace reeked of
Rasputinism five years ago,
and was an old-established
scandal even then. Yet as
recently as New Year week
we were getting cabled
accounts of the Czar’s move-
ments and manifestoes as
though lie were a sane
person of importance!
John J. Cohen, though the ,13th Speaker
of the N. S. Wales Leg. Assembly, is only
the second graduate of Sydney ’Varsity to
hold the position. “Toby” Barton was the
first. Colien graduated M.A. in 1881 and
was called to the Bar in ’94.
J. J. Stuckey, just dead in Adelaide at 74,
was one of those brilliant specialists who
never quite “arrive” unless they find their
own special groove to run in. About 1860
he was showing an almost uncanny aptitude
for mathematics at St. Peter’s College, Ade-
laide. He was the first South Australian
to go to Cambridge, and there he became a
“wrangler”—fifth in all England (of his
year) in his pet subject. But the Higher
Mathematics were hard to make a living out
of, and he took up Law, in which he proved
to be no better than other men less scho-
lastically brilliant. In later years, with
declining health, he returned to his first love,
and only last year published a book of
actuarial tables, the result of long and
patient calculation. Stuckey ought to have
been a Professor, and in these days of multi-
plication of universities doubtless would have
been one.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” : A controversy
is raging as to whether the recently-dead
Rev. Wm. Scott was the first Australian
astronomer. A relative of Mr. Dunlop, of
Governor Brisbane’s time, claims for him
the honor. Both are in error. The first
Australian astronomer was Lieut. William
Dawes, of the Marines, who was placed by
Governor Phillip in charge of the observa-
tory on Point Maskelyne, so named after
the then Astronomer Royal of England, but
afterwards called “Dawes Point.” The
observatory was built “to receive the astro-
nomical instruments sent out by the Board
of Longitude” for the purpose of observing
a comet which was expected to be seen about
the end of the year.
Signed off the other day at Forthside
(Tas.), John Daly, probably the Speck’s
oldest inhabitant. He came from County
Kerry in the misty long ago, and claimed to
he 106 years old. But the record will give
teetotallers no joy, for he learnt to respect
whisky and tobacco when he was a boy of 14
and enjoyed them to the end.
Died of wounds in France. Major Price
J. Morgan, a youngster that Launceston has
always expected a lot from, right from the
days when as a nipper he started to win
scholarships. He led the first batch of Tas-
manians to Duutroon, and went through
Gallipoli as” Lieutenant of the 9th Battery.
After going on to France lie was promoted
Captain of the 17th Battery; then he was
given a spell of staff work, and recently was
sent back to the 17th with the crown on liis
youthful shoulder.
Dead, at East Maitland (N.S.W.), George
T. Chambers, gent, one, etc., at the age of
74. He was born at Maitland, and came of
a family of lawyers; he himself was
admitted to practice in 1867. One
Charles Henry Chambers, solicitor, was
the first town-clerk of Sydney. This
early municipal official was the father
of Miss Lucy < lumbers, the well-known
contralto of the '(id's and ’7o’s. Another of
the family. Hugh John Chambers, was high
up in the profession in Victoria, and on one
occasion also distinguished himself as an
amateur actor, being Lieutenant Worthington
in “The Poor Gentleman” on the occasion of
a benefit to G. I’. Brooke.
Crossed the Jordan at 84. Alfred Marson
Nash Rose, of Campbelltown (N.S.W.). A
century ago his father, Thomas Rose, kept
The Rose and Crown, on the site now occu-
pied by the Daily Telegraph in Sydney.
The lucky pub-keeper had a grant of all the
block bounded by King, Elizabeth, Market
and Castlereagh streets, and made it bloom
as a garden. The pub faced Castlereagli-
street, and was the headquarters of Syd-
ney’s early “sports,” the racecourse being on
Hyde Park. In 1819 Governor Macquarie
wanted a. spot to build a large school-house,
and, deciding on a portion of Rose's garden,
informed Rose that if he would give up the
land he could have a grant lip the country.
Rose agreed, and Macquarie built his
academy, which modern Sydney knows as
the Girls’ High School. In lieu of it Rose
received 1300 acres on the east side of Camp-
belltown, and it was there his son died.
Jonathan Tozer, who passed out at Sydney
the other day, aged 62, was a brother of
now deceased Horace, one-time Queensland
politician and Agent-General. He had been
in the service of the Bank of N. S. Wales
for over 40 years, and had kept an eye on
the overdrafts at Mudgee, Crookwell, Coota-
munclra and other N.S.W. country towns.
Son Claude, a doctor, has been wearing
khaki with the A.M.C. in Gallipoli and
France for two years past.
Prof. Larry Rentoul has got His
Majesty’s special permission to publish a
pome entitled “Australia’s Message to King
George V.” The message would have been
just as truly loyal without the special per-
mission, but somehow Larry couldn’t be
happy till he got it. Pie wanted it, in fact,
for paragraphic advertisement of his new
booklet of verses. And there are people
who deny the usefulness of the monarchy !

J. T. McMahon, killed in action near
Lagnicourt on Easter Monday, was an old
boy of St. Joseph’s College, Hunter’s Hill
(N.S.W.), and three years ago got his B.E.
degree at Sydney Uni. Some months before
reaching his 21st birthday McMahon was a
demonstrator in the Uni. engineering school;
afterwards he was an engineer in the Ma
State’s Works Department.
HE GALLOPED ROUND AND ROUND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Senator Pearce at Shop >arton likened Mr. Tudor
and other members of the Official. Labor Party in
their treatment of Mr. Hughes to snarling curs at
the feet of a galloping horse. — Argus.
He galloped round and round and round and snorted
as he went;
Upon the task of getting-therc his energies were bent.
From here to yonder, lap by lap, persistently he
pressed
With eyes that sought the track ahead and deeply-
heaving- chest,
And as around the roaring ring so urgently he tore,
Each moment brought him to a spot where he had
been before.
He galloped round and round—and how the talent
yelled to see
The trained pack, hard upon his heels, pursue him
earnestly !
Like some swift shadow speeding through the Land
of Vain Desire
He sprinted round and round and round and never
seemed to tire.
A pale, stern look of superhuman confidence he wore.
And passed along to pass more posts that he had
passed before. ' ■
He galloped round like anything, in quite apparent
haste,
As though he had an urgvet call, with little time
to waste,
In much the manner, first and last, -of one by
hornet i stung ;
And all the time the pack pursued him, loudly giving
tongue;
While every stride lie compassed added something
to the score
Of moments when he reached a spot he'd often
reached before.
Ho galloped round and round and round and as lic_
made the pace
The pack pursued him, persevering grimly in the
chase.
Whither he went they went, of course, and while he
circled they
Kept circling, too, and snapped and snarled in quite
a serious way.
Yet, arovncl the roaring ring perspiringly they
tore,
Each moment brought them back again to where
they’d been before.
GLEN IRIS, VIC.
FRANK HENTY.

In this annual report Richard Teece, for 27
years general manager and actuary, says
good-bye to the policy-holders, as he has
reached the age at which the society’s Pro-
vident Fund calls him off the salary list.
The directors announce, however, that he
will continue to serve as consulting actuary ;
and any insurance office in the world would
be the better for having him alongside to
ask for an opinion. Mr. Teece has witnessed
tremendous changes not only in the figures
of his society but in the general attitude
towards life assurance. This, as well as
the prosperity of the country has produced a
Splendid increase in the business during his
period of office. Of the 555.091 policies
issued during the 68 years’ life of the'
society no less than 433.012 have been
issued since IS9O. Mainly because of this
preponderance of comparatively new busi-
ness no less than 54.2 p.c. of all the policies
ever issued in the Ordinary department are
still in force. And yet there is a lot of
work for the insurance missionary to do:
for, notwithstanding that Australia is
blessed with offices second to none in the
world, it carries less insurance per head
than Canada, with which it can most fairly
be compared. ‘ \
The past year has been notable for the
heaviness of the claims. Through the death
of members the society has had to pay out
£14512.240, and of this amount no less than
£471,480 is due to claims-arising from the
war: in the previous year the war claims
totalled £343,053. The result of the heavy
mortality among young and comparatively
young policy-holders is to rather seriously
affect bonuses. The following table show's
the history of the society in that respect:—
1888-1893 (boomyears). . 40.3 p.c. of premiums.

That, notwithstanding the heavy special
calls upon it, the society should be able to
devote oyer 30s: of every £5 to the purchase
of additional insurance for the premium-
payer is a very excellent performance. It
would not be possible if war had no eom-
uensations at all for an office like this. But
in the first place it has made money dearer.

No doubt special circumstances have been
helping a bit: many men. before setting out
to the Front, will have taken new policies
or increased old ones at special war-rates
without putting the society to any expense
for canvassing. But that probably no more

doubtless directly responsible for the entry,
“Special bonus to staff, £5420.”

pledged any of its future revenue in order
to subscribe to War loans. It owes nothing
except to its policyholders.

Of 264,247 policies issued in the 12 years
dining which the A.M.P. has taken this class
of business, 113,451. representing £3,998,927,
are void through death or lapse; and when
you run the eye down the column devoted
to claims, and find the total amounts to
only about- £77,000, you get some idea of
the deplorable, number of lapses. This in-
evitably makes this class of business expen-
sive : an almost exact one-third of all pre-
miums collected goes in exes., as against
about one-eighth in the Ordinary depart-
ment. The only sensible thing, therefore,
for any person to do, after he has got into
the insuring habit, is to transfer from the
Industrial to the Ordinary, and thereby
save very nearly 4s. in the £ of his pre-
miums.

The Colonial has got back to the average of
the profits it made just prior to the war.
There are banks which ought to be and no
doubt are making a lot more than they did
then; but the Colonial isn’t as well placed
as some of them. Here are ’ the balance-
sheet figures:—
Liabilities.
1914. 1916. 1917.
Notes £20,163 £18,525 £18,235
Bills . . . . . . 392,979 294,174 156,394
Free deposits . ; 1,723,788 1,886,188 1,782,360
Deposits at Interest. . 2,302,538 2,531,048 2,534,386
. Assets. -
Liquid assets . . 2,078,787 1,870,197 2,013,941.
Advances . . . . 2,845,215 3,387,430 3,023,027
Premises, etc. .. 195,606 197,438 196,195
So this bank has got only about £60.000
more free money than it had at the begin-
ning of 1914. True, it is using all of it,
and has about £65,000 less liquid and semi-
liquid assets. But it is doubtful whether it
is making as much profit out of those liquid
assets; for. though £130,000 more is in pub-
lic- securities, there is nearly £400,000 less
in "Bills and remittances in transitu and in
London,” which indicates a serious falling-
away in a very profitable branch of Austra-
lian banking business. Meantime, of course,
exes, are crawling up. “Note, income and
land taxes” cost £3771 last half-year, as
against £1942 in the half ended March, 1914 ;
ahd they aTe going to cost a lot more yet.
But prophecies about the future of Aus-
tralian banks and banking profits are dan-
gerous dissipations. This, however, can
be said pretty confidently: it is only when
Australian banks have to meet exceptional
losses of principal that they can fail to show
at least respectable profits. In other words,
in the easy banking conditions that prevail
in Australia the institutions can so accommo-
date themselves to circumstances that they
can make fair profits even when general
trade conditions are bad. It isn’t impos-
sible, of course, that they will make losses
of principal during the next few years. That
will depend largely upon the price we can
get for our staple products. If there should
be a bad slump in them, almost anything
may happen, in view of our tremendous
: foreign debt. We pay interest with our
products; and if Australia had to send three
bales of wool to do the work that one does
now, which would leave two bales less for
ourselves, even the banks would begin to feel
very unwell. The unknown quantity is the
state of the markets after the war. It seems
inevitable that those of Europe will be dis-
turbed. To what extent that will hurt us
will depend largely upon whether we have
energy and sense enough to develop anew
market in Asia. The market is there all
right; blit so far we haven’t got it. Why,
ev.-n among the pretty considerable Austra-
lian population of the Malay States tin-
fields, last Christmas was celebrated with,
among other things, English hams. And
the damper was mostly constructed of
American flour, which makes something that
tastes like putty in the Australian mouth.

This co. has a profit-sharing scheme, and-
that makes some of the figures in the table
differ from those in the published accounts.
For instance, in the year to the end of*
February, 1916, the accounts showed a profit
of £24,557 ; but £4107 was then transferred
for the benefit of the staff, reducing the
profit to £20,450. In the past year the
accounts show a profit of £24.786; but £1976
is similarly transferred. Of the profits thus
far dealt with £8570 is now capital repre-
sented by staff partners’ certificates. Just
how it -should be treated in such a table
as that which stands at the top of these
remarks admits of argument. In effect the
table as, presented shows, as nearly as pos-
sible, the position from the investing share-
holders’ point of view. And that seems
right, because the theory underlying all
profit-sharing schemes is that, by reason of
the greater interest which the employee will
take, the profit of the employer will at least
be as great in the long run as if he did not
give the employee anything at all. It will
be a fine thing if Wunderlichs and other
co-operators prove this to be so. And it
ought to be more than possible. In a busi-
ness of this size and character, for instance,
it should be easy for employees, by saving
time and material, to add not £1976. but
three times £1976, to the co.’s net earnings. ■
Any scheme which reduces; the waste of time ' 5
and material in Australia is certainly worth
three cheers. It is of national value and
worth more than all your “thrift weeks.”
Compared with the three years prior to
February. 1914. the past three years have
shown poor results. Nothing else could be
reasonably expected. The co. has been hit
two ways—perhaps three. The (perhaps)
third is through its name. “Wunderlich”
looked German ; but these Wunderlichs have
been British for three generations, and any-
thin!! less like Frightfulness than the artistic
idealists who formed this co. and still con-
trol it could not well be conceived. The
other two disabilities were (1) the slowing-
down of construction when the war started
and (2) the stoppage of shipments of
materials drawn from foreign parts and
such a rise in the price of other goods as to
put them out of bounds. The co.
will go on suffering for a good while yet
from the slowing-down in the construction
and modernisation of buildings. Apart from
the fact that Australia —-and Sydney in par-
ticular—had a borrowing orgy, which
always in Australia means a rush of city
and suburban buildings, we will have a big
foreign bill to pay presently ; and when we
have a big foreign bill to pay there is always
a movement back to the land, and the men
who go on the land are not —for a start, at
least—much good to Wunderlichs. Stili
Wunderlichs have imagination ; and in busi-
ness imagination is the quality that sets a
man making things to supply anew need
before it arrives. Sometimes he has to
create the need'; usually lie has to show
that it exists. Meanwhile note that the
present dividends don’t.absorb quite 75 p.c.
of the net unfits; and that is a handy
npirgin until the new business arrives.
Reserves are referred to in the table as
nominal. This paper's practice is not to
regard goodwill as an asset; but in this case
the goodwill could not for some years be
separated from other assets. Now. however,
it is known that goodwill stands against
£65,702 of the capital. Actually, therefore,
the co. lias tangible assets £28.675 in excess
of capital and liabilities, exclusive of the
£8570 of staff mirtners’ certificates. These
tangible assets include freeholds, factories,
stocks, book debts and £65.704 of “invest-
ments and deposits.” The capital is £40,000
in Ist 7 p.c. profs . £40.000 in 2nd 7 p.c.
prefs. and £142 501 in ordinaries. The
prefs., considering the interest, have a
sweet security; and that security is being
improved by every addition to the staff-
partners’ fund, if only because every such
addition adds to the value of the goodwill.

Broken Hill is still troubled. Mainly as
an outcome of I.W.W. activity in the
Miners’ Union there, another union was
formed some time ago; but the militants
refuse to regard its members as within the
pale, though they have been officially regis-
tered, and have just as much right to regard
the A.M.A. as the “scab” body. The em-
ployers naturally lean towards the new
union, the old one having shown that it is
prepared to disregard any Arbitration ruling
that it doesn’t like; and sooner or later
there is going to he trouble, and if the
Government isn’t watchful and firm the
outcome threatens to be very bad.
Seeing that it is preparing on its side for
a fight, the A.M.A. judges that the other
side is not asleep either; wherefore every
attempt made by the cos. to get labor in
from outside is resisted as part of a scheme
to strengthen their hands and weaken the
chances of a successful strike presently.
Just at present the B.H. South, in parti-
cular, is endeavoring to get men from out-
side. Its claim is that its lead mill, though
capable of handling' 7000 tons of ore per
week; is supplied with less than 5000 tons;
for the month ended April 7 the average
was only 4645 tons per week. The mine is
quite capable of supplying 7000 tons —it
could under pressure give 25 p.c. more—■
and that 2500 tons less are produced than
both mine and mill are capable of is said to
be wholly due to the shortage of men. It
is added that the Allies want lead badly,
while there are a couple of thousands of
men on the Sydneyside who have been nut
off railway work and want other jobs. The
A.M.A. contention is that the talk about
the Allies wanting lead is pretty and capti-
vating, but dishonest, inasmuch as it isn’t
concentrates the Allies want, but lead; and
the lead smelters can’t take any more con-
centrates than they are handling now; in
fact, the works at Port Pirie are said to
be simply choked up with supplies.
The whole question is one which calls for
careful inquiry and handling. If there is,
bona fide, work at Broken Hill for a large
proportion of the men who are being ds-
cliarged from railway construction jobs in
N. S. Wales, then they ought to be assisted
there; it should not be competent for any

By keeping its nose to the grindstone and
putting on no frills this co. manages to pay
its 4 p.c. and have perhaps a little more over
than enough to counter-balance .wear and
tear. This shows how the position has
changed in 10 years :—■
Dec., ’O6. Dec., ’l6.
Liabilities £40,708 £33,429
Assets other than fixed assets . . 17,318 12,522
Excess liabilities . . . . £23,390 £20,907
So the co. has paid for its repairs and
replacements and what small additions there
have been, and in 10 years is £2453 to the
good. Gas-sales in the second half of 1900
were £5728; last half-year, £6937. But there
has been a far greater improvement in the
revenue from by-products (coke, tar, etc.)
from £730 to £1345, and in some terms it is
more than that. On the other side all exes,
are higher—except directors’ and auditors’
fees. They, uever large, are now an extremely
modest £42. The co. has the bad luck to
have to compete with a municipal supply of
very cheaply-produced electric current; and
that undertaking dispenses with frills, too,
which makes the competition all the more
serious.

This is a remarkable little semi-co-operative
concern which regards the sale of the share-
holders’ milk at a fair price as its chief busi-
ness in life—not the making of dividends.
So, though profits have far exceeded the 10
p.c. dividend, the distribution hasn’t been
increased, the surplus being put into pro-
perty and plant to economically handle a far
greater turnover than was originally con-
templated. Actually on a share capital of
only £1462 (exclusive of £1827 forfeited)
this is how the business now stands:—
1900. 1910. 1915. 1916.

A lot certainly depends on the valuation of
the freehold and plant; but apparently the
assets are kept written down for deprecia-
tion, though the statements might be much
more definite on the point.
Increased smelting charges having made
it impossible for lodide (N.S.W.) to profit-
ably handle its low-grade oxidised ores, the
present co. is to be wound up. But as
George Blakemore and A. E. Savage think
there are very good chances in the sulphide
zone, those shareholders who care for the
gamble are being invited to throw into a
No Liability co. enough capital to push a
winze down a hundred feet or so.
Y Water, Emmaville (N.S.W.), last half-
year could not work up to the sampling esti-
mate of lib. tin oxide, so it finished up £852
to the bad. That didn’t daunt the office in
Melbourne. By taking £2050 from the old
equalisation fund and tacking on the carry-
forward of £350. it swung the account into
credit. Another wave of the pen and £2025
was_ swished on to the depreciation page,
leaving a small credit for the current term.
All this cross-entry doesn’t, however, remove
the bank blister of £14,828 or pay the £1507
due to sundry creditors. The reallv liquid
asset against the £16,000 qf debt is the stock
of oxide on hand valued at £B6BB. Fuel
and other items swell liquid and semi-liquid
assets to £15,580, so that if a forced sale
was inevitable, which it isn't* the co.’s
banker wouldn’t feel that he must fall
back on the plant to get his own back.
The trouble is that the rise in tin from £lB5
to £220 didn’t happen soon enough to coun-
terbalance the costs of handling stiff ground
going only fib. tin oxide. The hard truth is
also out that the tin is so tine that it
slithers away with the black sand. Moline
and Wilson have been considering how to
catch the stuff before it reaches the dump,
and they seem as radiant over settling the
point as weary holders will be when they
again see the glimmer of a div. ahead. Of
course, tin at £220 makes that outlook a lot
more rosy.
Bourke’s Hill Tin, Emmaville (N.S.W.),
reported a yield of 6 tons black tin in 28
days, and No. 1 plant on Preston’s block is
said to be capable of continuing this good
work. In March this plant recovered tin
worth £BSO at a cost of £450, but this only
refers to working' costs. The shifting of
No. 2 plant when labor is available will eat
a bit.. Like most of the New England tin
cos. Bourke’s Hill made leeway last half-
year, the accounts showing a loss of £sl.
The only dredging co. at Emmaville that
paid dividends was the Great Britain, which
is lightly weighted with a capital of only
£SOOO and has two plants at work. If the
war lias caused metallic tin to rise to £224
per ton, or 2s. per lb., it has also caused a
shortage of hardy New Englanders to man
the dredges and sluicing plants.
* * * *
New England (N.S.W.) tin dredging and
sluicing cos. found it difficult to make dirt
worth Is. per yard pay exes, last year. The
Tingha division .put up a good struggle, for
it produced 400 tons 10 cwt. of tin oxide
worth £55,228 from 929,420 cub. yds. High
costs, including the expense of shifting sluic-
ing plants from one locality to another,
caused a general slackness with regard to
profits and dividends, and such an important
co. as Cope’s Creek Central, which has three
plants, worked at a loss.
