In a short life Langi Logan South, next
door to the New Langi, Ararat (Vic.), has
swallowed calls £69,000, capital put to
credit at flotation £11,660, and other odd-
ments, the whole totalling over £BO,OOO.
What for? To win £7600 of gold which, of
course, had to be thrown back into the
gutter to keep the ' pumps jigging. George
Bryant, like all alluvial battlers, sticks to
his job of beating the water and doing a bit
of development work. Though shorter hours
and more pay have pacified the
worker, Bryant can’t get his mine manned.
So lie has to ladle out to his shareholders the
cold comfort that he is compelled to cut out
necessary work and stick to the spots where
a bit of gold can be grubbed out of the
gutter. This can’t go on for long, and
unless labor is more plentiful the tally of
calls will continue. With men available,
Bryant says he can earn profits, and as he
isn't a. boaster he has hard luck to be so
hung up.
* * * *
A week ago the Edna May crowd gloated
over the “official” story that there was to be
no reduction in the three-bob divs., that re-
serves were satisfactory, and that the output
could be maintained. Now they have joined
in the cry for economy because Mick Wil-
liams says that, not only has he lost several
days’ crushing through the break-down of
his pumps, but his grade has come down
because he has had to pull out more low-
grade dirt than usual. Also, when he had to
break through a dyke in the rise above 885 ft.
the water trouble at last showed itself. The
bulls are not feeling happy.
Leonora Gold Blocks (W.A.), having put
£26,500 to the credit of mine and plant
account and spent the cash, is now out to
make things hum. Asa start, the 10-head
mill is eating up 3000 tons of dump stuff.
A fortnight’s go at this went a shade better
than 4oz. to the ton, and the Melbourne
supporters crow that Tom Williams’s next
clean-up will be at least up to the estimated
50s. mark. Now that the stamps are falling
the big job ahead is to bustle on the 200 ft.
level east to join up with the level at the
bottom of the old incline-shaft stopes. Half
the way is gone, and as the blank between
the shoots has been passed, Williams will
soon know if he is to carry values for the
rest of the road. The 400 ft. level is
chasing at the heels of the 200 ft., and so it
can’t be long before reserves show up on a
decent scale, provided the gold remains in the
face. After that. Williams’s experience at
Marvel Loch ought to be a solid asset in
keeping down costs.
Melbourne has taken three more Westralian
options—the King of Creation near Laver-
ton. and two others at Kunanalling, in the
Coolgardie district. Returns running up to
nearly 4oz. are claimed from the latter two,
but neither has yet been tested at depth.
The locality has sent many a co. to an early
grave.
Many suggestions for the encouragement of
prospecting were discussed at the mining
conference just concluded at Kalgoorlie. One
was that £250,000 should be set aside annu-
ally ; another that 2$ p.c. of mining cos.’
profits should be compulsorily invested out-
side their present holdings. State aid for
prospectors similar to that in N. S. Wales
was advocated by another section of the con-
ference, while the liberalising of conditions
of taking up and holding mining areas was
claimed by others to be the solution. The
Minister promised to carry out some of the
suggestions made during the discussion and
to do his best to secure as big a grant as
possible to assist the industry. At a later
stage of the proceedings the Chamber of
Mines representative kicked against the pro-
posal to compel cos. to set aside portion of
their profits for prospecting. He pointed
out that, the directors of the principal mines
in the West had already spent hundreds of
thousands in search of new mines or in
developing their present holdings, and as
they were now being very heavily taxed it was
not the time to consider further impositions.
* * * *
“Oof Bird”: Market manipulation, for
which sharebrokers were mostly blamed by
Tom Duff and others, was feelingly dis-
cussed at the recent Ivalgoorlie mining con-
ference, and the Minister for Mines was
asked to make it illegal for any broker to
hold office as a director or legal manager of
any mining co. This, however, would not
overcome the difficulty, as the broker could
still have his dummy. The surest way to
purify the industry would be to increase
the status of the manager and have Boards
composed of men with mining knowledge
and some commercial experience. Bulling
or bearing is all right so long as it is
done honestly. But as a rule the director
who gets in first, and then gives his friends
the tip to operate, uses information to his
own advantage which he is paid by his co.
to use to its advantage. Once at a Board
meeting a telegram containing bad news
about a mine was handed in. Before the
meeting was over one of the directors became
doubled up with pain. He got up after a
bit and said he would go for some brandy.
But the chairman got to the door before him ;
turning the key in the lock he said, “No
you don’t! We will reach the Stock Ex-
change together.”
* * * *
The Bill to authorise the State purchase of
the Mount Molloy railway was re introduced
and finally passed by the Queensland Leg.
Council. This railway leaves the main line
42 miles from Cairns and runs to the once-
flourishing copper-mine at Rifle Creek, a dis-
tance of 20 miles. The price is £17.500, to
be paid in p.c. debentures with a currency
of 10 years, so it should not prove a bad
bargain for the State. Water in Rifle
Creek and Datches of basalt land will attract
the. cocky in time.

Somebody sends this paper a prospectus
(undated) of the Heazlewood River Alluvial
Gold and Tin Mining Cos., of Mt. Magnet
(Tas.). The co. is to be in 75 shares of
£lO each, of which the vendors and pro-
moters take 25. Whether the float has
obtained Bigjohn’s sanction isn’t disclosed;
but he surely won’t stop it. Instead, he
might rush the other 50 shares and Win the
War that way. It ought to be easy:—
Samples of wash assayed at Mount Bischoff grave
27.5 metallic tin and 60oz. gold per ton. Twelve
prospecting shafts showed wash carrying gold to
?Sft., not bottomed. Fair average value may be
struck at 20s. per yard practically over 160 acres.
The only other suggestion The Bulletin
has to make that the Mint might be
shifted alongside the property so as to be
handy.
A recent issue of ’Frisco Chronicle
devotes half a page -to the career of W. J.
Loring, formerly Australian mining-manager,
and nine years partner in B. M. and Cos.
Boring’s first job, at the age of 12, was as
tool nipper on a small Californian mine at
50 cents a day ; at 25 he was a mine super-
intendent. In 1902 l.e went to Westralia to
take charge of the Sons of Gwalia at £IBOO
a year, and four years later he became B. M.
and Co.’s general manager of 36 gold, silver,
lead and zinc mines in Australia. This
carried a salary < f £9OOO, and he held on to
it until 1908, when he bought “Hail Colum-
bia” Hoover’s one-third interest in the firm
and shifted his quarters to London. Loring
is now hustling for the firm in his native
California, and has re-opened and is operat-
ing with “great success” a group of mines
in which, as a mere child, he got his introduc-
tion to his profession.
Every engineer should be a subscriber to the
General Electric Review. It is a monthly maga-
zine containing descriptions of the latest develop-
ments and practical applications of electricity in
every sphere. No library is complete without it,
as the contents are original, instructive and com-
plete in every detail, being contributed by the
leading engineers of the world. The subscription is
only ten shillings (10s.) per year, so subscribe now,
or write to the Australian General Electric Co.,
Wentworth Avenue, Sydney, for a free sample
copy.— Advt.

sally used where the economy in steam-raising is
sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-
tion and excellent facilities for cleaning, the heavy
lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with. — Advt.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 30, 1917.
My Dear Arini, —
The Stricklands’ departure was a tear-
drenched ceremony. The little girls sobbed
alongside their delicate mother on the liner’s
deck, while the invalid sadly bowed to the
farewells of friendly faces' on the wharf.
Dady Edeline was carried on board at 8
o clock in the morning. At noon the plucky
lady smiled through her tears, while the
emotional feminines in the crowd below
enjoyed a good cry. It was the vision of all
tlicit threatens the helpless mother and her
pretty nurselings that melted the soft-
hearted Australian. When Mrs. George
Black and Mrs. Joe Cook, forgetting politics,
wept audibly i’n company, even Cabinet Mini-
sters put on an undertakery expression. But
their grief is not insupportable. Till the
popular kiddies puckered up their faces
the N. S. Wales Government was making a
decorous effort not to look too triumphant.
Our ex-Governor made a solemn farewell
to Sydney clad in the cocked hat and knee-
breeches in which he first entered th*e city.
The faithful Aide d’Apice went to Adelaide
with the party. It was remembered by the
crowd on the wharf that the younger Strick-
lands were little Australians leaving the only
homeland they know. Even eldest daughter
Mary was a mite of nine when she last saw
the gloomy old castle in Westmoreland. The
latest plan provides for the whole family dis-
embarking midway or thereabouts, and
spending a few months in a house in—-quite
a safe place.
Chief Justice Cullen took the oath of sub-
regal office at noon on Monday in Govern-
ment House. lie draws his legal pay and
half the State Governor’s screw besides
while Sir Gerald is getting through his six
months’ official leave. After that, there’ll
be the whole salary to go to the Lieut.-
Governor or whoever reigns at Macquarie-
street. There is some chance of Miss Hen-
derson. sister of the Commodore, staying
with the Cullens for a while. If so, there’ll
be a dominant note in the gentle hostess’s
vicinity
General Finn, Private Sec., and A.D.C.
Clifford go on with the Deputy Cocked Hat
at Government House.
As Lady Edeline Strickland wasn’t at>le
to go to a demonstration of farewell from the
National Council dames they went down
to Government House and ‘ handed their
parting words of goodwill done up in a
repousse leather case, with the wattle
blazoned on. its face.' The Tom Hughes lady.
Mrs. MacCallum, Miss Bose Scott and others
of that ilk supported the saddlery. The
Nat. Council, by the way, is considering
what can be done to make its thunder take
shape in legislation.
Anzac Day was all a-glitter with golden
sunshine, so there were no umbrellas in the
crowds that stamped the city to see the
khaki procession. The best bird’s-eye view
•>vas at the afternoon affair in the Show
ground. Excellency Helen in mourning garb
—a tribute to the dead of Gallipoli—was in
the official stand, where every shade in poli-
tics scrambled for a seat. The dais near
the edge of the show ring was ablaze with
color, like a tulip bed. Here were Archbishop
Wright in his best millinery-—gorgeous
touches of red and purple—Scotchbyterian
Prof. Mclntyre in his cap and gown, and
parson Carruthers, who leads the Wesleyans
in prayer at Conference—all decked in
ecclesiastic finery. Itabbi Cohen, under the
brim of a black hat like a soup plate, was
the cleric raven in this parterre of male
splendor. State Commandant George Lee
rode round the long khaki line early in the
event. Billjim can do it with some splendor
himself, for the flutter of silken banners
made rainbow glint of green and purple with
regal red and blue over the artillery lines.
Edmund Barton, a shadow of his former
portly person, had a seat in the official stand.
Like all free shows the Anzac Night con-
cert was rushed by about 3000 more people
than the Town Hall would hold. The com-
mittee, in handing out about 2500 tickets —
r/ratis —before the event, thought all would
be well. In the crush that made George-
street a live antbed at 7.30 p.m. many of
the ticketholders couldn’t even get inside the
municipal railing. A reliable witness, who
surged through the portal in the first rush,
claims that it was a matter of muscle, not
cardboard, and white-haired mothers of men
We tossed about like straws in a torrent,
h inally the doors were locked, and the con-
,lAsloa was s^lu t in. Recruiting speeches ate
46 minutes out of the heart of the concert.
Ireneh flags fluttered round the Union
•Jack at Rose Hall on Sunday morning.
About 40 men in tlie pale blue-grey that held
V erdun are staying at the convalescent home
in Darlmghurst. When the boat is ready
they return to New Caledonia to finish their
sick leave. Then back to the uproar in
h ranee. G.-G. Ferguson and his lady
motored in to have a word with the visitors.
Matron Fox was in charge, and Manly
\ .A.D. s (their day for washing up the
crockery) lined up while the Red Cross
clneftainess had a word with them. The
G.-G. spoke words of cheer to the French
in their own tongue, with the slow, careful
utterance (occasionally searching for a
word) in which a cautious Scot attacks a
loreign language. Mrs. Jim Ashton and
other Red Crossers were present at this
Sunday-at-Home.
Crossed over to Beyond last week, after
two years’ illness, Mrs. R. J. Sheridan, of
Aorth Sydney, sister of the late J. P. Garvan,
who founded one of our big insurance con-
cerns, and widow of a well-known figure in
rifle and cricket circles of years ago. Five
children survive, two daughters and three
sons; John, of the Equity Bar; Bob,
attached to the Supreme Court; and Denis,
of the Harbor Trust but now in khaki.
The Michael Meagher duo, of Bathurst
(he owns a Papal decoration, and so does
his father, John), departed this week to
have a look at the North, commonly called
the East, and sip the souchong and sakh of
our Allies up that way.
There’ll be a cosy cheque for the 2nd
D.A.C. and 22nd Howitzer Brigade Com-
forts Fund from an all-star show held in St.
James’s Hall on April 27. “The • Star
Spangled Banner” (Lilian Frost at the
piano) was followed by T. J. McMahon’s
vivacious account of Central Papua, where
domestic squabbles_ are occasionally settled
by an enraged native eating his motlier-in-
hiw, and Life in the Northern Territory.
Contralto Kate Rooney in velvet tones sang
“Softly Awakes.” Encores and a stack of
flowers. Nellie Leach, formerly a pupil of
the late Marchesi, warbled in tl'ie love scene
from “Bohhme” with tenor Martinelli,
Pianist Winifred Burston helped "violinist
Lionel Lawson make a brilliant attack on
Sarasate’s “Gipsy Dances,” Albert Goos-
sens (Miss Puech at the piano) stirred the
Pulses in the Toreador Song, and Rhoda
Kettlewell and pi’etty Dymphana Fox
danced till the curtain fell.
The big audience—“a lovely house to sing
to,” said Kate Rooney—included various pil-
grims from far-off Wahroouga. Dr. and Mrs.
Purser, the Ernest Blackwells, and 11. C.
Macfie and his dainty missus came from the
leafy heights. Mrs. Trefle brought daughter
Kathleen; sporting-man Tom Watson was
dragged from his easy chair by an enthusi-
astic wife and niece; E. Gard Trouton
backed up F. T. S. O’Donnell’s efforts to
get a waggon-load of blooms to the artists,
lion, concert manager, John Brownlow.
Hero is J. J. Cohen, now Speaker of the
State Assembly, and outgoing hon. sec. of
Hospital Saturday. The big man may be a
liaid nut to crack, but there’s soft kernel
inside. For many years he has done great
service as honorary organiser of the system
which rattles a collection-box in your face in
the first week in May—formerly on the
Saturday ; now on Friday.
On May 4 Hospital Friday girls will be
like red Indians on the trail of your utter-
most copper. And here is the brand-new
hon. secretary, A. L. Blythe, who has organ-
ised the 1917 campaign. The slim, alert ex-
Maorilander is a strenuous, whole-souled
patriot, who takes his seat in the Ameliora-
tion office at nine o’clock every morning,
and puts on his hat about 5 p.m. By way of
a short holiday from this honorary job he
started slogging at another—this hospital col-
lection fund. From May Moore's photo,
of Mr. Blythe you won’t judge wrongly
that he is all amiability until you
tread on his kilt. Then you’ll get proof,
perhaps, that he comes from fighting Camp-
bells and haughty Douglases, who gave a
clout when they might have turned the other
cheekbone. Baptised in the Scotchbyterian
faith, confirmed as a schoolboy at the famous
old Tunbridge School (Eng.) by the Arch-
bishop of Canterbury, the victim suffers no
ill effects from thus mixing his theology.
Tunbridge, by the way, is one of the wealthy
old King Edward VI. Foundation Schools,
with an endowment that has accumulated to
£30,000 a year. It spends a good deal of
this affluence supporting commercial schools,
and giving exhibitions to prize Oxford and
Cambridge scholars. As lion, adjudicator for
the Amelioration Committee, A. L. Blythe
now deals personally with the grievances or
otherwise of between 200 and 300 men a day.
If Mr. Blythe could only get a whisky or
even a beer monopoly! The earliest Hospi-
tal Fund organisers in Sydney managed it
and made £45,000 out of an equal number of
gallons of rum. While the resultant build-
ing was going up in Macquarie-street Gover-
nor Macquarie gave a ball in one of the
wards to celebrate a Royal birthday. In
those far-back times—a hundred years ago it
was—patients cooked their own food in the
wards, one of the alleged kitchens being used
as a morgue.
To surgeon James Bowman, w T ho took
charge of the chaos in 1819, belongs the
credit of putting Sydney’s hospitals in the
way to be the splendid institutions they are
to-day. lie had to fight the “heads” of the
old mixture of gaol and camp, but he cleaned
up the . place and disciplined the outlaw
“nurses.” One hopes the Principal Surgeon’s
benign ghost may occasionally waft its way
through the fine modern building which
stands on the site of the old Rum Hospital.
Sydney’s great surgeon, Alex. MacCor-
miek goes to England shortly. Ilis wife is
on the other side, and he is anxious to per-
sonally conduct her safely home to this blest
spot—as safely as the murderous sea-ser-
pents may allow.
The War Chest fete in the grounds of
Tregoyd (the Cullens’ nest at Mosman)
attracted a thousand or more visitors. Money
talks to money. Every other rich woman
in Sydney .had some part or lot in the show,
and there -is a big cheque to follow the sales.
Lady Helen Ferguson, under millinery with
a green ostrich featho” twined round the
crown, opened the affair. Then Haskett
Smith, A.D.C., took off his gloves to get
into busines as lion, porter of her Ex.’s pur-
chases. ■ One cute idea near the entrance
gate was a query stall. Anybody who had
lost his bearings or hungered for information
as to where he could spend most money in
an easy way, had only to worry the damsel
who had a ground plan spread out before
her. .Miss Eadith Walker left her palace up
the river to sell bargains. Mrs. Ralston,
Mrs. O. Friend and the Old Gold and Silver
folk were amongst the saleswomen. The
produce stall was so deeply embedded in
the crowd that wanted to help the cause and
carry home eggs and bacon to their larder
that a despairing “Can’t get near it” went
up from the thrifty. The tea-tent was in a
leafy dell. A queue of a hundred or two
thirsty women wailed hopelessly outside,
then fled for the remote tram.
Handsome Billy Brennan, a Light Horse-
man, was killed on April 20, while fighting the
Unspeakable Turk, led by the more Unspeak-
able Ilun, somewhere in the desert. The tall
Cornstalk was on a station up Moree way
when he heard the bugles call. That was IS
months or more ago, and .he has been busy
ever since. There’s a sheaf of sympathy for
solicitor W. F. Brennan and his wife, who
have another six-foot son in training at Salis-
bury.
Singers at patriotic shows have their own
sorrows, though they don’t obtrude them.
Nine-tenths of the population is writing
verse set to music, which is bursting through
with goodwill to Billjim Anzac. One noted
singer, who has been taking an alleged rest
in her native land, has been pursued with
applications to voice “the only patriotic song
of real merit.” There are thousands of sam-
ples, and some of the writers go in person to
lay their ode at the feet of the songbird.
“Rolls of music,” she sighs, “come my way
by post or personally delivered. My -home is
invaded by minstrels with MS. tucked under
one arm. These good people do not realise
that if their music were worth while (usually
it isn’t) artists in other countries make part
of their income by introducing new songs.
They don’t do it to oblige the composer. It’s
a financial proposition. Clara Butt gets 20
guineas and a royalty on the sale of any
song she introduces.”
Graythwaite is on the look-out for a small
parcel of groceries from every visitor to its
fete on May 5. You can take a necklet of
loaf-sugar to swell the gift.
What a melodious veteran is the silver-
locked Randal Woodhouse ! These elder men
—remember the technique of Bispham?—
learnt long ago how to sing their words. The
latter art is frankly despised by the bawd-
ing modern male—unless he has been trained
in opera. Woodhouse was one of the artists
at the Anzac concert who sent his song to the
end of the Town Hall.
Concerning chooks, Miss Rouse and Miss
Sands, who specialise in those feathered
swells, Rhode Island Reds, are giving nearly
the whole of the proceeds of their farm to
the War Chest. They reserve only the cost
of the feed. The 30tll Battalion Comforts
Fund gets a windfall out of the same bene-
faction.
There is another sad, fond mother at
Bellevue Hill, Mrs. J. P. Garvan having
learned last week that her youngest boy.
Dick (E.W.), had been killed in France,
where he was a lieut. in the R.F.A. Dick, a
Grammar School boy like his brilliant father
before him, had been two years at Sydney
Uni. when the greater duty called. He left
only friends behind him, for he was one of
those sunny athletes that the Australian
heart so warms to. He played polo when he
could ; and with the boxing gloves he held
the inter-State Uni. championship in his
class. In the field he seems to have shown
the same qualities, and almost the last thing
the family heard of him was that he had
been mentioned in dispatches. A brother,
Gerald, is in the field, and two brothers-in-
law.
Sydney ’Varsity women had a meeting on
Anzac night in the Great Hall, which is
handsome but tomb-like by artificial light.
The recently-returned Mrs. Harper—she is
the wife of the St. Andrew’s College boss—
told the company what she'd seen in the way
of munition-making in England.
The Soldiers’ Club is beginning to hear
the distant howl of the wolf, for foodstuff is
going up, and it feeds the Kangaroo at a
nominal price. Dr. Mary Booth, lion, secre-
tary, will welcome your subscription, be it
fat or lean.
Measles puts the Infants’ Home at Asli-
field into a mild state of quarantine. Hence
the annual meeting in the Women’s Club
rooms the other afternoon. Excellency Helen
took the chair.
Tenor Philip Wilson’s little Eva (she
used to be Eva Champion) has just turned
the corner after a serious operation.
Do you remember sweet singer Eileen
Boyd—now a war-widow in England? She
is trilling for a crust in the London halls,
and getting as much butter as the Ration
Chief can spare. Here are a few lines she"
sends
I meant to have returned to Australia on a visit
this year; but the war prevents. Yet how I long
to see my homeland again! Here we have had a
terrible winter —freezing cold. Sydney pianist
Budden Morris is doing pretty well, and Rosina
Buckman is also in the swim. Fred Leslie and Ivy
Shilling and Lucille Bonstead are likewise busy. But
things in general are pretty bad for artists, as out
of London half the places of amusement are closed
down.
Yours affectionately,
VANDOKIAN.

that is equal to all the difficulties of the most tremendous musical
compositions ever written for the piano, it gives you also simple,
accurate guidance that enables you to employ your new-found
power with a subtle artistry that few amateur musicians ever
develop.
Even though you have never played any instrument, the
Metrostyle, a device exclusive with the Pianola, is a clear and easily-
followed path to perfect interpretation. It is the master’s prompt-
ings and teachings simplified so you may understand without
tedious study.
The beautiful tone shading—the power of varied emphasis
which gives the Pianola touch such a startling semblance to that of
human fingers, is due to another of the exclusive Pianola devices —
the Themodist.
Many other important features and improvements have given
the Pianola a superiority over every other instrument of the player
type, that is recognised and endorsed by practicallv every great
musician of the present generation.
Yet the Stroud is not an expensive instrument.

of the different modes suitable to each individual
case. Only the best hair is used, and the workman-
ship is all that can be desired for fineness and finish.
Although doing all classes and modes of Postiche we
specially excel in our “Natural Parting,’’ so perfect is
this that it defies detection. Special attention is directed
to our Hair-dying and Suiting Branch, where
the methods adopted cannot be surpassed.
No matter how discolored or grey the hair
may be, after treatment the result is always

effects. The haircanbe washed in the usual way ;
the treatment is non-injurious, it acts as a

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, April 30, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Some of the convalescents who arrived on
hospital ships just before Anzac Day were
British Tommies and English officers from
Aden who had been sent voyaging to Aus-
tralia for a tonic. They were included in the
groups of returned Australians who marched
in the big procession. Some Naice girls who
had brought flowers from their gardens and
handfuls of cigarettes and cigars from Dad’s
smoking-room, were well forward in the crowd
at a corner. But they were dismayed on
finding that the Englishmen were almost
foreign, you know. “They couldn’t under-
stand half we said, and kept on saying ‘Beg
pawhdon—sorry.’ We had to repeat every-
thing !”
Mrs. Lowther Clarke and the St. Paul’s
Tent committee had a spread for returned
Billjims on Anzac Day. It was a lunch party
and followed the sad and chilly service in the
Cathedral next door, where Sassiety knelt on
cold stones and felt for a brief hour the
seriousness of the times we live in. The State
Governor carefully put his high silk hat
under the seat of the front pew and Lady
Stanley looked devout in her best church
bonnet. Victor Hood and Conant seemed
like pillars of the church, and Major Stewart,
military secretary to the G.-G., was in line.
Archbishop Clarke preached in the dim
morning light and an Anzac bugler sounded
the Haunting Last Post.
There was a special demonstration in the
Town Hall for the Anzac Anniversary. The
returned Anzacs roosted high on the organ-
loft seats and when the audience thundered
they seemed emabarrassed and looked round
for cover. It was an occasion that demanded
a display of the Australian flag, but the
only one I saw was mixed up inconspicu-
ously with half a dozen others. Below the
heroes of the day, where at mere social recep-
tions there are banks and banks of flowers,
there were cheerless bare spaces. The usual
scheme of Town Hall decoration places the
Union Jack in the centre of the south bal-
cony. The sudden < itry of the U.iLA. into
the war evidently upset the decorator, and
the proud banner of England was hurried
into a corner to make room for the thought-
ful old Stars and Stripes. But the flag I
wanted to see was Australia’s.
Both the big ecclesiastical chiefs sent
apologies, so Gaud Mayor Hennessy intoned
the speech-in-chief in a collar as much like a
parson’s choker as possible. He drew a
terribly long face and read from notes
(looked at • under his spectacles) as if he
were delivering a sermon. Various speakers
orated about scenes in Gallipoli which they
had never seen, and the Anzacs above sat still
ard looked thoughtful.
The afternoon tour of wounded and
/mimed men on decorated carts was painful
and unwise. These unlucky warriors stopped
before places like the Stock Exchange to
make speeches and" take up collections. The
passers-by crowded and gaped as street
crowds will, until it seemed less like a
patriotic appeal than a waxworks show.
There was no dignity about it. The care of
the disabled Anzacs is not a matter for
casual charity; it is a national obligation to
be reverently discharged.
A great weaving machine mounted on a
lorry paraded the streets on Anzac Button
Friday. Disabled soldier-weavers worked it
while mates appealed to able-bodied citizens
to go and work the guns.
Melbourne University had a carnival on
Saturday which brought in good money for
the University Red Cross and Patriotic
Fund, and gave the students a chance to let
off steam. In -the morning students dressed
as girls did the Block in Collins-street,
and bailed up astonished citizens with
bunches of tickets. The people who bought
them had the privilege of listening to Chan-
cellor Madden’s opening speech in Wilson
Hall at three o’clock. This time the Chan-
cellor appeared as his dapper little self and
held his shiny silk hat as a shield or buckler
while he prattled good advice.
Wilson Hall was arranged like a circus,
with tan on the floor and gaudy bazaar
stalls. The cedar seraphs that spread their
wings across the ceiling looked quite sniffy
about it.
Some man suddenly exclaimed, “Oh, look
at the duck !” and made fer the produce stall.
The Sweet student girl in attendance blushed
furiously, but the enthusiastic stranger was
not referring to her. One ’Varsity sport
had been out shooting plump wildfowl, and
another had sent in an enticing basket of
fresh trout. Girls, with carrots and pump-
kins stencilled on their caps, sold vegetables
and the Frank Madden girls dressed as
gypsies and sold sweets. A fortune-teller
sat in the middle of the pond on a raft and
heard her clients one by one. The Meds.
had a lolly stall, and their assistants were
robed in black paper stencilled with white
skeletons. Some students roasted chestnuts
and others carted ice-cream. The biologists
sold fruit, flowers and vegetables, and had
the Stone Age history of a germ done in
sugar fondant.
Three girls—until Sutherland, Miss Farr
and Miss W ilson—did lightning pencil-por-
traits for a fee of one shilling per patient.
High tea kept the visitors on hand until the
evening shows and illuminations, and later
there was an open-air supper near the pond
under the electric bulbs.
The rich A. B. Kents, of Narre Warren,
have given their most precious possessions
to this war. Last week news came through
of the death of another son killed in action,
the third. One of the brave boys carried a
D.S.O. into the shadows.
Dr. Mary de Garis, of Melbourne, lately
went off from London to Salonika to help
mend our army, or any other army that
happens to be about. She is with the
Scottish Women’s Unit. Her pater and
mater have been wintering in Guernsey, but
braved the Kaiser’s U’s to cross over and
wave a farewell hanky at her.
Squatter James Bussell’s widow has suf-
fered heavily through the war. Two of Iter
four soldier sons have died on service. The
second boy succumbed to meningitis when
in camp here; and now Captain Jack has
fallen beside his guns. Elder son Philip,
owner of the family sheep acres, is a British
officer; the fourth is with the Anzacs.
Lieut. Kennedy Smith, M.L.A. for Dun-
das, who left long ago for service abroad
and was reported last year as being on hos-
pital duty in the British blackblocks, has
been sighted by a Melbourne cobber in the
war zone. He was described as looking-very
old and frazzled, and as though his share of
the war-burden has been full and plenty ; but
he was still going strong in the right direc-
tion.
The Bulletin used to make disrespectful
remarks on the “flame-colored whiskers” of
Samuel Wilson, K.C.M.G., who tried long
ago to acclimatise salmon in Victorian
waters. Those whiskers were emblems of
the pluck which snobbish old “Sir Salmon”
bequeathed to his offspring. There were
four Wilson boys. One of them fell in the
Boer War and another was badly wounded.
The other two —both British Army captains
—have been killed in the present war. The
youngest fell in France last month.
The Norton Grimwades’ flying son, Lieu-
tenant Fred, who fell in the Hun lines, has
turned up in a Switzerland internment camp
looking thin and ill ; but the worst of the
anxiety is over for his parents.
Adelaide’s observations :—-
Gallipoli Day is now an annual affair at Unley,
and on Saturday gorgeous weather backed up the
committee’s efforts to rake in money for Red Cross
and Trench Comforts funds. A procession, sports
and “continental” were items of the programme,
also an “empty saddle” appeal for recruits. On
the same day St. Peter’s and Prince Alfred Colleges
held their annual sports (for the twentieth time)
at the Oval. The Governor and Lady Galway and
many old boys back from France or Egypt were
spectators. The Cup for the winning team went to
Prince Alfred by a comfortable margin of 19 points,
and it was sent home in charge of the captain by
Lady Galway.
Surgeon-Capt. N. C. Shierlaw died from wounds
on April 11. He enlisted when war broke out, and
he finishes with a record of excellent work. It is
only a few weeks ago that news' came through that
he had the Military Cross.
This has been a wildly patriotic week —Anzac
Day, Win-the-War Day and the usual batch of patri-
otic fStes. One of the fetes, with the Artillery
Comforts Fund as its object, was at Herbert Phil-
lips’s house at Walkerville, where the garden is - all
that could be wanted for such an affair. Mrs. F.
W. Woodcock, whose major-husband is at the Front
with the artillery, shouldered a good deal of the
responsibility as secretary.
Luck in the way of weather • was with the soldiers
on Anzac Day. The Cheer-ups lunched about 800 of
them in the Hut, and 1500 or so on the flat below
(the Hut lunchers were returned men). At night
the C.-U.’s arranged a reunion for men who actually
landed on the Peninsula on April 25 two years ago.
At the lunch the Commandant gave some graphic
word pictures of his memories of Anzac and its
heroes. Various appeals, including those of the
“empty saddle,” brought in more recruits, and a
final effort for funds was made by a concert at the
Exhibition in the evening, with members of the
Gonsalez Opera Co. as the star attractions.
On Friday Lady Galway headed the women’s Win-
the-War procession and made one of her usual apt
speeches at its conclusion. Canvassing for Win-the-
War League members was keen, but where we are
to wear our badges if any more such leagues are
started, only the very portly know.
Kindly John Robertson, who died at Elderslie at
the end of the week, will be badly missed in the
south-east. The eldest son of the late Robertson of
Struan (also John), one of the south-east’s earliest
settlers, he was born at Struan in 55 afid became
manager of Elderslie in ’79, three years after his
father purchased it, and he lived there till he died.
Laurie, the lively young stepdaughter of
Alick Pearson, of Berwick, has promised to
marry Phil, son of machinery merchant
Edward Bevan.
William Pearson, once of Kilmany Park,
Gippsland, and now of London, is marrying
oft his handsome daughters quickly. Mimi
was wedded a few months ago to a Lieut.
Jackson, who travels with a title in his kit-
bag. Now the tall willowy Helen is
engaged to Captain Campbell Russell, a
submarine officer whose father looks in at
the Bank of Australasia in London. Heiress
Helen broke down in health after too much
lied Crossing here and has been, Jiaving a
holiday abroad with her mother.
Jeremy Peyton-Jones, who is related to
the racing Dick Caseys, of South Yarra,
lately married Margaret Grosvenor, a daugh-
ter of the Hon. Mrs. Norman Grosvenor, of
London. The Peyton-Jones family lived in
Melbourne before the war, and the newly-
weds met in Paris, where ..both were, doing
hospital work with the Anglo-French Red
Cross service. Mrs. Jeremy is a charming
girl, and has good looks after the style of
Lily Elsie.
Philip Russell, the squattocrat who is
owned by the swift four-legged Harriet
Graham, has just been elected to the V.R.C.
committee, in succession to R. G. Casey,
resigned and* abroad.
Bananalander Mrs. I’arkes gave a party
at the Oriental to welcome home Mrs. Cass,
wife of Colonel Cass. She has been in Eng-
land. The air was full of talk and tea and
buttered toast, and the Bowes Kelly dame
and a clutch of her daughters and the Fred
Knights, the McKinnons, De Jersey Gruts
and Mrs. Ledingham (just back from Maori-
land) said the right things to the lady. Mrs.
Sellheim led a military team.
Thanks to the Syme fete, the Foundlings’
Home is richer by f5OO-odd—all garnered
during one afternoon. Lieut. Miles O’Hara
—on a fortnight’s furlough from England—
heaped up quite a lot of coin with a merry-
go-round which he worked. Mrs. Geoffrey
Syme herself made nearly £lOO with her
dressed Australian dolls. Australian-made
baskets were sold, and, once you had a
basket, you wanted something to put in it.
Mrs. Mackay and Mrs. Thompson, helped by
Tackie Fenner, netted £lOO, and Sam Mackay
threw in his £lOO cheque as a help to his
wife’s stall. Foundling babies were on show
—but not for sale. Mrs. O’Hara was
organiser-in-chief and helped at a stall in the
tented bazaar street where the assistants
raged furiously together like the nations in
the oratorio.
George Armstrong was a tired, sad-looking
passenger on the Sydney express last week.
His young wife is still very ill, but he is
anxious to meet his mother who is due to
arrive early this month. There was some
talk of Melba grand-opera in America, but
the war silenced it.
The Glaciarium opened for the winter on
Saturday afternoon, and the snow-shovel
boys began their cheerful clatter once again
in the big building. There was a fine roll-up,
and dapper little manager Watson smiled
till the ice began to melt. There are glowing
radiators for the comfort of non-skaters, and
the cafS has broken out into a new decora-
tive scheme of warm pink cherry-blossom
suggestive of spring. The gilded college boys
came up in great numbers and splashed on
two tickets each, naming their particular
college girl for the second ticket and booking
up a locker for her.
The Hennessy dame arrived wrapped in furs
and surrounded by daughters. Mrs. Winter-
Irving came along with her daughter, and
the Mcßeans, Armytages, Goodissons and
Johnstons rolled along for the first slither.
Quiet old Rutherglen got very excited last
week when Hughes approached. The girls
were out with flowers, the streets were
flagged, the hall where he was to speak was
packed half an hour before schedule time,
and the mayor and all his tribe waited pom-
pously at the front gate of the village. When
news came through that the orator had been
delayed everybody settled down to a long
wait. The mayor and his party were still
afield when a very small man was quietly
ushered on to the hall platform by a police-
man. He sat there for fully quarter of an
hour before somebody realised who it was,
and went out to cooee for the mayor.
Hughes had entered by the back gate.
Dick Ramsden, recently found dead in a
London park, with a vein opened in his arm,
was a well-known Bananaland squatter in the
old days and a frequent Cup visitor to Mel-
bourne during the Brasseys’ term, when Mrs.
Freeman Thomas was at the zenith of her
popularity, and George Chirnside and his
sporting help-meet were running the Arthur
Blackwoods’ old house. On those festive
occasions “Dick” Ramsden, Gordon Lyon
and other sports kept the ball rolling. Later
on, he married, and settled down in an Eng-
lish hunting district.
■ Melba is almost here, and her students are
feverish with excitement. They have bought
new white dresses to wear on her arrival, and
every spare moment is spent in practising
scales. The white dresses are in direct
response to Melba’s request or perhaps order.
She believes that the soul of the woman
artist is affected by her clothes and that she
whoops like an angel in white. The Fitz-
clarence lady and accompanist Ruby Gray
are with her, and Coombe Cottage is being
scrubbed day and night.
Pianist St. Leger returned to Melbourne
with a big batch of sick men. lie personally
battled with pneumonia, and is having what
he calls "a home trip” to buck him up.
The young man is really an Englishman;
hut he has adopted this sunny country. His
shipmates were a batch of French convales-
cents. Melbourne has got quite accustomed
to the uniforms of delicate blue-grey and
the caps with cerise pipings. The motors
that bore them away were heaped with pale-
yellow wattle, and the effective color scheme
looked almost deliberate.
They’re all doing it. Ada Crossley lately
made her first appearance in a vaudeville
setting at the Grand Theatre, Birmingham,
and has booked up for a long engagement.
She is to appear in London later on.
Russian violinist Max Selinslcy gave two
recitals only in Melbourne this season.
Tuesday and Thursday were the evenings on
which the serious young artist performed.
The highly-decorative Jessie Masson was his
accomplice at the piano. She wore her blue-
black hair correctly coiffured, and inhabited
a dressmaker’s dream which was glued to
one bare shoulder by a buttonette of roses.
The shoulder-sleeves were like miniature
ballet skirts of tulle, and the rest of the dress
was silk-swathed and bound and tethered
by more buttonettes of roses. Jessie played
with all the superb self-satisfaction of the
expensively-dressed. She took the piano-
part of Schumann’s Sonata in D Minor and
shared the applause with the star. Flowers
were handed up by the cartload, and an em-
barrassed Selinsky gingerly took them in
hands already too much occupied with his
precious violin and bow. He looked
furiously at the usher, at his own
hands and at the loads of flowers. Then
he thought better of dropping the vege-
tables which had been thrust upon him and
carried them away as if he were putting out
the cat. Dr. Hamilton Russell was con-
spicuous at the second Concert; he was in
khaki, wearing the marks of the returned.
The music-loving medico has evidently had a
severe grilling. His once silver hair is now
snow-white, and his complexion has gone a
fine brick-red.
The Italians are coming back, after all,
to give two concerts on Monday and Tues-
day, May 14 and 15, and the women wor-
shippers of the three singing gods are pre-
paring flowers and food.
- Nellie Ivolle makes a beautifully unreal
swaggie in the Australian pantomime at
the Princess’s. She wears “moles” made of
brown velvet, I think, a dark shirt, an old
felt hat, a handkerchief round her neck, and
carries a stage swag on her shoulders. She
comes, singing robustly, from Kosciusko, and
tells the house all about it in a strong,
twangful contralto. The voice is quite mas-
culine, and reminds people of Rodda Perry,
the boy contralto who was “brought out”
by Newbury.
The orchestra at the Princess’s uses a lot
of hairpins and nose powder. A mild young
man, with a pale face, has the baton and
piano, a trombonist blows a gale, and a pale
brother beats three drums ; but all the other
musicians are women. One girl with a
Paderewski mop of fair hair plays a harpsi-
chord or harmonium. Another fetching
young person shows a deep V in the back
of her dress and saws at the fiddle. A.jnost'
comfortable lady settles plumply ifito a deep
chair and puffs her cheeks over an oboe.
The really saucy one is the little girl who
tootles on a cornet, while her hair hangs; in
a tricky Psyche-knot. A tall, languorous
lady droops over the big bass viol.
R. S-. Smythe celebrated his S4th birthday
at Balwyn a couple of weeks back. The
old showman is still fairly hearty, and there
is no noticeable impairment of his faculty
for uttering witty and graceful ambiguities.
He is not so active as he was, however, and
nowadays regards a journey to Melbourne
as foreign travel.
Graham Browne, matinee idol with Marie
Tempest, is likely to start a new craze among
our naicest young men-—the ones who still
pay strict attention to male fashions. Browne
parts his hair carefully from his brow right
over to where his collar buttons at the back
of his neck. A beautiful woman tried this
hairdressing trick in bygone days. Ruby
Steele Wallace, of the almost-forgotten
divorce case, -parted her thick fair hair for a
while from a fine ivory scalp-line at the
back ; but, then, Ruby had heaps of heavy
plaits to pile on her classic brow.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
“Chink”: The observations of who
tries to boost Gregory Mathews as a fleld-birdologist
on the strength of the fact that he and Gregory
wagged it in the bush together 20 years ago, do
not move me. I’ve met the same Mathews in recent
times, and reckoned him a good fellow personally
and largely a well-meaning fellow ornithologically.
Instead of studying Australia’s birds in rational
fashion his chief aim seemed to be to murder the
little beauties by the hundred in order to split
feathers in the matter of technical names. The
confusion resulting from this foolishness troubles me
a heap less than the loss of so many good Aus-
tralians f.or no practical benefit. Such “work” is
of no use to the country, and I don’t believe that
any man who had really cobbered up with the bush-
birds at any time would countenance their sacrifice
for such a purpose.
“8.D.”: Two kinds of algaroba were
introduced into Queensland over 40 years ago.
In his “Cultural Industries of Queensland,” pub-
lished in 1883, L. A. Bernays says that he had
handled and tasted the fruit of one of them, grown
at Bowen Park. These trees were grown from seed
which the British Colonial Office, on the advice of
Sir Joseph Hooker, got from America and distributed
for planting in the drier regions of South Africa and
Australia. Bernays produces some evidence which hints
that it might be well to go slow on the algaroba
business. The U.S. Department of Agriculture quoted
an instance of a fine horse killed by eating about a
pound of the pods. This was in Jamaica, and the
Department admitted that the seeds seemed to be
safely used in their native habitat—which is not
Australia. Baron Von Mueller’s theory was that
the pods were suitable as food only for animals that
chew the cud, and so get rid of. distending gases. The
seeds, by the way, arc particularly liable to be
attacked by insects; Bernays states that not more
than a fifth of those sent to Queensland from KeW
were good.
* * '* *
“MeNoi'tli”: I dunno whether Gregory
Mathews is a “cabinet naturalist” or not (vide
“Chink” and “L.P.” in recent Bullletins), but 1
do know some of the men he employed to chase
specimens and collect data for his new catalogue of
Australian birds. 11 obin Kemp, a collector with
many years of experience in West Africa, Maoriland
and elsewhere, was one of them. Cape York (N.Q.)
being of great ornithological importance on account
of the frequency of migratory birds thereabouts,
Mathews sent Kemp there a.s his most trusted col-
lector. Kemp stayed with me at my ranch at Cape
York for some months, and 1 had numerous oppor-
tunities of observing the painstaking care and atten-
tion to detail that characterised his work. Time and
labor were of no importance when Kemp was out to
verify anything. He was up to. all the dodges of the
trade, such as wearing clothes that harmonised with
the' adjacent scenery when observing particularly shy .
birds. The camp Binghis often clicked their teeth
in wonder when Robin came out of the scrub on to
the beach dressed in a green shirt, blue pants and a
hat garlanded with leaves. It will be mainly as a
result of Kemp’s endeavors that Mathews’s catalogue
will contain the fullest and most authoritative infor-
mation about North Australia’s birds o’ passage.
“H.Y.E.” : A recent aboriginality says the
breeding time of the black swan is December.
Writer, however, has found the nest along South
Coast (N.S.W.) lakes, with seven eggs, early in
June and, again, early in July. The black and other
wild ducks are also early breeders. I have found
well-fledged “flapper” black ducks in November on
chilly Monaro. Baked swan’s eggs were, by the way,
a favorite food of the aborigines, while roasted
cygnets came a good second. The impart,icular wild
man didn’t in the least mind the fishy flavor of the
eggs or the rankness of swan meat. Nothing came
amiss to his hardy palate—particularly the white-
man’s firewater, and in early South Coast days the
average sample would kill at 40 rods. A long-
forgotten VI.1). ran one of the pioneer shanties in
addition to his practice. He was one of the victims.

“T.McL.”: Isn’t it time to think about

“E.S.S.” : If everybody’s, finished speaking
about vocal night-birds, I wish to’remark that I was
referring' only to singing birds. I like the cheery
chirp of Willie Wagtail, but I can’t admit him as a
songster. The magpie carols in the greying dawn,
but is strictly diurnal; if it raises a voice at night
it is only when it has been disturbed. There is no
singer among the cuckoos, though the call of the
Australian cuckoo is one of the sweetest bird-notes 1
know. The mountain-thrush sings softly and melodi-
ously after sundown. It sings to the daylight’s close,
as the magpie sings to the passing of night. Nothing
has been mentioned that can be added to the three 1
named—the reed-warbler, the bush lark and the
blue-wren.
“Spreader” : Apart from the cost of keep,
which is next to nothing, an analysis of the habits
and. qualities of the milch goat discloses facts with
which every family should become familiar. Two
prime factors are (1) the animals are practically
immune to all diseases that affect man, if we except
Malta fever, which so far hasn’t appeared in this
country, and (2) their milk is the most nourishing
food for infants and invalids known to science. It
contains on an average 84 per cent, of cream-fat as
against 4 per cent, in cow's milk. The latter also
runs second as a producer of curd and bone-forming
material. The flesh of the wether goat has more
nutrient qualities than that of the sheep; and once
the taste is acquired it becomes a very popular item
on the bill of fare. A nan shut off in a small
portion of the backyard will turn the kitchen peel-
ing-!. crusts, etc., into the richest and purest milk.
Through the absence of fresh blood the goats in
Australia have deteriorated to such an extent that
they can .be relied on to give only a very small
quantity of milk, but the quality remains unim-
paired. The recent importation of Swiss goats by
the N. S. Wales Government with the hope of increas-
ing the flow of juice by cross-breeding was a
commendable act. The success of *tlie experiment
rests on what means are taken to educate the public
to the many advantages to be gained by keeping a
nan. The risk from infection of tuberculosis through
milk fades away when nanny supplies the fluid, and
i his in itself should be enough to set the Public
Health departments thinking. The Swiss-Australian
cross shows signs of great promise, and the Taronga
Park (Sydney) Zoo authorities are moving in the
matter of importing a Nubian buck from India, as
the latter cross with the English goat is considered
better than the Swiss. The demonstration farm at
Nyngan has a limited supply of Australian nannies
stinted to pure Swiss billies for sale free on rail at
the modest Sinn of ss. each, and the supply can be
increased to meet a big demand.

THE ADMIRAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The recent deciphering of inscriptions on certain
ancient monuments gives interesting details about an
ancient navigator presumed to be Noah. Disclosures
of great interest are promised.”
When Noah was the Admiral and launched the
cruiser Ark,
The dolphins and the porpoises said “Iley !
Come, all you long leviathans and join the jolly
lark—-
The Old ’Un’s got his clipper under weigh.”
When Noah was the Admiral and things were looking
glum
He’d sing to all his devils blue “Belay!”
He’d pipe his crew around him and serve out a tot
o’ rum
And, when he’d got ’em livened up and gay,
There’s one would dance a hornpipe and there’s one
would start a jig,
And one would lift a chantey o’er the-sea,
And Noah, he would murmur: “She’s a craft that’s
tight and trig,
And just the sort to suit the likes o’ me.”
And when the sinners’ corpses floated peaceful by his
ship
He’d sermonise his little familee:
“In case you’ve not repented yet, just take a sailor’s
tip,
And never do your sailing on the sea!”
And once when Ham was stirring up rebellion in
the crew
He took that black disturber by the ear,
lie triced him to the mainmast and he gave him
forty-two
And branded him “a blanky mutineer.”
Ilis instincts they were healthy and wdien Mrs.
Japheth went
And loaded up her cabin bunk with twins
Said he: “The little kiddies for our blessing they
were lent;
The women—they were sent us for our sins!”
He was on the strict teetotal every third or second
day
And only drank to drive away the blues,
And, as he said, his only aim in putting grog away
Was to keep his jolly sailors off the booze.
He took a tender fancy to a curly crocodile
Who also was a messmate on the main;
And when the rains were over, he’d tranship him to
the Nile
And let him start a useful race again.
At last the floods had blotted out the wickedness of
man,
Except the chosen samples on the bark;
He muttered, sotter vochey, “What a blessing was
the plan
To save some little Vices in the Ark!”
He settled in a cottage on the slopes of Ararat,
But paid a frequent visit to the sea,
And spent his time a-fishing in an old an’ battered
hat
And pants that bellied badly at the knee;
And once, when fellow-fishers, in their quaint,
romantic wise,
Did stretch it till they stirred his sluggish blood,
He cheeked them with “Avast there with your damned
infernal lies!
Remember, I was fishing in the Flood!”
In wrath they rose and smote him in the midriff in
his prime
And sent him where departed sailors go,
And carved upon his monument in rude, insulting
rhyme:
“He went wdien he was piped for from below!”
Envoi.
M hen Noah was the Admiral the world was all at
sea,
And Man was gone, his laughter and his tears ;
But Missus Shorn and Missus llam had hopes of You
and Me
A down the shining vistas of the Years!

OLDING’S COACHWORRS, 47 Parramatta Road, SYDNEY
“Cave-man”: “Salex” (B. 29/3/T7) men-
tions a cave on the Hawkesbury (N.S.W.) upon the
walls of which are handprints ranging in size from
a baby’s up to a man’s. Half-way down “The Gulf,”
on the eastern fail of the N. S. Wales Dividing
Range, between Rylstone and Bylong, similar prints
may be seen. These are mostly the size of a
woman’s hand, perfect in s'hape and extending as
far as the wrist. Among the prints is one of a
child’s foot. In color they are reddish grey. Local
tradition has it that the oldest inhabitant tried to
chisel certain portions out, but that the imprint
remained the same through the sandstone as on the
surface.

“Wart”: Close to my verandah is a pepper-
mint tree in which a pair of white eyes and a pair of
fantail flycatchers have their nests; also a pear-tree
in which a brace of goldfinches cuddle; and a blue-
gum in which a pair of black-and-white magpies are
rearing a family. All of these occasionally entertain
me of a night with a tune, but none so often or so
melodiously as the magpies. The best singer in the
Tasmanian bush is the shrike-thrush, known locally
as “Whistling Dick.” His dress is a dove-grey,
shading to olive green on the back; a perfect protec-
tion when viewed against the background of blue-
gum. He can do a liquid gurgle with any nightingale.
Also, lie remarks that he’s “Pretty, pretty Dick,
pretty Dick,” getting his local name from this call.
He is one of the most easily-tamed of bush birds, and
will call regularly for a meal of crumbs, and walk
right in to protest if the dinner happens to be late.
He goes round the verandah hunting in the corners
for stray insects and into the passage to search
behind the pictures and curtains. The missus seems to
lie much more shy. Dick probably destroys as many
grubs and insects as any bird his size, and most
bush dwellers would as soon shoot a robin.
“Arthur S.”: “8.H.” is certainly wrong
when he says that eight horses are equal to one
good elephant for hauling. About 12 months ago,
when horses and motor tractors were scarce in
England, a firm in Sheffield employed an elephant
for dragging loads of iron and steel. She was a
youngster of 25, only halt-grown, but she could get
away with a load of eight or nine tons with ease.
This is the work of a four-horse team, and she
moved twice as quickly as horses could have done,
so that her value was doubled. A full-grown Jumbo
would do much more than that. Feed is a problem.
My friend could eat almost a sack of potatoes at a
meal.
“Hay”: Re aboriginal sign language and
the use of Masonic symbols. W. P. Auld was one
of Stuart’s party when the South-to-North trip was
made through Centralia, and on his return I heard
him relate many of their experiences. After leaving
Attack Creek (so called on account of an encounter
with the blacks) the party had no further trouble
till it was near the Indian Ocean, when a number of
abos. became aggressive and threw spears and
boomerangs. Stuart, not wishing to fire on them if
he could help it, waved green bushes in the fashion
of a signal, and to his surprise one of the blacks
returned some Masonic signs, which the explorer
answered, and there were no further hostile actions.
The Centralian abo., in the early days, when he
wished to express deep disgust or contempt, just
looked unutterable' things and spat. “No” was signi-
fied by jerking the head or hand in a downward
direction; “yes,” by an upward movement. I never
knew him to point to any object or in any direction
with his hand. He always used a well-projected pair
of lips. Strange, to say, when very angry his first
impulse was to hurl the epitaph “red” or “gory” at
his enemy. The great Australian adjective is no
misnomer, for Binghi called his brother “bychoo
bychoo” long ago.
“Gumleaf” : I’m puzzled to know why the
mosquitoes do not trouble the blacks. In the
Walgett district (N.S.W.) this year the pest has
been particularly ravenous, and it was simply im-
possible to do without a net. In some cases chained
dogs were killed and fowls were tortured to death,
while horses and cattle were forced to keep moving
about all night to free themselves. Yet Binghi was
able to lie down anywhere and remain unmolested.
“Wart”: “Leo Lear” (B. 15/3/T7) asks
if any Abo. has seen a spine-tail swift settle, and, if
so, when and where. I saw five land on the trunk of
a peppermint-gum at the mouth of the Pipes- River
(Tas.). They seemed to have taken shelter, like our-
selves, from a rain-shower. They did not perch as
other birds, but clung closely to the bark, and when
leaving they seemed to throw themselves off and
went like a shot out of a gun. Swifts in Tasmania
have a bad name among beekeepers. One or two
will fly about in front of a hive and catch every
bee that comes out. For this reason they are very
often shot and, dissected, the stomachs are found full
of half-digested honey manufacturers. About Launces-
. ton they seem to arrive always at the end of the
summer and are not usually seen for more than a
couple of days. I have never been able to find out
where they disappear to, whether to the north or to
the south. Like “Leo Lear” I have noticed that
tlic swifts are only visible immediately before bad
weather. Is it because in fine weather they fly so
high as to be invisible?
“V.” : Burial customs varied a good deal
among different tribes of NT. S. Wales aboriginals.
For one of the last ox the Monaro full-blooded blacks
a hole was dug about eight feet deep by three feet
wide, and into this the body, enclosed in sheets, of
bark, whicli contained also the dead man’s blaiikets
and tomahawk, was stood upright. The earth was
then thrown in and stamped down, and a sharp stake
was driven into the soil immediately over the, head
of the corpse. In some instances the body was
simply jambed into a nook in a cave, in others it
was placed on top of a network of vines and left
to rot; but on the coast burial most frequently took
place in the sandy soil about salt lakes and estuaries.
A little time : ago I found an aboriginal skull in
sandy soil at the point of debouchure of a creek 'into
a salt lake on -the South Coast. Further exploration
unearthed some finger-bones. The skull was -in
good preservation, but one front tooth (knocked out
to mark arrival at manhood) was missing from the
upper jaw, and the skull was fractured near the
right ear, its former owner having most probably
been killed in one of the frequent tribal fights.
“Jim Carraway” : Nature is a revengeful
old lad\ r who doesn’t like being disturbed. In the
Victorian irrigation areas stinlcwort now promises
to keep the settlers lying awake. Cutting the pest
only makes' it thrive; it has to be rooted out,
bagged and burnt before its funeral service can be
read. The trouble is traced back to the loads of
cracked stone brought from Bendigo to line the
irrigation channels and prevent erosion. But the
erosion would have been less of a calamity to the
irrigators than the introduction of the stinkwort pest.

“Melos” : Simon, the Dago, with a dis-
colored eye and a badly-damaged bugle, sat on the
doorstep of the wrecked stek and oyst shop. “You
look as though you have been at the war,” I re-
marked. He shook his head sadly and answered:
“I no likea speeka da war. Lars night da big
Australia Billjim shap come inna da shop an’ he
say, ‘Hullo Daig! Who winna da war?’ I dink he
say, ‘Who beginna da war?’ I say, ‘German, of
course.’ Den he start —poncha me on da noce; he
belta da oyst at the wind’ an’ breaka da sbair and
ruba da butt’ on da wall, an’ breaka da crock. But
he’s not doo bad sliap. He pay da doc’ an’ da plates
an’ da wind’ an’ all da dings he break, an’ say he’s
verra sorra; it was a leedle meestake.”

“Te Babarantiko”: I would like to pour a
little cold water on these glowing descriptions of
what can be done cheaply in the food.line in Papua
and Northern Australia. I’ll lay long odds that
“McNorth,” while writing his enchanting bill of
SILVER LINING.
“Well, there’s one thing about this place,
mate — there’s no flies.”
fare, had one eye speculatively on the tins of roast
beef and stewed tripe, trying to decide which to kill
for dinner; and I suspect that Beatrice Grimshaw’s
pretty little picture was drawn after the usual excel-
lent dinner of Southern Australian products at the
Samarai Cosmopolitan Hotel. I have just returned
from extended wanderings in another group where
“everything- man requires can be grown,” but never
is grown, because the whites there, like the whites
at Papua, prefer to buy the potatoes, onions, fruit
and vegetables that steamers bring from Sydney,
instead of the insipid slush the tropics produce. One
of my later experiences (and I have had many) is
that, where game used to be plentiful, the noise and
presence of humanity while clearing and planting
drives the wild things into the inaccessible jungle,
where the white man cannot follow even if he has
no objection to himself serving as provender for the
local man-eaters. Persistent dynamiting scares
the fish to other grounds. I have had to go eight
miles to get a bagful of fish—not nearly enough for
a large labor force. Birds, too, become shy and are
difficult to get. Crocodiles gratefully collected our
tame ducks and the tame pigs. A fat-headed Head
Office had supplied the pigs, but would not allow
the time and labor to build croa-proof stockades.
Where wild pigs, wallabies and cassowaries had been
plentiful a few months before, I have had a native
hunter out all day and far into the night, and he
has returned with all the cartridges and without
seeing signs of game. But there were sweet potatoes
enough—hundreds of acres of them! Also all the
ills that follow a liberal sweet-potato diet.
“D.F.” : The now extinct Tasmanian Black
Brother didn’t leave many relics behind, but a
waddy dug up recently at Lagoon Bay, on Forestier
Peninsula, shows that he could do a good job some-
times. It’s a very solid little weapon, carved
out of a beautiful bit of she-oak, 21-1 inches
long, inches round the business end and tapering
very gradually and smoothly down to four inches
at the handle. It must be about 100 years since it
was made, but it’s as good as new and as sound as
a bell. The architect obviously picked out a young
tree of about the right size to carve his masterpiece
out of, but even so it must have been a big under-
taking with his tools.
* * » *
“M.G.” : In regard to the sheep-fly, what
about employing the great vulture of India for
cleaning up the. carrion which is the pest’s breeding-
place ? The vulture eats carrion only; he is not
like a small bird, hard to locate and shoot, if he
should become predatory; he is of considerable size
and eats gluttonously. I refer to the large vultures,
of course. The smaller varieties are not individually
big enough scavengers to be worth considering. It
is certain that fly-breeding is also done in manu-
factured goods, and blankets, flannel, etc., have to
be watched or they will be blown. I myself have
seen the full-grown maggots fall out of woollens,
and old Riverina housekeepers, as a result of experi-
ence, pepper blankets before putting them away, to
secure them from the curse. Flannel the same.
Shopkeepers in some places keep such goods wrapped
in calico. I remember hawkers doing likewise. Once
at Silverton, about 28 years ago, the summer being
fearfully hot, the flies died on window-sills, and the
larvae must liave hatched in their dead bodies. The
maggots grew to full size. I judge that the strong
fed on the weak. My landlady was most careful
about tucking calico sheets all over beds, and saw
to it that no blanket touched the floor.
THE FARM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tlie misty rains have passed ;
The sun is dazzling bright;
Where’er the eye is cast
Trees swim in clearest light,
As though in water glassed.
The farm looks washed and clean,
White stubble and red loam ;
Shy birds the furrows glean ;
Cows slowly straggle home,
Cropping at shoots of green.
Small clouds like flocks of sheep
Lie massed in highest air;
Below the live flocks keep
The apple-orchard bare,
Or in the thick shade sleep.
And playful shadows dance
Upon the furthest hill.
But what unearthly trance
Keeps all the house so 5ti11?....
A boy lies dead in France.

“Half-moon”: I noticed in a recent
Bulletin that a Victorian firm imported
sherry fungus from Spain and turned it
loose in the casks to give the grape-juice
the genuine flavor. Sets me thinking of the
concern of the Federal authorities that we
should have “pure spirit distilled by potstill
or similar process at a strength not exceed-
ing 45 overproof and matured in wood for
a period of two years.” Here’s how they
observe the conditions in a big rum factory
that I know of. The molasses fermentation,
which needs an experienced man, is looked
after by youths and is hurried by means of
(ires underneath the fermenting backs. The
distilling of the feints (result of first dis-
tillation) is done by very amateur distillers
under the supervision of Excise officers,
who very often know little or nothing about
stills or spirit. The spirit comes over at
strengths varying from 25 o.p. to 45 o.p.
and often pretty rank tack, too, calculated
to send a man to Hell at express speed. They
get a huge vat nearly full of such stuff, leave
it there for two years and call that storage
in wood. Experts reckon that the beneficial
effect on the spirit is practically nil. Storage
in casks would draw the teeth of the stagger
juice to some extent.
“N.Z.”: Here in Melbourne the milkman
suspected of infusing Yan-Yean into his ex-
tract is pursued by a special type of sleuth
maintained by the State; but the man re-
sponsible for the country’s butter can mix
as much water with the grease as he pleases,
and there is apparently no one to say him
nay. If there is anyone to say him nay, it
is said in a tone of voice that may not be
heard above the. din of the city, and the
small remark is lost in the rush of business.
One-third of the average pound of butter
delivered at some suburban back doors is
water, introduced by a handy little appliance
with which buttermen are well acquainted.
The popular idea that water and grease
won’t mix is at once a comfort and a deli-
cious jest to the humorous tradesman, who
blends them daily and deftly to his own
great advantage.
“Musketoon”: Stop the machinery for a
minute and listen to this pastoral. A flock
of store ewes were sold last month to a
Nygigan .(N.S.W.) buyer for 325. Gd. per
head. Without being lifted, they were re-
sold within a week to a farmer in Grenfell
district for 355. Gd. A fortnight later they
were passed on for 385., and the last recorded
sale was for 41s. per head, and still going*
strong, although they haven’t been shifted
from the place where they were first sold.
Within the next year or so everybody in the
district will have owned those sheep for a
few minutes and made a profit out of them.
Perhaps Joe Cook remembers a party
. which used to throw scorn at “Fisher’s
Flimsies,” for no better reason, of course,
than that the Labor Government introduced
the Commonwealth note circulation. But how
completely that circulation is now doing its
work and pouring scorn on Joe Cook and his
party was shown by the N.S.W. Railway
deposit after the Easter holidays. The pay-
in was £135,175, and only 30s. of it was gold.
“Trallwm”: Here’s another patriot win-
ning the war and establishing new industries.
He blew into our town the other day with an
“All-British” business card, intended to - be
hung on the wall of all patriotic homes to
show who were the town’s true Britishers
from whom the real patriot should buy his
Japanese underwear, his American boots
and his Norwegian fish. It cost ss. per
inch to be an All-Briton, and dozens of-
easily-scared shopkeepers and widowed
“ham-and-beefs” forked out their scanty
dollars lest they imperil their poor little
businesses through, being classed as only
part-Britons or even whole Germans. Yet
no parson raised his ready wail about this.
** * *
“Bathurst”: Here’s another for Webster’s
Post Office. Over a week ago I posted an
order, along with the cash, to a Sydney fruit
agency, and a couple of days later followed
it up with a. telegram. A*speedy reply came
back that the wire had got there 0.K., but
that the letter had not been sighted. Nosing
round, I found that the letter was still snug
inside the mail-box on our railway platform.
There were so many others there that a
visiting-card might have been edged in with
a paper-knife. The box is still choked at
the time of writing.
It has at last been frankly admitted that
the catch of fish off the Victorian coast is
nowadays being limited by a fishermen’s com-
bine. “The fisherman at Queenscliff,” for
instance, according to the Argus, “has found
that when the barraeouta are plentiful and
the full catch is sent to market, the total
monetary return is less than when the sup-
ply is moderate.” So the fishermen’s associ-
ation, instead of taking out 30 baskets when
the fish are running freely, now knocks oil :
at 20. Papers like the Argus call this sort
of thing private enterprise, and assure the -
public that it and the Bible are the secrets 1
of Britain’s greatness. 1
“O’Pliimerty” : Very little is known now |
of Collins, first Governor of Tasmania and
founder of Hobart, but one letter is worth
noting. His stores were provided by contrac-
tors in England, and he wrote complaining
that “the axes will not cut, the gimlets will
not bore, the gum-trees set the wretched
saws at defiance, the clothing is shoddy and
the thread is rotten.” Further, while a
number of guns of all sorts had been sup-
plied, the ammunition was all for one
calibre; also the boots sent him were made
on the assumption that all feet were of the
same dimension. Collins concluded by ex-
pressing the wish that the contractors might
be sent out to him under sentence. He says
it would give him “unspeakable pleasure to
set them to work with the wretched tools
they have provided.” There was some excuse
for Collins depending on imports, but he’d
turn in his grave if he knew -that even yet
Australia hasn’t learned to make its own
gimlet, and that much of it would still
rather get its suit-lengths from abroad than
buy them at home.
* * * *
Up country in S. Aus., especially on the
Peninsula, the mice plague is bad, too. A
store at Point Lome was lately attacked in
the night and the invaders devoured most
of the. stock, including boots, the wood of
lead-pencils and the labels on tinned pro-
visions. The banquet cost £3OO. That was
bad enough ; but when next night the boss
was awakened by the devils gnawing at his
fingernails preparatory to picking his bones,
lie reckoned it was time to push on.
A Victorian mallee-hen lately had two £lO
notes chewed up by mice; but he managed to
piece enough of the fragments together to
show the numbers, and the Federal Treasury
will issue two fresh tenners. It he hadn’t
been able to turn the trick, some bank man-
ager might have been driven grey by a
desperate customer trying to pay a handful
of dead mice into his current account.
All the Medes and Persians seem to be
working on the wharves now. According
to the waterside workers’ award, seven to
eight is the breakfast hour; but when a ship
in a hurry to get away had been swallowing
cargo all night at overtime rates and only
seven slings remained to be hoisted in, it
was reckoned (this being war-time) that the
workers might reasonably finish the job be-
fore adjourning. It was put that way to a
Melbourne gang lately; but. though they
were to get time and a half for the 20-
minute job, plus- the extra shilling for work-
ing during a meal hour, they turned it down.
With the river pilot aboard and the tug
alongside, the ship had to wait until eight
struck: then a fresh gang tackled the frag-
ment of a job and had to be paid 3s. Gu. a
man for it, as no lumper can be engaged for
less than two hours.
“Petifi”: To get to wind’ard of the Hun
submarine Uncle Sam proposes to build- a
fleet of wooden cargo-carriers. Each vessel
—cost about £6o,Q6Q—will be 2000 tons,
oil-engined, armed and manned. by a crew
of 35. Sam calculates -that during the first
year’s carpentering he can turn out three
such arks daily. Vessels of this make would
have these advantages : they would be small,
mastless and smokeless, and consequently of
much less visibility than the ordinary type
of large steel ship. Also, they would be
more easily manoeuvred if attacked, and
their comparatively shallow draught would
give them a good chance of passing over a
torpedo that would get the big fellow. Mari-
time history suggests some other possible
advantages. The old “wooden-wall,” even
when riddled like a colander “ twixt wind
and water,” took its own time to go to D.
Jones, and gave its crew at least a sporting-
chance to get away, which the modern steel
liner mostly doesn’t. Years back a wooden
ship, with a cargo of lumber, was dismasted
and abandoned at sea. A warship, sent for
the purpose, found the waterlogged derelict
and bombarded her with live shell at short
range. Rut the almost submerged hulk
refused to sink. Finally the lumber was
heaved overboard and the hull broken up, a
bit at a time, so to speak.
* ■» * *
“Tom E. Ruff”: The shortage of. shipping
is responsible for some record wheat stacks
in South Australia. Very little of last sea-
son’s record harvest of 40,000,000 bushels
has been shipped and in addition a big pro-
portion of - the previous crop is still in the
hands of the agents of the local Harvest
Board. There are big stacks right through
the country, but the . main centres are
Port Adelaide (between'two and three mil-
lion bags). Wallaroo (about one and a
half million bags) and Port Pirie
(approaching the million mark). Port Lin-
coln. the main port for the new wheat grow-
ing area on Eyre Peninsula, also has its
trifle. Weevils' have not proved such a
curse as might be expected. The carpets of
mice and the scarcity of iron for roofing are
the chief troubles of those concerned.
“Si Hie”: Since the days of Sam Slick
the horse-dealer has been hard to beat for
originality and resource. Lately in search
of a likely nag I happened on an ad. which
ran like this: —
For sale. I have decided to part with my well-
known grey mare Mollv Bnwn to approved buyer.
(Mrs.l Maria Heep, Godiva-street.
Reciting to myself “The Arab’s Farewell to
His Steed.” I hastened out and found that
Molly had a variation of housemaid’s knee
and spavined hock, and her teeth had long
since lost their birth certificate; further-
more. Maria was the wife of a famous horse-
knacker who-didn’t mind trying to vary the
business a little.
FRESH MEAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tim hot hoofs bruise the powdered dust,
The slayer's eyes are keen and hard,
A royal life excites his lust,
The kindliest beast in all the yard.
The sharp lash flays it to the crush,
Its heaving body strains the bars,
Tense nostrils sniff the scented bush,
The wind-whipped rnulga and belars.
And all Creation’s love of life
Flashes from those fear-maddened eyes
That eon at once the whetted knife,.
The blinding glory of the skies.
And all Creation’s ruthless power
Lies heavy on the slayer's hand :
The careless force that leaves its dower
Where bleaching bones make fat the land.
A flash of steel, a muffled thud!
The stunned beast crashes to the sward,
While through the spurting jets of blood
The quick knife seeks the spinal cord.
All smeared with dust the glossy coat,
While life goes shuddering to its close,
And from a gash beneath the throat
The red blood flows, and flows, and flows.
The very posts with blood are dyed,
Coined with red the sheath-knives ply,
lied hands peel oft' the smoking hide
And swing the steaming carcase high.
Ilot darkness hems the station round,
The stockmen’s huts are all alight,
Come smells of cooking, and the sound:
“It's killing-day: fresh meat to-night.”

Two Years i n the Antarctic
with “His Master’s Voice”
Marooned on the cheerless ice !
Shut in by howling blizzards —nothing to do but sit around, save at rare
intervals! But they had a “Gramophone”—and Gramophone records —
and so enjoyed music and vivid memories of the life they left behind them
SIB ERNEST SHACKLETON writes:—
i WELLINGTON, N.Z., Btli March, 1917.
Dear Sir, —
In answer to yours, the Gramophone was of the greatest use and
pleasure to everyone during the long winter months spent in the
Antarctic, cut off, as we were, from all social amenities, with only the
squawk of the penguin and the roar of the blizzard for diversity of
sound.
I think that no Expedition should go to the Antarctic without “His
Master’s Voice’’ Gramophone, for it certainly bridges the miles of
stormy ocean that lie between the Antarctic and civilisation.
Believe me, dear Sirs, yours truly,
(Sgd.) E. H. SHACKLETON.
>A awn U
“GRAMOPHONE’
Registered Trade Mark
No matter where you go “His Master’s Voice” would take
good cheer and good music to break the dull hours of
monotony.
OBTAINABLE EVERYWHERE.
Instrument catalogue, record lists and name of nearest
accredited dealer on request.
The Gramophone Co, Ltd.
