POLITICAL
POINTS
The anti-conscriptiouists having shown
their teeth, Hughes last week “pledged him-
self, the Government and the party not to
enforce conscription, either by statute or by
regulation, during the lifetime of the forth-
coming Parliament.” In other words: “We
must Win this War. If we have any prin-
ciples, we must be prepared to swallow them
to Win this War. We must even leave off
sending soldiers to the Front if that be
necessary in order to win this elec —I mean
this War.”
The leader of the I’hewsionists last week
pledged himself, his Government and his
party not to introduce conscription either
by regulation or by statute during the life
of the forthcoming Parliament. There is
therefore not one shred of reason why Con-
scriptionists, as Conscriptionists, should
vote for the Phewsion in preference to the
Labor party. One is not more definitely
pledged against Conscription than the other.
It remains then for the people to decide
between them on other grounds. The Phew-
sion, as W. G. Spence admits, is not
a party at all —it is two parties, hopelessly
divided against one another on almost all
points. It includes men like Josiah Thomas,
as uncompromising a Socialist as there is in
Australian politics, and other men like Bruce
Smith, Willie Kelly, a Fairbairn and a
Manifold. That isn’t a party : it is a mere
rabble without a policy. The Laborites are
at least a party with a policy. They pro-
mise Protection, for instance. The Phew-
sion doesn’t. How could it —how could
W. G. Spence and Manifold, Josiah Thomas
and Willie Kelly, De Largie and Bruce
Smith agree upon any policy?
WINNING THE WAR.
What a travesty on democracy our sys-
tem of government is'! In N. S. Wales the
other day there was an election of legisla-
tors, and the people chose the men they
would have to make laws for them for the
next three years. They declined to accept
the services of certain men; rightly or
wrongly they said these were not the sort
they wanted—they wanted Brown to make
laws for them, not Jones. Immediately that
this election by the people was over, eight
or 10 men, constituting the Cabinet, decided
to put Jones into the Council, where he could
veto practically all Brown’s proposals. And
more than that—whereas, no matter what
the people thought of Jones, they couldn’t
make him a legislator for more than three
years, this non-elective Cabinet put Brown
where he can, if he desires, block the pro-
posed legislation of the people’s representa-
tives for the rest of his life. By getting
elected he could be a legislator for three
years at most. Through not getting elected
lie is made a legislator maybe for 33 years.
The Proportional Representation League
(Vic.) is once more pushing itself under the
notice of the electors, now that the open
season for politicians is here again. The
league favors proportional representation
for both Houses. For the Senate this would
of course mean that the intermediate elector,
who can’t stand either the red-flag bigots
of one section or the dishonest schemers and
syndicates of the other, would get a
hearing in Parliament if he went the right
way about his business; and that would be
a good starf in the right direction, though
a complete system of proportional represen-
tation for the Reps, as well would look more
like the real democratic thing. The results
of the existing arrangements for preferential
voting in Victoria have thus far done little
for the in-between elector. For instance,
the only candidates at the recent Benambra
by-election were four vari-colored “Liberals.”
THE FILING SQUAD.
W. M. HUGHES (to Australian Sentiment):
“And now , sir, I have read you the
warrant; all that remains is for you to
say the word."
[MICK PAUL, 214 George Street, Sydney.]
The shifty Mr. Hughes foresees the possi-
bility of a great reverse to our land armies,
and if that happened he would have another
conscription referendum. Evidently Hughes
thinks that the promise of another referen-
dum in this far-off Commonwealth would
urge a broken British Army on to victory.
Such a practical statesman is Mr. Hughes!
[Edaiund Fisher, 369 Collins Street, Melbourne.]
The S. M. Herald has been shaking
her umbrella at Arthur Rae, N. S.
Wales Labor candidate for the Senate,
because he admits frankly that as a
theory of government he believes in
Republicanism. For that reason alone the
old lady says that Rae is not worthy of a
vote. The dear, dull, blundering old soul
forgets that she isn’t ' now barracking for
her old party, but for Josiah Thomas, De
Largie, Henderson, Buzacott, Bamford,
Sleath, Archibald, Lamond; Spence and vari-
ous Socialists and extremists. Will she
please tell us, what their opinions are on this
matter, and whether they are unworthy of a
vote, too?
Having overhauled Canberra and dis-
covered a little of the appalling waste that
has gone on there, the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment might now turn Wilfred Blacket
on to the job of exposing the waste that is
still going on at Lithgow, where some men
are making small arms and others are seeing
that they don’t make too many. It shouldn’t
be difficult to discover what similar small
arms cost to make elsetvhere; and a com-
parison of that figure with what they cost
at Lithgow, with the most up-to-date
machinery, ought to make Australia gasp.
The Bulletin theory that a gale blew
the Maoriland Labor party inside out and
filled it with so much wind that 57 years
will be needed to bale it all away, looks
truer every week. The party has just been
running candidates for town councils on
“anti-conscription” tickets; and it is about
the weirdest lunacy this paper had heard of
til] Hughes started winning the war with
hunks of dictionary. Military service having
nothing to do with Bumble, these frantic
people were beaten at the polls, and deserved
to be. If Labor can produce Bumbles of
great capacity in the management of the
parish pump, good enough! If it cannot,
w r hat in the name of Whakamarina is the
good of producing a collection of windbags
to make a noise about a matter they have
nothing to do with while the citizens are left
to perish in the chasms on the public high-
ways ?
James Manifold, of Camperdown (Vic.),
who is mainly occupied just now in trying
to get back into his Corangamite seat in the
Reps., declared the other day that the intro-
duction of an effective protectionist tariff
was “impossible during the war.” Manifold’s
chief reason for this conclusion, already
stated in painfully discreet verbiage by other
members of his party, is this: —
A request has been received from the British Gov-
ernment —and a request at this time is more than a
demand—that the tariff should not be altered during
the war, as it would offend some of the Allies.
When the British Government lately decided
to prohibit the importation of certain
articles, it went on with the job, merely
expressing polite regrets at having to ban
the products of France with the rest. Is
Australia less self-governing than England?
As a matter of fact, what “some of the
Allies” object to is that Australia should
give preference to any of the Allies. But
they never have objected to Australia offer-
ing an equal protective tariff against them
all; and it is because the Jingo Imperialists
insist on preference to one of them that this
country, in their hands, is likely to be in
very serious trouble presently.
A “pool” in which the public will take no
more than an academic interest was spoken
of at the Vic. State School Teachers’ Con-
ference. It was called
a “house pool,” the
sensible idea being to
“pool” the rents of
the mansions . the
State erects for its
teachers. Many of
them are in the back-
blocks and cost more,
as a rule, the further
back they are. The
Government, w i t li
charming impartiality,
charges an all-round
5 per cent. —-interest
and sinking-fund—on
the outlay as rent,
the result being that
some dominies pay
£2O a year for a large,
brick suburban house,
built in the days of
low prices, while
others must part up
£3O a year for a four-roomed kennel in the
Mallee.
In Victoria the Fusion decided that, for
the sake of appearances, it would be a good
thing for a party allegedly anxious to Win
the War to be able to point to a few khaki
candidates, so the > National Federation
selected three returned soldiers, Sergeants
Fozard, Tracey and Copeland —and patri-
otically set them to contest three absolutely
hopeless seats. The three are up against
Mathews (Melbourne Ports), Dr. “Billy”
Maloney (Melbourne) and Frank Tudor
(Yarra) respectively.
[B. J. H. Owen, 75 Glenhuntly Road, JSlwood, Vic.]
A scandalous story of “war profits” conies
from the softgoods trade. A firm was known
to have a large quantity of stuff which the
Defence Department would need, and every-
body wondered why nobody came along in
uniform to snap up the goods. At last some-
body in uniform did come along to inspect
the stuff; but he objected to the price, and
went away. Next day a mere civilian came
along and bought all those goods. Later on,
the Defence Department took them at just
double the price. Everybody in the rag-trade
knows the story. Every bagman’s dog barks
it. It is no use asking when the Defence
Department is going to inquire into such
things. It is the Defence Department which
does such things. Can you expect a fool to
honestly inquire into his own folly—or a
burglar into his own burglaries'?
Scaddan, M.L.A., late Labor Premier in
Westralia, has cut a deplorable figure ever
since the Conscription issue was raised.
Beginning with a fervent adulation of
Hughes when the little man spoke in Perth
on his return from England, Scaddan
wobbled to an expression of repugnance to
compulsion during his by-election campaign,
when the opinions of the electors of Brown
Hill-Ivanhoe were in doubt. Safely restored
to Parliament, unhappy Jack gradually
assumed an Imperialistic tinge. Then came
the Labor Congress. Brought right < up
against it, he made theatrical noises to the
effect thatjiis whole heart and life had been
devoted to the Cause. (“I never will desert
you, Mr. Micawber.”) He also took part
in the proceedings which resulted in the
firing out of all who declined to embrace
the faith of the Tudorites. But things began
to move upon the goldfields. A meeting in
Scaddan’s electorate, convened to pronounce
a sentence of political death upon the
Hughesites, was captured by the other
crowd. Then a great light burst upon
Scaddan, and he found that it would be
base desertion to leave those who had
remained faithful to Hughes. So another
metallic-looking substance lias been scratched
to reveal a lath with white-ants in it.
It wasn’t human to expect the hard-bor-
rowing Holman to remain in Australia while
the great British advance was on.
If there is one thing the Argus _ hates
more than a Labor politician it is too
honest a man. Lately it has been very
angry with Irvine, who persists in advocat-
ing conscription, when the Phewsion _ says
that it has “sunk” the conscription issue,
just as it has sunk the Tariff issue and
every other national question. The Argus
remarks
The “straight” man will always be admired and
trusted ; but, whether .we like it or not, there is
such a thing as expediency in politics.
That is to say, although patriotism may
demand conscription, which a few months
ago the Argus declared was “absolutely
essential to the preservation of our national
independence,” “expediency,” which ranks
above patriotism, demands that the anti-
Labor party should be returned to power,
even though in the process we have to
abandon the men .whom we alleged the other
day were dying in the trenches for lack of
reinforcements.
[B. J. H. Owen, 75 Glenhuntly Road, Elwood, Vic.]
A Melbourne printing firm claims to have
captured the trade in postcards for Aus-
tralia, including photographic views of
local scenery. The capture is easy enough
under present conditions; the trouble will
be to keep the trade after the war. Views
of Australian beauty-spots and city build-
ings used to be sent to Germany and repro-
duced by the million for this market. The
tariff gave us no protection, and the Phew-
sion declares that it cannot or will not
give us any. If it isn’t Germany that ex-
ports cheap postcards to Australia in the
uncertain future it may be any other country
for all that Cook and Hughes will guarantee
to the contrary. So the picture postcard
printers are presumably going to nail their
hopes to the Labor mast.
For electioneering purposes Hughes per-
sists that the Labor party is supported by
the 1.W.W., although, of coul-se, he knows
that a fundamental principle of the I.W.W.
is that its members shall not support any
political party. One of their rules is that
members shall not even allow their names
to be placed on the electoral rolls, and many
of them have been prosecuted in conse-
quence. Furthermore, the I.W,W. is most
bitter in its assaults upon the Labor party,
the Socialists and others claiming to repre-
sent the workers. It argues that its best
friends are the Tories, as their actions are
most calculated to bring about the revolu-
tion for which the I.W.W. longs. So if
members of the I.W.W. could be induced to
go to the poll at all their votes would be
cast for Hughes and his principals.
[B. J. IT. Owen, 75 Glenhuntly Road, Elwood, Vic.]
If it were a matter of taste the flavor of
the Phewsion would be gamey enough to win
15 wars.
THE VELVET PAW.
THE BORE: “By jove, you know, you must
think me frightfully rude for not keep-
ing my appointment yesterday .”
THE BORED: “Oh, weren't you there either?"
The “National” party lias plastered Mel-
bourne with posters: “The Labor party has
blown out its brains.” It means Hughes.
We know, of course, that the Liberals don’t
like Hughes, but still they needn’t have
announced beforehand that he was blown
out.
Phewsion could hardly expect that an
Iceberg would temper the wind to a born
sham.
THE SACRIFICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Vote for Cook and Hughes and. Win the War. —-
Election cry.
To waste one’s chances is a deadly crime,
And yet how many lives pass unfulfilled!
Young Brown at twenty had scarce reached his prime,
A keen, wild-hearted boy of sturdy build,
But he in far Gallipoli was killed.
Alas! His death can give no cause for pride;.
He might have voted at this crucial time
And saved his country —if he hadn't died.
Binks stayed at home and helped his country’s trade
By shipping silken goods from far Japan,
And curious things, by strange brown workmen made
In lands on which no strike had laid its ban ;
He did his bit as only merchants can,
And when his party leaders called for more
With bleeding heart his final debt he paid:
He gave his vote to Phews —and won the wait.
EMERALD, VIC.

This page has received a sheaf of letters
in explanation of accompanying formulae relative to
the calculation of totalisator dividends, from which
it transpires that there are various elaborate ways
of doing simple arithmetic. Why people should want
to wrestle with these complicated methods of going
the wrong way round, Heaven only knows. A
totalisator dividend consists of the amount available
on each investment after the total sum has been
divided into the necessary number of parts. Where
is there any necessity for getting tangled up into
multiplications and decimals to determine what this
is ? Having discovered how much money there was
to divide and how many were to join in the cut, the
writer found for 20 years that simple division
brought him within 2d. of actual truth every time,
and dividing by division is good enough for him
now. One cheerful correspondent with a formula
and three decimals demonstrates for the good of this
paper’s soul that the correct dividend on ,a single-
horse race in which there are 132 £1 tickets on the
winner out of a total of £756 is not (as stated by
“T.” (B. 26/4/’l7) £5 Is., but a little under
£2 11s. This seems to be just the chap to run
the machine—out at the ponies.
* * * *
If the yarn cabled from London about
Britain holding 4000 racehorses, each consuming' 141 b.
of oats per day, is dinkum, it is an even-money bet
that some cheap nags can be bought there just now,
by an}' Australian buyer who masters the problems
of how to get animals past the submarine and how
to feed them on the way out. These are not easy
problems, because the torpedo seems to be_ mighty
active and the likelihood of John Bull allowing food
for horses to be exported is fairly remote. Heaven
knows what is going to become of all these horses!
If they are turned out to grass there is bound to be
a yell, that the feed should be going into the interior
pf beef cattle. If they are kept on oats the circum-
stance would stir the community into fury. Whether
they ought to be appropriated for soup must in any
case become a burning question. Nothing would
make Australia realise the havoc caused by war so
much. as the news that animals capable of winning
the Newmarket were being sold for 4|d. a lb. That
would seem like the end of all things.
■» * * *
A crowd of ill-advised punters took 2
to 1 about Whitefield for the Glenhuntly Cup at
Caulfield last Saturday, and the big English horse
never gave them a hope of collecting. Aleconner
and the coming-on Eurobin finished a long way in
front of the favorite, and they were not good enough
to beat Semi-Blue at the weights. Semi-Blue is
evidently a gee of some class in handicap company.
* * * *
The handsome Eurobin, who won the
Doncaster last year before going amiss, has made
several appearances in public of. late and seems to
be in a fair way to recover his lost ' form. The
gelding must have been a good one when he captured
the Doncaster with Bst. 121 b. up, granting that he
was well placed in the crowd. A good position is
almost essential to victory in the Epsom or Don-
caster. Old sports reckon that nothing should be
heavily backed for a crowded mile handicap at
Randwick, if there are 20 triers, until the draw
for positions has shown how things are likely to go.
Some of the positivists say that “nothing after
number 10 has a hope.” The rule has exceptions,
but, anyhow, the marbles drawn by the first three
nags this year were numbered 8, 1 and 13 respec-
tively. And it was only the weight that stopped
Polvcrates, next to the rails.
* * * *
That good mare Carlita landed in Aus-
tralia a motherless foal, her dam having died on
the way over from Maoriland. Sold for 125 guineas
she afterwards annexed £lB,OOO, and her _ name
stands fourth on the list of Australian turf winners.
She won more than any other mare raced in the
Commonwealth. Now she goes to Linacre and the
paddock. It will be interesting to see what happens
to her family. Top-class racing ladies have not as
a rule been successful at the harem. Perhaps they
have been hurried there too quickly from the track
and ought to have been spelled for a year.
* * * *
A neddy to come into prominence down
in the Southern end of the Ma State lately is the
Poseidon —Court Beauty colt Royal Dude. Last
week he annexed three events, one at Albury and
two at Wagga. The latter successes included the
Wagga Cup. Royal Dude has always been, the pro-
perty of the O’Brien brothers, and prior to shifting
himself from Sydney was trained at Randwick by
Tuckermap, upon whom the mantle of the late Isaac
Earnshaw fell when Isaac took the trail to Sunset.
Royal Dude is a good stamp of colt. Having struck
form above the average he may be returned to Sydney
to interview an allegedly better class of animal than
he has recently been introduced to.
* * * *
Denman. Stakes and Tattersall’s Cup
double this year was one a lot of people failed to
discover, both going to outsiders. There were some
smart sprinters in the six furlongs event —La Steppe,
Snow Girl, Spurn, Dame Acre, Ardrossan, Quinn’s
Post among the number. Therefore Dave Whitton,
reckoned among the moderates, escaped notice and
Israel’s clamorous invitations to invest at 15-20 to 1
fell on ears that were deaf. La Steppe looked a
sure thing till Dame Acre and Ardrossan came into
the picture; but while these two, who started at
10’s, were arguing the point, Dave Whitton swooped
down, and MacSliortodds whooped him past the
terminus.
* * * *
Jimmy McMahon’s Karanaud King won
City Tattersall’s Cup at Randwick last year and
made the local punter feel very sorry for "himself.
James persuaded his bony nag to do the same thing
again last week. Meantime Karanaud King had
been confirming the reputation he had earned pre-
viously. Every time the "stable backed him it went
down heavily. Wherefore on Thursday Ike was
willing to oblige with 14 and 15 to T in a 16-horse
race, the public demanding Rushford as a rule,
with occasional helpings of Black Nut, Ulvg’s Isle
and Tofua. Nothing had a chance with the winner
who ran to the front at seven furlongs and stayed
there until it was too late to go back. Yet there
was nothing very surprising in all this. In the
Sydney. Cup Karanaud King did as well as anything
else for a mile and a half. .But he is no horse for
punters.
A stable has luck sometimes and some-
times the other thing. Rosehill trainer Booth
missed getting the real article at City Tattersall’s
meeting last week by providing, instead of the
winning- double, the horses that registered the two
seconds—Dame Acre and Rushford. Dodging a
double as narrowly as this is even more calamitous
than getting a leg in and having the other chopped
off - V
It is not often racegoers see a horse alive
one stride and cold meat the next, but they saw
it at Randwick on Saturday. Rongopai rose at the
hurdle opposite the Leger stand, hit the timber with
his knees and pitching straight forward on to his
head was dead before the rest of his body struck
the ground. He fell like a bag of lead, without
a tremor. Rongopai was bred in Maoriland. He
won a race or two about Sydney, but latterly seemed
to have grown disgusted with the cargo deposited on
him by handicappers.
In every race other than the Hurdles run
at City Tattersall’s meeting at Randwick the placed
horses were, with one exception, sired by _an im-
ported animal. The exception was itself an importa-
tion.

A smart youngster was seen out at Moore-
field (N.S.W.) on Saturday. Lady Swynford rushed
past everything else in the straight in the two-year-
old event and secured the big end of the stake by
half a length from Cool Lass. Lady Swynford won
a race at Menangle recently, and is well out of the
ruck. She was favorite with “Leslie English’s” dis-
appointing filly Nancy Sykes, but Nancy finished
a long way back. Her Ladyship was bred by W. and
F. A. Moses and is the first get of the imported
stallion Cardinal 'Beaufort to win races; in fact, she
is one. of the first of the sire’s progeny to compete.
The dam, Desmine, was also imported. This filly is
likely to be heard from again. 4

“Stringer” : Why doesn’t the A. J.C. order
its stewards to take the public into their confidence ?
When a hcrrse or jockey is disqualified on a metro-
politan course the bare fact is announced, and the
people who keep the game going are left to draw
their own conclusions about the reason. Recently, in
South Aus., the stewards resolved to let the public
into the secret, and the step is much appreciated.
If an owner is guilty of a wrongful act or a jockey
has proved too strong in the elbows, the fact should
be made known. As it is now, a-disqualified person
may spread any old yarn he thinks best and gain
a lot of sympathy, whereas if the real innards came
into the limelight the happening might act as a
deterrent on individuals who only allow their nags
to be slipped when the price in the betting ring
happens to be to their liking.
“Yarrum” : Jack Rice recently humped a
tidy weight, and did well with it; but what price
Gleeson’s Planet, that with 16st. 101 b. up at Wollar,
near Mudgee (N.S.W.), arrived an easy winner!
There were certainly no imported nags behind him,
but they were all “racehorses.”
What the Melbourne place fielders call
“the odds” get a kick from the Age. But the
Melbourne punter ought to see what these people do
at Randwick. It is plain murder, as this page has
remarked on many previous occasions. Take 6 to 4
on a couple of horses, 6 to 4 against two or three
more, even money a few and the rest 2 to I—-that's
about the general programme. The thing is a
reflection on the A.J.0., which licenses this public
fleecing of its patrons. Either the game should be
stopped altogether or some reasonable oversight exer-
cised. The writer has a notion that the best use to
which the totalisator could be put under the existing
system would- be to run it as a 1,2, 3 concern
with a flat division of investments. The results
would surprise the club, the public and Ike.
The Federal Government amusement tax
picked up £2579 at the A.J.O. Autumn four-days’
meeting at Randwick.
* , * * *
Dead, IT. C. Dangar, for 47 years a com-
mitteeman of the A.J.O. (he resigned in February)
and owner of a name familiar in the history of the
Australian turf. He bred many good horses and
raced a few, of which one was Gibraltar, winner of
the A.J.C. Derby and V.R.C. St. Leger, and the
best of all, Positano, imported for stud purposes,
but put into training to see what he was like. The
old man was 87, a native of N. S, Wales, a fine whip
in his day, and a rare good sportsman all his life.
Among those temporarily out of the Big
Fight are Chaplain P. S. Waddy (wounded) and
Lieut.-Col. R. F. Beardmore (ill). Waddy, the elder
brother of “Gar” and “Mick,” lifted a bat in Aus-
tralian cricket; and besides getting a “blue” at
Oxford (Eng.) played with Surrey county. He last
year resigned the headmaStership of King’s School,
Parramatta (N.S.W.), for khaki. Beardmore, who
did a lot for N.S.W. rifle-shooting, won the D.S.O.
and was wounded in the Somme battle.

“J.F.H.” (London) : Old-time Australian
pugilist Frank Slavin continues to hammer away at
the Huns as a member of the Canadian contingent.
Latest information about him is that he has returned
to the trenches after a partial breakdown. He was
offered two months’ leave to recuperate, but begged
his Colonel to allow him to resume duty, and the
Colonel acquiesced. Frank P. is credited with the
statement: “I don’t think Fritz will last much
longer. He would not want peace if the shoe did
not pinch somewhere. Fancy me in my early days
wanting to quit before the contest was decided if I
was winning! Ridiculous!” Fifty-seven days of
continuous shell-firing all around- got upon his
nerves, but the old tough ’un is all right again and
hopes to be on the spot when Germany throws up
the sponge.
When Darcy went away in a coal bunker
Australians waited to hear where he was being
starred. Instead there seems to be a message in
the newspapers every other day about the place in
which he has been barred. Meanwhile Darcy is
getting a fair deal from some of the newspapers
in Yankeeland. which do the young man the justice
of recalling “his great patriotic and self-sacrificing
efforts in Australia on behalf of his wounded country-
men.” Some of the yarns ought to appeal to Uncle
Sam. Here is one: “He travelled round the country
at one time for 500 miles taking part in entertain-
ments given to raise money for the wounded, paying
his own expenses. Eighty-seven towns were visited
and £250,000 was raised.” Yes, and then the
Censor, actuated by professional jealousy, went and
kept it out of the papers !
Mr. James Clabby, of U.S.A., is middle-
weight champion of Australia, having qualified by
length of residence to compete for the title with
Mr. Thomas Uren. They competed on Saturday at
Sydney Stadium and gave a fine exhibition of clever-
ness in hugging one another. It was hug and cuddle
and i>ush for 16 rounds —dexterous, no doubt, but
inexpressibly dreary. Clabby had a bit the best of
the weariness, and in the real boxing over the last
three rounds did better than Tommy. Wherefore
James got the verdict. But as an affray between
champions this conflict may not live more than a
century in the history of bash. There are rumors of
devastation ensuing between Clabby and Dave Smith
and MeGoorty.
A stranger, Bert Andrews, engaged with
Wave Geike at Brisbane on Saturday night. Albert
started proceedings with the austere fervor of an
anti-booze fanatic, and looked like transforming
Waverley into mutton. Then he suddenly assumed
the stiffness of a hot-pie cart and until the tenth
round was content to cut and run or hang on to
brother Geike. Thereupon the referee yawned with
great weariness and pushed Mr. Andrews off the
premises.
“E.V.K.” : A recent fight between a Chow
and an Abo. at Melbourne Stadium reminded me of
an argument I saw years ago between a Chinese
hawker and “a bloke be the name av Lynch.” It
was a willing go, and there was something doing
for the whole of five rounds, when the Chow retired
with a much-damaged countenance, while Lynch’s
clothes were clawed to ribbons. A bystander remarked
to John that he had got the worst of it. “No fe-ah !”
cheerfully replied the Celestial; “my face welly soon
bettah. Him got buy new clothes.”
* * * *
The end of the billiard battle between the
Lindrum brothers in Melbourne last week was a
crushing defeat for Walter, calculated to make that
gifted left-hander realise the fact that he is still
very young. It was not a championship test match,
the brethren having agreed to be sparing with the
red hazards from baulk, in which Walter is pro-
bably the most proficient. They mostly confined
themselves to the art of keeping the balls together,
the youngster endeavoring to prove himself as
expert as the champion at top-of-the-table play, and
somewhat quicker. He had a lot the worst of the
luck, as it happened, but he suffered more from his
ambition to be rapid—and then had to sit down and
watch his brother piling up the points which might
have been his had he nursed the balls more care-
fully. The licking will do young Walter good, but
meantime he feels cut up at being beaten by a third
of the game, which leaves him a long way from
championship form.
Strong-man Clarence Weber is rather keen
on motor-cycling for recreation. He is seen nowa-
days riding a 7-h.p. Indian, with four little Webers
in the sidecar. His own vehicle is a powerplus
Indian.
* * * *
The actual winner of the 200-mile motor-
cycle race run in Melbourne the other Monday had
the worst of luck. Eric Taylor, with Srnin. start,
was two miles in front of the field a mile from home,
when he ran out of petrol. He had then covered
more than 200 of the 203.8 miles, and had the fuel
held out he would have scored fastest time for the
course, as well as fastest lap.

A collection of “sports” described as
the Goulburfi (N.S.W.) Terrier Club had an outing
the other day. One of the dogs competing in the
bloodthirsty game was named Nurse Cavell!
Stan J. Young, of East Sydney, is a new
star in N. S. Wales athletics. The diversified efforts
with which he won the club championship were—
first in high jump, putting 161 b. shot, hop, step
and jump, 220yds. flat; tie (with W. Hutton) _in
broad jump; second in 120yds. hurdles and putting
121 b. shot; thirds in javelin throwing, 440yds.
hurdles and BSoyds. flat.
» * *• *
The “intercollegiate” sports in Adelaide
are restricted to the two schools of Prince Alfred
and St. Peter, and the former won this year by just
a comfortable margin—65 to 46. It was mostly the
work of three boys, Tassie the sprinter, Fowler the
jumper, and Wilson the all-rounder. Fowler, who
already held the intercollegiate record for the
hurdles, cut it down this year to 16 2-5.
* * * *
During the first meeting of the Associated
Schools’ Athletic Union held at Hobart the other
day, Launceston Church Grammar School accumu-
lated most points and a couple of promising sprinters
were discovered. Oldham, of Launceston, won the
100yds., 220yds. and 440yds. open championships;
Overell, of Hutchins’s School, the 440yds., 200yds.
and 100yds. championships limited to kids under 15.
The art of teaching the young idea how to scoot
seems to be going ahead by leaps and bounds in the
Speck.
* * * *
“Geordie”: I only want to doff my hat to
Sydney Soccer Club. When the war gong banged
there was a club membership of 120; last week it
numbered three —and each of the trio has good
reasons for being at home. If there is a club with a
better record let it stand up en masse or forever
hold its peace.
» * * *
Parramatta A’s fine shoot in the Metro-
politan championship grade (14 shots, 800yds.) at
Randwick on Saturday will take some rubbing out.
Eight scores (out of 10) aggregated 533 points—an
average of 66.62 out of a possible 70. .Of 112 whose
scores were counted in,. W. Green and W. Clark
were the only pluggers to notch 69. The effort
lifted the club’s aggregate to 2406, and with 88
points lead over Randwick Institute the team is well
ahead of the crowd in the rush for the title.
“Eryander”: I didn’t take shot-scatter
into account (“Foo-lar,” B. 26/4/’l7) because the
time-velocity variable is fundamentally concerned
only with the line from the muzzle to the exact
centre of the shot-diagram at striking-point. The
allowance for spread comes in only as a corollary,
and can be based upon the fact that, with a 12-gun
choked to 13, the diameter of the shot-disc works out
at one inch per yard of distance. A 12-14 choke
gives fin. per yard and a “relief” bore up to 1£
inch.

“Caly”: Re those flying-shot allowances
quoted from Curtis and; Harvey’s “Shooter’s Year
Book” (B. 19/4/’l7). Aren’t they delightful?—
sft. 9.6 in., Bft. 2.6 in., sft. 10.9 in., Bft. 6.lin.
Decimals! Ye gods and travelling ducks! Show
me the sport who can gauge a distance of sft. 10.9 in.
ahead of a gun-scared duck, and - I’ll be his man
Friday every opening-day for life. Ask any ordi-
nary wild-poulterer what “lead” he gives and he’ll
say: “Oh, about so-and-so.” He’s not finicky as to
inches and fractions because he has tested at a kero-
sene tin and knows what his gun scatters. The rest
is practice pure and simple.

“Groper”: Sample this for a pedestrian
record. A couple of years ago a “sand-wader” while
driving out beyond Yalgoo (W.A.) lost himself and
decided to camp until morning. Whilst unhitching
his horses one of them started forward and knocked
him down, the wheel passed over him and he was
left behind with a badly fractured leg, beside other
' minor injuries. He lay there throughout that night,
during the next day and until early the following
morning, when a wandering binghi happened along
within a mile or two and found a couple of horses
meandering about with trace-chains rattling about
their heels. Binghi followed their tracks and at
last came across the distressed sand-mariner, who had
been without water for the couple of days. The
blackfellow wasted no time in considering the situa-
tion, but after giving the injured man a drink and
covering him over with a couple of bags he turned
his bows for Yalgoo (between 60 anil 70 miles
away as an abo. travels) to get the only available
person capable of attending to these affairs. Arriving
at the township he explained the position to the
“doctor” (who is the local school-teacher, but who,
as side-lines, takes on any little job from under-
taker to dentist), and they set out in a buggy, and
the wreck was snugly in bed in Yalgoo before mid-
day on the following morning. The “doctor” vows
that binghi did his little ramble in from seven to
eight hours.
“Half-moon” : Had a day’s fishing recently
(on Friday, too) around Torres Strait reefs with
a line and caught fish of all sizes and varieties
until it got monotonous. Several fathoms down you
could see the clear water literally alive with them.
In shallower water my Binghi picked out some big
ones with the harpoon.' There were rock cod, trevalli,
salmon, blue fish, white fish, etc., about 15 or. IS
varieties. To finish up, the black captured a few
crabs and ■ also secured from the reef interstices, as
a result of some fancy diving, half a dozen lobsters.
Throw in the flask of whisky and the run home in
the glory of a tropical sunset, and I reckon my
chance of fetching up first in the fishing competition
is odds on. P.S.—Forgot to “record that we also
speared a turtle.
“E.”: Rock-fishers sometimes hoist a
small, chubby dark brown fish with very fine scales
resembling a sweep but without the silvery belly.
The stranger is often thrown away as being of
doubtful edibility, but it is of fair table variety.
I have taken this customer —his name is scaly
fin— W ith a small hook on the East coast, using
cungevoi or crab as bait. Another weighty gentle-
man of prime quality is the blue-belly, or lilac-
banded parrot fish. Fried, he is somewhat soft and
mushy, but when dried he is nearly, if not quite,
equal to ling. An irregular splash of intensely
bright blue under the chin serves to distinguish this
species. Our ling fish, too, though differing but
slightlv from the ling of commerce, is often thrown
awav as “cat-fish” because of the barbel on its chin.
The flesh is soft, but it is good when dried and
smoked.
* * * *
“Barb”: The red morwong, or sea-carp,
doesn’t appear in the market as often as it should.
Any warm evening in summer these fine food-fish
may be seen patrolling the rocky foreshores which
run from the north end of Balmoral to Chinaman’s
beach (Port Jackson). I have taken fair-sized ones
in that quarter with cungevoi and crab, but flesh-
bait is useless. The adult fish, which runs to nearly
2ft. in length, is mainly an outsider, rarely visit-
ing the lower parts of the harbor. Color, reddish-
brown. with pale orange fins; the side fins have
the characteristic wing-like shape common to all
the morwong family. The sea-carp is just as good
as its relatives the dusky, or common, morwong and
the jackass fish, the latter being distinguished 1W a
brown band across the shoulders. By the way, the
common morwong is often miscalled sea-perch, of
which there are several varieties. Another less
valuable member of the morwong family is the
grotesque cockatoo fish, a weed-feeder mainly, but
which bites occasionally at cungevoi or crab. Writer
has taken this freak in fairly deep water between
Bermagui and Tathra, South Coast of N. S. Wales,
on rocky grounds. The bird-like formation of
the head is remarkable. One would not be sur-
prised to hear the owner, grunt out the detestable
gag, “Pretty cocky/’
* * * *
“Bob D.”: Concerning “H.V.Es” and his
2ft. sandworm bait (B. 19/4/’l7). I have seen
worms 3ft. 6in. long dug from the Deewhy Beach
(N.S.W.). The fisherman didn't waste a lump of
good meat on them, either. He dragged a dry bone
over the sand and the inhabitants came out to
investigate the matter. The easiest way to get them
when once they show their heads is to put a shovel
down underneath them.
“H.Y.E.”: Pike, of which there are at
least two varieties in Australian seas, don’t come to
the table much, although they are said to be plentiful.
As a rule, before the bai.t reaches the noses
of the more aristocratic pike, some plebeian parrot-
fish or leatherjacket dashes out and scoffs_ it. As
pike feed close inshore it should be practicable to
take them in quantity by use of the trammel net.
With the line fish-bait or crab is efficient lure. The
flesh is on the soft side, '■’’.erefore should be cooked
ouieklv. He fries well.

“WITH HEALTH
Everything is a Source of Pleasure.**
—without it, nothing else, whatever it may be, is enjoyable.
Even the other personal blessings—a great mind, a happy
temperament—are degraded and dwarfed for want of it.
Everything should be secondary to health.
There’s no reason why you should not be healthy—Nature
intended you to be so.
The various organs of your body have certain functions to

Rebuked for standing idly by while a
neighbor beat his wife, a witness in a Rich-
mond (Vic.) assault case explained: “Well,
I did think it a bit of a cheek of him.”
The Victorian police force has one Chester-
field at least. Giving evidence at Fitzroy
(Melb.) the other day, he said: “When I
went into the bar the prisoner had the man
down on the floor, beating him about the
head with a soda bottle. I stopped him. I
said : ‘Haven't you got any manners?’ ”
Melbourne was lately looking for a .lost,
stolen or strayed comet, which the Observa-
tory had failed to observe, although it had
been spotted by numerous ''outsiders, includ-
ing an enginedriver, who said it was one of
tae largest he had ever seen. Evidently he
thinks in universes, and comets are the play-
things of his idle hours. He saw it “between
the Ferntree Gully railway line and the
point of sunrise.” Anyone having noticed
the stray, which, though it has a long tail,
bears no visible brands, is expected to im- •
pound it and notify the Observatory. Pos-
sibly it will be sold to defray agistment
expenses.
Kitchen jobs bore Billjim stiff. A Mari-
byrnong soldier got rounds of applause
rrom his cobbers the other day when on the
onions and spuds job. “What did you do in
the big war, Daddy?” he lisped in infantile
accents. Then in a deep bass: “Peeled onions
and potatoes, my boy.” Even the bombardier
charged with seeing the job fairly done
had to mask a grin with his hand.
A rather dusty lady entered a Sydney sub-
urban registrar’s office and applied for voters’
enrolment forms “for meself and ’usband.”
lhe official, having satisfactorily filled in
her card, proceeded to write up hubby’s elec-
toral pedigree, but was stopped dead with :
I say, Mister, I'll take the old man’s home
with me and let him fill it in himself” Then
in a confidential whisper: “To tell vou the
truth, I only know ’is nickname!”
“Fitzshrapnel”: We called him Rowdy
-Bill because he had never been known to
waste a word. One night he was returning
from the club by the usual route —over the
fence—-ami landed square in front of the
orderly-officer doing rounds. “Here, where
do you think you're going?” inquired that
surprised individual. “Back,” replied the
equally-surprised Bill, and. hurried in that
direction.
Some time ago a body of Victorian enthusi-
usts, assisted by the patronage and blessing
of the David Hennessys and the Frank Mad-
dens, started to encourage the raw-silk
industry in this country. Grub-farms were
established m various back-gardens and the
State provided large numbers of white mul-
berry trees at a penny each. Now, owing
to the non-arrival of silk-winding machinery
*rom ranee, the concern is in a state of
suspended animation. Its members at
present can only sit back and listen to their
mulberry-trees growing and hope for a bet-
ter time. Of course, the contriving of a
reliable Australian silk-winder is out of the
question, although in days gone by there
used to be 10-year-old Australian school-kids
who made practicable cocoon-strippers with
a pw bits of wire and cardboard, upon
which they skeined inconceivable quantities
of pale, lettuce-fed silk. But the idea of
making anything of the sort in this coun-
try for dealing with silk on a large scale
must naturally be quite beyond the powers
of the local manufacturer.
The bad habit of picking up things has
lingered with Margaret Herbert, of Sydney,
who earned three months in a suburban
court reecntly with a hand-saw, hammer
and shirt belonging to a tram conductor who
wasnt looking. The poor old thing (69
now) began to be convicted in ’B5, and to
date has scored 137 convictions. She has
walked hundreds of miles to gaol with scores
of nice policemen, many of them now dead
or pensioned off with corns. She will pro-
bably score 140 before the end of the year ;
and every added notch is a burst of derision
at the methods of the Law.
I The fine record of a; Trafalgar (Vic.)
family, which was opened with a D.S.O. by
Major George Matson Nicholas, is being car-
ned on by his brother, Lieut. Brvon Nicho-
las, who, at 24, has gained the Military
Gross. The Major, a fine soldier, was killed
in action almost at once after returning to
the 1 roiit from London, where he had mar-
ried Hilda Rix, a well-known Victorian girl.
Another brother has returned to Australia
and a fourth is still with the forces. Young
Bryon, whose aquiline nose and decided chin
give him a sticking resemblance to Welling-
ton as a young man, left as a private in
May, 1945. served five months in Gallipoli,
and went to France, being transferred from
Light Horse to Infantry. He got his com-
mission at an officers’ training-school in
Blighty, where he headed the list of success-
ful Australians.
“Vic”: The most remarkable Chinese in
Melbourne are Rose Quong, barrister Ah
Ket, and the Church of England Rev. J.
Gheong, M.A. At a concert in the Cathedral
Ghapter House last week, Rose Quong gave
a recital from “As You Like It,” with a
charming embodiment of Rosalind. Yon
could, easily mistake her for an Italian or a
Spaniard, and the same may be said of Ah
Ivet and Cheong. Cheong’s preaching,
always carefully subdued, is much admired
by the Anglicans. To his High Church
manner he adds a flavoring of Orientalism
that captures all feminine hearts. If he
were a white with far less ability, he would
get a snug vicarage.
Here is a. versatile Australian. L. E.
Dawson left with the Light Horse, trans-
ferred to a camel corps and has now joined
the air service. He is a son of pilot Daw-
son, of Torres Strait.
Edwin Jones, of Melbourne’s Ancient
Mariners, who initiated the sea-scout move-
ment. and has got; GO lads in training at
Albert Park, is off to Britain with the idea
of spending a skipper’s holiday in the North
►Sea aboard a mine-sweeper. If the sea-
scout training is continued on the lines that
Jones laid down, and in the same quiet sort
of way, it will do a lot of good to a growing
number of growing boys, and to he Com-
monwealth. The danger to be most care-
fully avoided is Sassiety patronage, which
is so very apt to follow a success of this
sort—and claim credit for it.

Miss Gladys Cooper Explains
Some Beauty Secrets.
The Popular London Star Favors Simple Methods.
I have been asked to give a few simple recipes that I know, either through personal
use or by observation, to be valuable to the toilet, and which are within the reach of the
average woman. In these days of £lOO facial treatments and elaborate and expensive
beautifying processes my suggestions may read like lessons in economy, but they are
not especially so intended. They are merely practical suggestions, in which the keynote
is “effectiveness.” All the materials or ingredients which I mention are either already
at hand in the home or may be readily procured from the chemist. Fortunately Ido not
suffer from the ailments or troubles enumerated below, but some people who are not so
fortunate have told me their experiences, and with your permission I will set forth some
remedies which they have found to be efficacious.
Home-made Hair Tonic.
M.v acquaintances say that a good stimulant
to the natural growth of the hair is borauium.
A very simple, effective and safe stimulating
lotion made up by mixing one ounce of bora-
nium with i pint of bay rum. This may be
applied to the hair roots occasionally with the
finger-tips with good results.
Complexion Renewals.
Complexion exports advise me that a normal,
healthy complexion is constantly renewing it-
self by dropping off tiny flakes of worn-out
tissue, thus revealing the fresh young skin
underneath. They say that when this process
is checked by age, expo-
sure or some of iLuay
causes, the complexion
becomes dull and ugly.
The rational treatment
recommended is to help
the skin perform the
natural functions of
“shedding” worn-out tis-
sue. For this purpose. I
am informed, there is
nothing so good as pure
mercolized wax, used for
a few nights, just as you
would use a face cream.
It is claimed that it pos-
sesses a special affinity’
for the effete scarf-skin,
which it quickly removes
by absorbing it. The face.
I am assured, will soon
look much younger and
prettier under this treat-
ment.
The Curling Iron.
Don't use a hot iron to
curl your hair. Some of
my friends make the cun-
ningest sort of curls
wherever they want
them simply by damp-
ing the hair with liquid
silmerine before retiring
at night. When the hair is dry in the morn-
ing it will he softly curly just where you
want it to he. This method is perfectly harm-
less, even beneficial to the hair, and the curls
last a long time. The liquid is quite pleasant
and neither sticky nor greasy.
How to Shampoo.
Most women, I am informed, do not know
how to use stallax properly when shampooing
with itl Unless the hair is naturally very
oily, a stallax shampoo may sometimes leave
it rather dry. But lam told if you will apply
olive oil freely to your hair and scalp just
before shampooing with stallax, the result is
most delightful. The hair will be left clean,
soft, bright and wavy, the olive oil having
properly balanced the action of this wonderful
hair cleanser.
Is Powder Necessary?
A great many women object to using pow-
der, for various reasons. The following
formula is a good one: Dissolve an ounce of
:
jar.
'
-i
MISS GLADYS COOPER.
Photo Wrather & Buy.
cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of water, or
witcli-liazel, and use it as a face lotion,
smoothing the skin with the fingers until it
is dry. This method is, perfectly harmless.
I am told that a really beautiful, natural, vel-
vety bloom results and remains for many hours
quite unaffected by the most trying conditions
out of doors or in the ballroom or theatre,
and that it gives a much more natural appear-
ance to some sains than does ordinary powder.
Grey Hair.
I have observed many attempts of many
people to conceal grey hair. Some of these
experiments were amusing, some disastrous,
and some were success-
ful. Personally I believe
I shall let my hair turn
when the appointed time
comes, but if I were
going to try to evade it,
I would give a trial to a
real old “grandmother”
formula that would prob-
ably do the work. This
formula. I am informed,
has been used with de-
crees of success for many,
generations, and consists
merely of two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite
mixed with three ounces
of bay rum. It is ap-
plied to the grey hair
a few times with a small
sponge, and ladies tell
me it appears to darken
the hair to a natural
shade, not like a dye. but
gradually and naturally.
Miscellaneous Hints.
A few useful hints from
my friends on aids to
beauty, are as follows:
Theeyebrowsand lashes
may be made to grow
stronger and darker by
applying mennaline, a
specially delicate pomade, with the finger-tips
at night.
Rouge is often obvious, and many women
therefore prefer to use powdered colliandum,
which gives a most natural tint.
The lips may be prevented from drying and
chapping by touching them with a stick
of prolactum, which also gives them a good
color.
Freckles are most effectively combated with
ammoniated mercury creams, of which jetta-
line is the best.
Note.—This interesting article on beauty
culture in general was written by Miss Gladys
Cooper at the request of the manufacturers of
Pilenta Soap;—the best complexion soap in the
world. On stile by all chemists. M

Up the hill the Canadians came,

For everything had all gone wrong.
His trenches lost, his men gone mad,
No Iron Cross—his luck was very bad.
But on the brave Canadians tore
And with the Huns they wiped the floor

They’d won a lot, and they’re winning still.

“F.N.” : The Great Public schools of Vic-
toria, which already have a remarkable record
of war-service, have arranged with the De-
fence Department to organise a new A.I.F.
unit, to consist entirely of men who have
passed through one or other of the six asso-
ciated schools of Melbourne and Geelong.
Recruits will camp, train, embark and light
together; and the plan will lit in neatly with
the decision of the military authorities to
make 19 years the minimum age for uncondi-
tional enlistment. There will be drawbacks, of
course, such as the inevitable suggestion of
snobbery; but the first sniff of battle-smoke
will settle all that.
A while back a Victorian firm of millers
missed £OOOO worth of sacks, and after
much investigation the police commandeered
one Jackson, an employee, and told him
they were quite sure he had annexed the
bags in company with others suspected, and
charged him with conspiring to defraud in
conjunction with one of these others.
Jackson pleaded guilty and disinterred £47?
from his backyard, the same being, he said,
his share of the proceeds of the deal. The
jury acquitted Jackson’s friend, and as the
law said Jackson couldn’t conspire with
himself he was discharged also. This ought
to have been hint enough to the law, but
that animal, being in a dense mood, seized
another of Jackson’s friends and charged
the two with conspiracy. Again Jackson
pleaded guilty, again the jury acquitted the
other and again Jackson was discharged. As
it hasn’t to pay for this foolishness out of
its own feed-bag, the law is probably willing
to continue till it gets tired. Ever since the
days when Matthew Davies found himself
in the dock this paper has deemed “con-
spiracy” a fool of a charge. Couldn’t some
dominating personality be persuaded to pass
a short Act declaring that a man may con-
spire with himself?

IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free, 1/3. Free sample posted.
ROCKE, TOMPSITT & CO., Melbourne.

IN BANANA LAND
“Yes” may be Right; “No” may be
Right; but “Yneos” is Absurd.
The one excuse for the Leg. Council is that
it is a “House of Review,” auditing the pro-
posed legislation of the people’s Chamber.
Blit, as a matter of fact, the Queensland
Council spends most of its time dropping its
own ideas into the Assembly’s Bills, and
making them incongruous. The result is
a crop of inconsistencies which merely give
work for the lawyers. The measure which
created the Insurance Department is a case
in point. The Assembly proposed to make
workmen’s compensation insurance a State
monopoly, on the ground that, since the
State made the insurance of all kinds of
workpeople obligatory, it was the State’s
business to provide insurance at the lowest
possible cost; and you can’t get insurance
at the lowest possible cost if there is com-
petition and canvassers have to be employed.
The monopoly idea was thus the foundation
on which the Government built up the mea-
sure. But the Council threw iu a clause
which was inconsistent with the spirit of all
the rest—a clause providing that the Gov-
ernment might authorise private cos. to take
workmen’s compensation risks. The result
has been chaos and costs; and the trouble
is even now wending its expensive wav to
the Privy Council. If the Leg. Council'bad
the job of passing an insurance bill it would
probably have produced a workable measure,
especially if. as usually happens, the busi-
ness was left to half a dozen members. So
also the Assembly could be trusted to pro-
duce a workable measure, though different
in principle. But to hand the job over to
two Houses is merely to invite the same
sort of trouble that you would get if you
hired an architect to produce a set of plans
for a freezing chamber and then passed the
drawings and specifications on to another
architect whose idea was that what you
really needed was a crematorium. Wo ought
to face the situation. Let us have either the
Assembly or the Council. If popular gov-
ernment is a success we will have the ;
Assembly. IT it is a failure let-us frankly
do away with the Assembly. To pull our
own legs, as we are doing now. is merely an
expensive and irritating farce.
Both Methodism and Liberalism in
Queensland lost a tower of strength when
Hiram Barnes passed out last week at 84.
He was a Sussex man who landed in X. S.
Wales as a boy, and finally migrated to
Queensland. Cobb and Co. engaged his
energies first; then he dived into business
for himself, starting the now important con-
cern of Barnes and Co. Ten children, 49
grandchildren and 19 great-grandchildren
survive the old man; two of the sons have
made a mark in Queensland State politics.
“Nick”; Rockhampton is feeling sick just
now. The town is kept going by: (1) West-
ern Queensland trade; (2) Mount Morgan:
(3) Lakes Creek Meat Works. At Mount
Morgan 1600 men came out over a dispute
as to whether Judge McCauley really meant
absolute preference to unionists in a recent
Arbitration Court judgment. In all. 2500
men are directly affected, and Mount Mor-
gan’s output of 890 tons of copper per month
is reduced to nil. Another 500 men are
thrown out of work at Lakes Creek on
account of a rumpus between the wharf-
laborers and the Federal shipping authori-
ties. Rockhampton’s oversea exports are
shipped from Port Alma, about 40 miles from
town. The “port” is simply a wharf built on
mangrove swamps. There is no township, ■
and labor has to be taken from the town.
The men claim payment for all time away
from home, as no sleeping accommodation is
provided at. the Port. The Board responsi-
ble for shippiug Australia’s frozen meat
replies: “Well, anyway, we have more frozen
meat than we can find bottoms for.” So it
seems that the Shippiug Board regards this
trouble as a bit of luck that will relieve it of
the worry of looking round for boats. The
provision of about 40 camp-stretchers with
mosquito-nets would settle the Lakes Creek
trouble. A bit of straight talk to all con-
cerned would put the Mount Morgan fool-
ishness at an end. Both disputes have lasted
for weeks, and though civilisation is clamor-
ing for food and copper, nobody seems to
care. The Win-tlie-War politician is too
busy electioneering.
The Bananaland Government, having
established butchers’ shops, hotels, sawmills,
cattle stations, coal mines and other ven-
tures, has been deputationised to put up a
State crematorium. Already 1000 acres
are utilised for cemeteries in and around
Brisbane and another 700 acres are re-
served at Mount Gravatt. For the crema-
torium five acres would suffice, and the
cost would be only £5OOO. Doctors, barris-
ters and parsons supported the request, and
Home Secretary Huxham promised that the
necessary Bill would be introduced next
session. But in this matter it looks as
though the State ought to adopt the prin-
ciple which it applies to the extension of
gas mains. The citizen who wants a gas
service has to undertake to be a customer.
Suppose the Government were to require a
personal guarantee of 5000 customers
before proceeding with the new heating
premises? That might save the thing being
such a frosty crematorium as the one with
which Adelaide has for years been supplied.
A patch in the Legislative Council case
before the Bananaland Full Court:—
Mr. Justice Lukin: Is the question of whether the
Commonwealth Act prevails a question of the limits
inter se of the constitutional powers of the Common-
wealth? In my opinion it is not.
The Attorney-General: Well, in my opinion it is.
Mr. Justice Lukin: It does not matter what your
opinion is.
. The Attorney-General (emphatically) : And perhaps
it does not matter what your Honor’s opinion is.
either.
The Chief Justice: It is not customary, Mr.
Attorney, to tell a Judge that vour opinion is con-
trary to his.
The Attorney-General: And it is not. customary for
a Judge to tell me that it does not matter what mv
opinion is.
But Queensland people have got quite used
to that sort of thing.
DAVID AND JONATHAN.
Democracy : ‘Ms the Lord liveth, and as my soul liveth, there is hut a step between
me and death.”
“Good Australian,” from ’Frisco:—
One “F. E. Barbat, a distinguished Australian”
who was “a member of the Queensland Parliament
irom 1911 to 1915,” has been busy interviewing the
’Frisco yellow press and predicting- civil war in Aus-
tralia very soon. He says the battles.of the coming-
war will be the same as those of the American Civil
War, and just as bloody. “The Australian people
are' accustomed to get everything from their Govern-
ment. To satisfy their demands, the Government has
borrowed from England. If it cannot borrow any
more I fear civil war will result.” And again:
“Matters will come to a head when our thousands of
soldiers return from the war looking for work and
livelihoods. Only civil war can result unless we get
a national Parliament.” Who is this “distinguished
Australian”? He told the newspaper men: “I am
touring the United States on behalf of the Common-
wealth Government and the Government of Queens-
land to secure a supply of rails and rolling-stock for
1600 miles of Australian railroads.” Is this true?
Is it possible any such sapheaded person lias been
sent to represent Australia as a business negotiator?
What sort of tact does a man possess who states
definitely that “civil war is near in Australia. The
differences of the Socialists and the capitalists will
lead to revolution” ? But who is “Mr. F. E. Barbat,”
anyway ?
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 28, 1917.
My Dear Thargo.mindah,—
Their Exes, have been on the move this
week. First, they visited the Show at
Toowoomba; then Lady Gookl-Adams broke
open a fete on behalf of the Y.W.C.A; next,
she put the finishing touches to a tea-party
at Brancepeth. the hospitable home of
Mayor and Mayoress Hetherington. This
party was in honor of Miss Godfrey, of Mel-
bourne, a leading and kindly light in the
Seamen’s Mission.
The Governor filled in part of his time at
the end of the week presiding at an enter-
tainment in Centennial Hall in aid of the
Hamilton branch of the Red Cross Society.
Mrs. Ted Barry and Miss Massey Jackson
were the organisers, and they had all the
help that could be' accepted from generous
amateurs.
Anzac Day saw the town crowded on the
route of the military march. The returned
soldiers were led by the men of the 9th
Battalion, the first Australian unit to re-
ceive its baptism of blood and fire at Galli-
poli. Last year there was a little heart-
burning. The 9th demanded to be at the
head of the column, but they were told by a
shocked official that Light Horse always led.
The argument was settled on this occasion
by the gallant 15th. who refused to fall-in
at all unless their “mates of the 9th” went
in front. So men of these two battalions
headed the advance with representatives of
tlie 2nd and stli Light Horse following.
The G.-G. took the salute at the base in
Market Square; his Ex. Goold-Adams and
her Ex., Mrs. T. J. Ryan, several Ministers
and their wives, the Mayor and Mayoress,
Rrig.-General Irvine and Brig.-General
Foster accompanied him. Over 400 returned
men and 1322 members of the A.I.F.
tramped past.
Four medals were presented by the Gover-
nor-General. L’te. Bernard James Dillon, of
our 9th, received a military medal and a
thunderous ovation. He is a Longreach
man, and obtained his decoration and a
smashed arm at Pozieres.
St. George’s Day (Monday) was another
gold-braid and khaki occasion. At the con-
cert in the Exhibition Hall there was a
record attendance, and amongst the audience
were the two Governors, Lady Goold-Adams,
Premier Ryan and Ms missus, an aide or so.
one Mayor and ever so many real Mackays
in old khaki uniforms. It was a great night.
Atherton, a Royal Flying Corps man and
an old Rockhampton boy, recently fell from
the clouds, or thereabouts. When the
onlookers went along with a broom to sweep
up the pieces all the fragments they could
find belonged to a tooth. However, the doc-
tor said something about shock, and ordered
six months’ leave, much to the bird-man’s
disgust.
Mrs. A. Anderson, the energetic secretary
to the Red Cross Society, has gone into hos-
pital. She has done so much patriotic work
for two years past that the temporary break-
down is understandable.
Another bull’s-eye to the little blind god.
The potted are Lieut. Alex. Russell, only
son of the Russells of Mawallok, Beaufort.
and Jess Fairbairn, a daughter of the squat-
ting Fairbairns, of Logan Downs.
Pianist Lucy Baker (or, rather, Mrs.
Levy, as she has been for some years)
dropped in upon us this week from Singa-
pore. She brought her two babies to inspect
Bananaland.
J. S. Cameron, who owned a collection of
antiques, said to be the finest in Queens-
land, died suddenly on Thursday, aged 54.
His Ex. Mun-Ferguson has taken back
with him to the south about 30 specimens of
Queensland timbers, the beauties of which
he intends to bring to the notice of available
markets. That’s easy. All he has to do, as
you know, my dear, is to label them “Im-
ported,” and the rush of customers will
astonish him.
Died of wounds in France, Lieut. Beau-
mont Bundle, son-in-law of F. D. Needham,
formerly of Canning Downs, and brother of
Mrs. Moncrieff Scott, of Warwick. Deceased
enlisted as a private in the Remount Unit,
then got a commission and joined the artil-
lery. He served in the S'African war,
where his brother, Major Jack, was killed.
At St. Stephen’s Cathedral on Anzac Day
the collection was handed with pleasure to
the Residential Club for Returned Soldiers
and Sailors. The collectors didn't, of course,
shake the plate at the many damaged
Anzacs in the congregation, but at the finish
of the service one boy in khaki marched up
the aisle with a hatful of silver and a
message that he and his mates insisted on
sharing in the good work. Father Coen, who
taught the lesson of the day, is a brother of
Major Fred Coen, recently killed in action,
and Father McKenna, who assisted in the
service, has also lost a brother at the Front.
Lieut. Lloyd, whose father is railway
traffic-manager in this division, has now
gained the Military Cross, and, at the same
time, alas, a wound —dimensions unknown.
The young man who left Australia as Cor-
poral Lloyd served a term at Gallipoli, after-
wards going to France, and began his col-
lection of decorations with the D.C.M. after
Pozieres.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Half-moon”: Ads. in the Queensland
papers proclaim the advantages of employ-
ing the office of the Public Curator instead
of a lawyer to fix up wills, administration
of estates, etc. Here is a sample of its expe-
ditious methods, My father died in May.
1916, with £2OO insurance, practically his
only estate, and leaving his will properly
completed. The beneficiary was his faithful
housekeeper. Almost a year has gone by and
she is still waiting for her money. About
three months after date of death the insur-
ance was claimed by the Curator and, I
presume, duly paid to him. Several letters
written to the office recently anent the
estate have not received the courtesy of a
reply, f thought that the office was created
to simplify legal processes and expedite ad-
ministration matters; but I begin to suspect
that the papers in this case are :enmeshed in
red tape.
Minister for Lands Hunter has a nasty
habit of making people pay their debts to the
Government. The other day he had a squar-
ing-up with the Gregory North Rabbit
Board, with the result that, if all goes well,
the State will benefit to the tune of £23,800.
This Board was established in 1892, and
money was lent to it for the construction of
a rabbit-proof fence. The arrangement was
that the Board was to levy a rate on the
squatters to provide interest and redemp-
tion. The rate struck was not sufficiently
high, and since 1899 no payment has
been made. Under pressure from the Mini-
ster. however, the Board has now agreed to
hand over £2OOO, a year, commencing with a
payment for 1916.
“Auriferous”: Re “Platman’s” story (B.
12/4/T7) of Barclay navigating the Burde-
kin river. I do not believe any one in an
open boat could successfully pass the rapids.
Even supposing you got over the Falls, how
could any human skili steer a boat through
the boiling cauldron and huge granite bould-
ers that infest the river just below the Falls?
Abou t 1885 a to an named Stubley and a
mate took it into their heads that thev could
navigate the Burdekin from Satchell’s 111
miles from Ravenswood) down to the sea.
They were seen passing the junction of the
Suttor in a borrowed boat, but they never
turned up to give a record of the remainder
of their journey.

Grey Hair Unnecessary.
A simple, old-fashioned home-made lotion that
will restore the color of youth.
One need not resort to the very question-
able expedient of liair dye in order not to
have grey hair. The grey hair can easily
be changed back to a natural color in a
few days’ time merely by the application
of a simple, old-fashioned and perfectly
harmless home-made lotion. Procure from
your chemist two ounces of tammalite con-
centrate and mix it with three ounces of bay
rum. Apply this to the hair a few times
with a small sponge and you will soon have
the pleasure of seeing your grey hair gradu-
ally darkening to the desired shade. The
lotion is pleasant, not sticky or greasy, and
does not injure the hair in any way. [1

Big Ben tried to
pull through with-
out advancing his
price. The odds
were too great.
Before the first
advance, he placed
advance orders for
material at favourable
prices. The prices he
now pays are double
and treble the old.
He gamely took ad-
vances in freight which

Robert Bruce Walker, who defeated
Brinsley Hall for the Hawkesbury seat m
the N.S.W. Assembly, is son ot William
Walker, who represented the same district
from 1860 to 1869 and had a seat in the
Leg. Council from 1887 until Ins death in
1908. His father was a clergyman who
settled at Windsor in 1868, and became a
schoolmaster in the old Hawkesbury town ,
he had come to Sydney the previous year
under the auspices of Dr. Lang. Lhe Jjew
member is connected with some or the oldest
families in Australia, Wm. Walker having
married first in 1803, Mary, daughter ot
Jonathan Hassall, whose ancestor was one
of the missionaries to Tahiti in the seventeen-
nineties.
Most men aren’t much in the swim when
they get to be 80; but Joseph Hatch, who
is mixed up with Macquarie Island and the
penguin-oil business, is right there, lo be
better able to give a hand with his schooner,
the Rachel Cohen, when she goes nosing-
round the Macquaries, he lately put in some
spare time in the summer learning to swim.
Joseph arrived in Melbourne in 1857, but has
put in most of his time at Invercargill
(M.L.). About 10 years-ago he got a lease
of Macquarie Island from the Speck, which
is supposed to own it, and has gone into
the only industry that flourishes there —the
boiling down of the local penguins to get the
oil, which is used in making cordage;
George Fairbairn, who once held the
proud position of member for Faulkner, and
is now standing for the Senate, recently
celebrated his 62nd birthday. But he doesn’t
look within miles of it. Easily the most
ornamental of the four woolly Fairbairn
brothers, he . is “white” every inch of him —
and he owns 74. Mrs. Fairbairn spends
her life in Red Cross work when she isn’t
nursing the new grandson or canvassing.
Some years ago, when daughter Alison
wedded Major Balmain, now fighting in
France, Toorak nearly broke a bloodvessel
with excitement over the marriage with a
cavalryman. The little village was stiff
with arches and flags—its own mother
wouldn’t have known it.
In putting the case for the poorly-paid
fourth and fifth-class women teachers of
Victoria (Parliament has declared that they
must be paid four-fifths of the salaries of
men teachers, but most of them get barely
two-thirds), one speaker at the Teachers’
Conference made a point that should be
thought over. “The women,” she said, “be-
ing patriotic, refused to harass the Govern-
ment during the war. Result—indifference.
The railway men, being unpatriotic, threat-
ened to strike. Result —increased pay.”
Peacock’s quaint Government lately got
busy at “economising” and “increasing pro-
duction” in a new direction when the
Rochester fruit-growers asked to be allowed
to slay ’possums, which have increased under
protection and become a nuisance. Peacock
told Rochester it might destroy ail the ’pos-
sums it could up till June 30,\but it must
on no account sell the skins! Some of these
brilliant wits will presently be telling land-
owners to destroy their rabbits, but to be
sure not to use them as food.
Go to Forbes (N.S.W.) if you want to
keep on paying your life-insurance premiums.
Malo Guihot and his wife lately celebrated
the 70th anniversary of their wedding. Malo.
who is a native of France, is 93, and Eliza-
beth, his wife, who was once Miss Robert-
son, of Crookwell (N.S.W.), is BS. There
are 11 sons and daughters, 58 grandchildren
and 59 great-grandchildren.
“A.H.C.”: The passing of old George
Brown, veteran Methodist missionary, has
touched off a magazine full of reminiscences
concerning the part he played in the sequel
to the massacre of native teachers in New
Britain in ’7B. As a matter of fact, Brown
neither formed nor led the expedition against
cannibal chief Talile and the other mur-
derers ; he simply went with it to do the
best he could to prevent unnecessary blood-
shed. Though punishment was limited to
the murderers, Brown was put on trial
before Chief Justice Gorrv in Fiji, and it
was not the latter’s fault that he was
acquitted. On his return voyage from Fiji,
the John Wesley came near foundering, and
subsequently Brown was invited by a pious
brother to say what his thoughts were on
that occasion. It was an easy one for
Brown. “I thought how glad old Gorry
would be that, whilst lie could not hang me,
Providence had drowned me.”
“X.P.D.” from Salisbury Plain
T don’t know who holds the record for long service
in the Empire’s forces (Active List), hut 1 think
that the tally of a straight-backed member of the
A.I.F. now on duty amidst the slush of an English
winter would take some beating. He joined the
regular army 49 years ago, and is now 62 (they
took them young in the ’6o’s). His ribbons number
one more than Kitchener’s and one lesss than
Itoberts’s (or the other way about), and his decora-
tions include a V.C. and the Star of India. Though
iiis rank now isn’t that of a coloricl, Billjim gener-
ally looks upon him with as much respect as if - he
carried crossed swords.
“Peg-Leg”: T. P. O’Connor, the new
English film censor, wants to see the cinema-
tograph in English schools. Victoria already
has it in nearly all the country schools in
the State. Besides being used for educa-
tional purposes, the cinema frequently lights
np to swell the school funds. The films
shown to the classes are mostly descriptive
of travel and industry. A small machine is
used, and the pictures are obtained from a
film library run by the suppliers of the
instrument.
There are to be some awkward questions
for country candidates at the Vic. State elec-
tions next December, At Benalla the o.ther
day the municipalities which have got to-
gether as the Decentralisation and Country
Development League made themselves a
platform, and one plank includes the reduc-
tion of the Assembly from 65 to 46 members.
They reckon, too, that 26 Leg. Councillors
are enough, that the State could not be any
worse off with five Ministers instead of eight,
and without a State Governor and a lot of
State Parliamentary frippery. Jim Cur-
now and other leading lights in the new
crusade want to see the State Parliament
wiped out altogether, but they reckon that
this is as much as the weaker brethren will
swallow just now.

THE ROYAL BANK of AUSTRALIA LIMITED
HEAD OFFICE: MELBOURNE, VICTORIA.
FIFTY-EIGHTH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 27th April, 1917.
The Directors have pleasure in placing before Shareholders the annexed Balance-sheet and Statement
of Accounts for the Half-year ending 31st March, duly audited and certified.
The profits, after providing for a bonus to the staff, amount to £22,164 8 1
which it is proposed to deal with as follows, viz.:—
To pay a Dividend at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum .. . . .. .. £l2 000 0 0
Transfer to Reserve Fund (making it £270,000) .. .. .. .. ’. io’ooo 0 0
And to add to the amount carried forward from last balance .. !! . ! 164 8 1
„ . . , , , £22,164 8 1
The Balance carried forward from last Half-year, £7991/8/3, will thus be increased to £8155/16/4.
By order of the Board. H. T. WILSON, General Manager.

I, Hugh Thurburn Wilson, General Manager of the Royal Bank of Australia Limited, do solemnly
and sincerely declare: —
That th£ Reserve Fund Account, amounting to £260,000, and the accumulated profits, amounting
to £30,155/16/4, are used in the business.
That the accompanying Statement and Balance-sheet of the Bank is to the best of my knowledge
and belief true in every particular.
Names, addresses and occupations of the persons who are Directors of the Company at the date of
the statement: —Randal James Alcock, J.P., merchant, Melbourne; Honorable Sir William H. Irvine, K.C.,
M.H.R., barrister, Melbourne; William George Sharp, gentleman, Melbourne; Alexander Macneil,
merchant, Melbourne.
And I make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue
of the provisions of an Act of the Parliament of Victoria rendering persons making a false declaration
punishable for wilful and corrupt perjury.
Declared at Melbourne, in the State of Victoria, this 17th day of April, one thousand nine hundred
and seventeen. Before me, B. H. Alston, J.P. H. T. WILSON.
We certify that we have audited the above balance-sheet, and that the same is correct; that we have
not observed nor become acquainted during the audit with any breach of the “Companies Acts’’ committed
by the Company, or any Director, Manager or Employee thereof; and that all our requisitions as
auditors, including those with regard to the private balance-sheet, have been complied with.
Melbourne, 18th April, 1917. THOMAS KIDD, F.S.A.A., Eng. 1 » 1H
ARTHUR W. CLEVELAND, F.I.A.V. / ' u 110 '

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
To the Shareholders of The Royal Bank of Australia Limited. Melbourne, 18th April, 1917.
Ladies and Gentlemen, —We report that we have audited the Books and Accounts of your Bank for
the Half-year ending 31st March, 1917, and that so far as we are in a position to form an opinion,
from the information and explanations which we h ive required, the Balance-sheet and Accounts have
been drawn up in accordance with the provisions of the “Companies Acts,” and present a true and
