The Inky Way.
On the principle that under Argus man-
agement the heaviest rise to the top, John
Mackintosh succeeds the late E. T. Fricker
as editor of the Australasian. Mackintosh
is a youthful-looking and benign colossus
whose speech and writings are in keeping
with his fruity voice and ripe conservative
view's. He is well over six feet in height,
and built in proportion. Before joining the
Argus as one of the leader-writing staff
some seven years ago, he was on the Inky
Way in the West. From the caligraphic
point of view his copy is excellent, and, no
doubt, now that he leaves the direct tutelage
of Argus editor Cunningham to edit on his
own. it can be trusted not to pledge the
Australasian to free love, sharing out,
voting for Frank Tudor, and similar devices
of the Evil One. Mackintosh is barely 40.
* * * *
“Segundo”: It was in the days when edi-
tor Cunningham, of the Argus, had just
returned from England and the Press Con-
ference, bringing with him his honorary
“Litt.D.” By-and-bye it began to seep
and percolate through the office that he
was to be addressed as “Doctor.” Now,
standing in a certain position on the oppo-
site side of Collins-street, and looking up
to the Argus first floor, one can see Cun-
ningham at the anvil where he forges the
fetters —no, that’s not right—at the shrine
where he tends the ancient light of Con-
servatism. One who knew him, standing
in the position hereinbefore mentioned, and
catching sight of the ponderous editorial
brow, remarked to the friend whom he was
showing the sights: “And there’s the prin-
cipal Collins-street Doctor —writing out
prescriptions which no one ever takes.”
“ J.G.”: What is the longest Australian
newspaper title? I nominate the Eltiiam
AND WHITTLESEA SHIRES ADVERTISER AND
Diamond Creek Valley Advocate, late
The Evelyn Observer, printed at Hurst
Bridge, Victoria. The top-heavy weekly was
first published in the bush at Kangaroo
Grounds in ’72, and stayed there modestly as
the Evelyn Observer till the other day,
when the plant was removed to Hurst Bridge.
When new r s is scarce the whiskered sub-
scribers get their money’s worth by reading
the new title.
* * * *
“Bill Bowyang” (London) ; “Inside” jour-
nalists have been watching with the greatest
interest a duel between Reuter’s and the
organisation known as Wireless Press.
Wireless has advantages in the matter of
speed, and of late has been an easy first in
landing most of the daily communiques,
especially the Russian and Roumanian. But
it can’t yet reach the old standard of accu-
racy.
* * * *
A suggestion that interviews are not
always printed as written has given Judge
Darling, the comic-relief of the English
Bench, a chance for a cut at the Bar. “If
I were a journalist, and I wrote one thing
and the newspapers printed another, I should
resign. In the same way, if I passed sen-
tence of death on a prisoner, and the sheriff
went and hanged the counsel, I should
resign—if it happened often.”
* * * *
“Adelaide”: Though Melbourne and Syd-
ney have claimed just-dead T. W. Harris
for the past 30 years, he is well remembered
by the older generation of Adelaide journal-
ists. He arrived here from England as a
small boy in ISSO, and was commercial
reporter on the Register— with the job also
of sending _ wires to the Argus and S. M.
Herald— .till commerce tempted him away
in 1886.
** t « »
Gladesville (Sydney) Mental Hospital has
got a new paper in place of the Gladesville
Gossip, which went out when the patient
who ran it left the institution five years
ago. The new sheet is called the Tarban
Trumpet —Tarban Creek was Gladesville’s
original name. The versatile inmate who
does the writing, typesetting, editing and
printing covers a wide range from comments
upon the war to items of personal and local
importance.
“Rustler”: Severely wounded in action,
Jack Craig, editor and proprietor of the
Wedderburn (Vic.) Express. Although
badly damaged about the head he hopes to
have another slap at the Huns before again
taking over the management of his paper
from the voluntary committee of townspeople
who are at present running the show.
,** * *
Norman Malcolm, who was on Adelaide
Register before he went to the war, has
found his 16 stone unsuited to Egyptian
deserts, and is back in Adelaide recuperat-
ing. Malcolm, who draws a captain’s, screw,
learned the fighting business in S’Afriea.
Since that scramble he has collected his cash
in many nets. In Westralia he was stock-
inspector ; when that game lost its charm
he went to Adelaide prospecting for para-
graphs.
MY MUSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My muse is a minx with a spell for a smile.
She gallops a waggon of whims through the skies,
And teasings capricious and pranks all the while
She pours upon me who would sing to her eyes.
She coaxes me on like a siren at play,
Then leaves me alone with a shadow to doze.
As faithless as Venus, she flits with a fay,
Or marries her moods to the rim of a rose.
I went to her house and I opened the door
To peep at the exquisite corridors there,
And thousands of dreams that were strewing the floor
Cried “Constancy only can win the most fair.”
And so on'her lily-white doorstep I wait,
Or wander away with my lover in glee.
Now faithful, now fickle I toy with my Fate,
For I am a maiden as wilful as she.
N.S.W.

The Dark Ladye.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A GREY moth fluttered, muffling through
the gloom,
Save which n'o other sound there
was at all;
A grey moth fluttered and then all was still.
In twilit wonder, scarce mine eyes had skill
To pierce the distant darkness, whence the
fall
Of Her slow treading footsteps told the doom
That I so feared and longed £or was at hand.
Again the grey moth fluttered and was gone;
So did I wait Her coming and alone, v
lu this mad, magic, dream-enamored land.
"All will be well,” I murmured to my heart:
"Be brave and cling with love unto thy fear,
As zest alike to gift of boon or bane!”
And yet I trembled, murmuring. Again
I heard Her footfall as She came more near,
While all the mystic darkness seemed a part
Of this Her coming, only that Her eyes
Now looked upon me with a holy gaze
As made me kneel obeisant, so to praise
The peace that dwelt within them, angel-
wise.
And then She came towards me, and I felt
A hand laid on my head and heard a voice
Soft, mellow-throated, saying to me, “Rise,
Who loves me truly loves for aye and dies.
Behold the Bride who waits thee of thy
choice!”
Yet, craven fear upon me, still I knelt
And dared not do the thing She bade me do,
Spurning Her gift, unworthily afraid.
I heard Her walking from me down the
glade,
And, with Her going, peace went from me
too.
Whel'e, earlier had been darkness now was
light,
Where earlier had been silence now was
sound,
And where was peace was torment now in-
stead.
A million demons gibbered in my head,
And, yelling, whirled about me and around,
Nor stilled them.once nor granted me respite.
And what a light! Vermilion clotted suns
That swelled and heaved and, overbloated,
burst,
Were teeming near me, till the air accurst
Stank and w r as thick with blood and foam
at once.
Then One whose flesh but hung in tattered
strips
Upon her creaking bones beside me sat.
Grinned in my face and chucked me on the
chin,
Declaring as She did so, mid the din
Of screaming rabble round us, “I am That
Who made thee fear the bridal!” and Her
lips
Cracked as She said it, “I am Fear-of-Deatli!
The charnel Thing that makes a people fear
That Other’s- footstep when She wanders
near!”
She disappeared and then I drew my breath.
The darkness came again and still I knelt.
The air was sweet and holy, and a voice
Soft, mellow-throated, said unto me, “Rise,
Who loves me truly loves for aye and dies.
Behold the Bride who waits thee of thy
choice!”
No fear was on me, peace was all I felt:
Then I arose and took Her by the hand.
A grey moth fluttered by us and was gone
And only She and I were there alone,
In that mad, magic, dream-enamored land.
JIAOBILA^D.
BERNARD INGLERT.
The Lease.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAVE some pity for landlords, especi-
ally my own landlords. I’ve had quite
a number of them who needed it. They
were people with a bad habit of calling on
Monday morning for the rent, as if they
expected to get it. Some landlords don’t
call. I have known those who have, instead,
sent men—disreputable-looking men. But
even those landlords who don’t call or send
seem to expect their rent just the same.
Hope is a wonderful thing, and expectation
is a large edition de luxe of hope.
The landlords who deserve the most pity
are those who possess the very little pro-
perty which, apparently, they regard as a
dangerous thing; say, one cottage besides
the humpy they are living in. It gives them
so much anxiety that it would be very much
better for them if they hadn’t got it. When
it is empty they worry about the rates and
taxes; when it is occupied they are terribly
afraid that the tenants will knock it down
with a tack-hammer ; and when the tenants
move they rush round frantically to make
sure that the carriers haven’t taken the
house with the furniture.
I had an office once next to a house and
estate agent who had one ewe-lamb house of
his own. He advertised that it was to let,
giving tlie neighborhood and the rent —a
very cheap rent for a close-to-the-city house
—and he wanted a “selected tenant.” I
didn’t know this until I arrived in the morn-
ing and found the stairs and the landings
crowded with people. Some of them had
brought their breakfast, in order to be in
time. They rushed me when I approached,
and that is how I learnt about the advt.
Later, when the real landlord came, I heard
sounds of great anger outside my door. The
applicants for the house went in one by one,
and passed out breathing anger. One of them
came into my room to let me know what he
thought of my neighbors, and others stood in
the doorway, listening, and approved. It
appeared that the house-owner was particu-
larly careful about the “selected tenant.” He
took names and addresses, and the names
and addresses of previous landlords, and
inspected rent receipts; but not even the
bribe of a month’s rent in advance would
tempt him to let the place until he had com-
municated with the previous landlords, and
ascertained all about the morals and disposi-
tion of the would-be tenant. One angry
woman snorted: “Wonder he didn't ask to
see me marriage-lines!”
He got his tenant, of course. Also, a few
weeks later, he got a bailiff. I noticed the
shabby, beery-lookifig individual coming in
every morning; and, one day, when my neigh-
bor’s door was locked, he confided to me that
he was trying, without success, to effect an
entrance into the recently-let property, and
had to report every day. Finally, he re-
ported that he had got in and taken posses-
sion of the blinds left on the windows. Care-
ful selection, apparently, is not always as
successful as it should be.
Once I had a landlord who lived next door
to his one letting property. He told me in
an impressive way that an M.P. lived across
the street, next to Blinks, the big softgoods
merchant, and that Alderman Smith resided
on the pther side of the cottage; and he
charged all these things in the rent. He
seemed grieved to hear that I had several
children, but he overlooked it; and he asked
me if I was prepared to sign a lease for
twelve months. The light fashion in which
I assented appeared to grieve him; and he
insisted on reading the document to me
before I signed it. Then he carried his copy
reverently to the safe, and seemed upset at
the offhand manner in which I folded mine
and put it in my pocket.
When the furniture arrived he was on the
spot advising the vanman how to get it in
without scratching the doors. This seemed to
spur the carters to greater efforts, which
wouldn’t have mattered so much only it was
my furniture.
His troubles didn’t cease when the van-
men had gone, because about that time I
started putting up the blinds. It’s a job I
tire of, and when I was Weary of turning
screws I put the fixtures up with nails for
a change. I hadn’t been engaged at the
hammering for long, however, before the
landlord came running in with a gimlet and
screwdriver. He seemed to doubt my word
when I said I had some, and insisted on
leaving his. Later, when the hammering
continued, he called in twice to ask ner-
vously if he could be of any assistance. Even
after that I could see him, with the light at
the back of him, peering anxiously through
the slats of his blinds. I had finished the
curtain-fixing, but now and again, in a spare
moment, I knocked the hammer against the
window-frame just to see the effect. It was
a sport that lasted for several nights.
When I came home next night my wife
said that she was sure the place would not
suit us. I reminded her that a week before
she had been sure that it would suit us, and,
anyway, I had signed a lease; but she said
that previously she had not known that the
landlord was one of the fixtures. It appears
that he called in shortly after I left to re-
mark that young Hamer was using the
pieces of asphalt, where it was broken, as
coal for his trains ; and since then he had
been in half a dozen times, ostensibly to see
what needed to be done to the copper, but
really because he heard hammering in the
kitchen, or because he heard nothing and
wanted to see what it was all about. Later
on he called in to see me, and made, in an
apologetic way, seven complaints about (1)
the children, (2) the dog, (3) the girl, and
(4 to 7) generally.
As days went on my wife became more
convinced that the place would not suit us.
She said that the landlord was a nuisance,
and wasn’t a bit mollified when I told her
I would get the Council’s officer down to in-
spect him and see if he could not be removed.
She declared that, lease or no lease, she was
not going to live in a place where a Jack-in-
the-box lived next door, and his face
appeared over the fence every time the slight-
est noise occurred to see which part. of the
house was being knocked down. She had
choked him off the habit of coming in so
freouently by asking him, one morning, if
he had not better stay to lunch; it would
save him the trouble of coming back after-
wards. My wife can be sarcastic, some-
times. I know. She told me when I came
home one morning, certainly not later than
four, that breakfast was late that morning,
as she had been too tired to wake the girl.
On Sunday I prepared to lay out my
vegetable garden. I have seldom eaten any
vegetables grown by myself, but that is
because I move. I have no doubt other
people have eaten them ; and I like a kitchen
garden. As I prepared the first bed the face
looked over the fence before I was ready
with anything to throw, and said it was
Sunday.
“I know,” I said shortly.
“What are you going to do?” asked y
landlord.
“Lay out a vegetable garden,” I answered.
“You can’t do it!” he protested.
“I can,” I retorted. “I’ve done it, often.”
“But your lease says you can’t. You are
not to remove any of the shrubs or plants in
the garden.”
“I’m not going to remove them,” I said,
“just put them in another place—in a heap
at the end of the garden.”
He groaned, and disappeared; but
shortly was back again, with his lease, and
'he insisted upon reading it over to me. He
seemed ridiculously like a parson reading the
lesson for the day. He was quite reverential
over it. I went on digging, and said
“Amen” at the end of it.
“So you see you can’t change the garden
without my consent,” he said.
“So yon see I can!” I retorted, still
digging.
“The lease says you can't.”
I thought he was going to read it again.
“If I can’t do what I like with my own
garden, I’ll find another house,” I said.
“You can’t. The lease is for twelve
months,” he answered.
“Then, if you object to me doing as I
like here,” I said, “cancel the lease.”
“No,” he answered. “I don’t want to do
anything unpleasant, but if you spoil the
garden I will sue you on the terms of the
lease. Listen!”
And he did start to re-read the lease. I
went inside and left him at it. ,
That evening I agreed with my wife that
the house would not suit us, and I went in
to see my landlord, after tea. and said so.

HAMER.
The Concerto in E-fiat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FAHLBERG’S recollection was both
distinct and vivid. Damn that offi-
cious clod of a conductor, and curse
that infernal first movement with its tempo
ffuisto! On no less than four occasion's dur-
ing the rehearsal had Kestor the Omnisci-
ent assured him that if his execution did not
improve his hard-won reputation Avas
as good as buried. In spite of his sup-
pressed anger Fahlberg was subconsciously
aware of a • defect in himself which re-
fused to be localised. Through some
obscure cause his fingers seemed to have
been recently toughened, and their supple-
ness destroyed as though by hard manual
labor, and his mind was positively torpid.
During the last interval he tried to reason
out this peculiar feeling of mental and
physical deterioration. He observed that
the electric radiance which usually enveloped
the platform in a harsh yellow glare was
replaced by a kind of nebulous greyness
which came from overhead. Then he real-
ised that the greyness Avas merely the day-
light entering through the opened roof. He
could hear a muffled but persistent, roaring
Avhich seemed to be sweeping round jthe
exterior of the concert hall in stormy gusts.
He suddenly stared and listened; for the
twilit hall was being gradually filled with
strange shadowy figures moving away from
the platform towards the distant obscurity
of the main entrance, and every figure seemed
to call to him. He watched in astonishment.
The forms moved silently and with delibera-
tion, and appeared to be emerging from some
opening just above his head*—stepping in-
stantly into visibility, as it were. He began
to suffer from an overpowering desire to
join them. Just as he was about to rise
from his chair, the warning rattle of the
conductor’s baton on the music-stand
abruptly awakened him.
For a short time he played with his
accustomed fiery strength. Then he felt
that cramp was developing in his shoul-
ders. He tried for a few seconds to remove
his attention from the visualised music, and
by sheer will-power flood his arms with new
virility. He knew that he was swaying on
the chair. The piano seemed to be chang-
ing into a squat and shining beast, and
every key he touched gripped his
finger-tips like a. claw. Then the oflicious-
ness of the unspeakable Kestor culminated
in an appalling insult. He leaned over and
smashed his baton on the back of the pian-
ist’s right hand with incredible violence.
For the moment Fahlberg was petrified;
then everything visible dissolved into a
scarlet mist....
Quarter of an hour afterwards a wounded
man was carried out of a shell-crater by a
Red Cross attendant and assisted to the
temporary dressing-station. His face was
blotched with clay, and the expression of his
eyes was that of a somnambulist. Out of
the right sleeve there projected a dirty red
mass of linen which swung restlessly in the
opening.
“Shattered hand, sir,” reported the attend-
ant.
“Ernst!” called the doctor in charge.
“Eh?” replied the second doctor sleepily.
“The right 0ne....0ff at the wrist. Fix
the stump for him, will you ?.. .. God ! Who
is this man? I know his face.”
The second doctor gazed dreamily at the
erect figure for several moments.
“Where have I seen you before?” he
mumbled jerkily. “Where have.... Heaven !
It is Erik Fahlberg, the pianist!”
Then he realised the significance of the
lost hand. “I am sorry,” he said gently,
and motioned to the attendant to remove the
man’s jacket.
The wounded man made no reply and con-
tinued to stare fixedly in front of him. The
doctor took him by the left arm and led him
to the chair beside an oilcloth-covered bench.
He lifted the right arm, laid it flat on the
cloth, and commenced to unloosen the foul
bandage.
“Shock, too!” commented the senior doc-
tor, glancing across the limp body of an
amputation case which was being hoisted
from the floor. “Give him a mouthful of
No. 12.” Then he addressed his own patient,
who had been awakened into consciousness
by naked contact with cold oilcloth:
“Gently, my son,” he said soothingly.
“In three minutes you will be sleeping like
a child. Another cylinder, Emil!”
The noise outside the cottage increased in
violence. The intermittent spattering of
leaden rain against the eastern wall became
merged into a continuous staccato rattling.
Little puffs of plaster spurted from betweeii
the tormented bricks until the air was turbid
with floating whiteness. Overhead, the
scarred and splintered beams shook and
swayed against the open sky, and the criss-
crossed shadows flung down on the dirty
floor moved weirdly from side to side like a
drunken trellis. The attendant picked up a
heap of cut and torn uniforms and pushed
it out through the shattered window. He
suddenly fell backwards to the floor as if
twitched by an invisible wire, and lay still.
“Fool!” bellowed the stout senior. “Did
I not tell you to keep below the ledge? Now
I have but one assistant—one assistant, and
fifty men in agony! God in Heaven! May
the Devil reject you—you thoughtless
swine!”
A young man lying on the floor behind
him commenced to groan chokingly. The
doctor turned swiftly and lightly touched the
distorted face. “To-morrow night,” he said
briskly, “the hospital train will arrive at
Munich, and a proud mother will meet her
brave son. So!” And the dying boy Avas
comforted.
Just above the tangle of beams and iron-
work overhead something burst into a white
cloud and sprayed the interior of the cottage
with whirring splinters. The junior doctor
slid sideways to the floor and rolled under
the bench, and his senior collapsed like a
deflated figure and fell across the unconscious
body of his patient.
Fahlberg rose from the chair, stood facing
the door, and then strode across and
flung it open. He returned to the chair,
dragged it a few inches back from the bench
and sat down calmly. A wounded sapper
over near the door attempted to rise to his
feet, and in so doing displaced a box of
bandages, which fell with a crash. Fahl-
berg swung round, glared at the offender and
rapped sharply against the bench with the
knuckles of his left hand.
“Silence, dog!” he shouted angrily, and
the sapper lay still.
Along the edge of the bench was a long
crimson stain which seemed to fascinate the
seated man. He suddenly straightened his
body and extended his left hand with its
long, febrile fingers. The fingers sank down-
wards, struck the smeared edge, and raced
along to the left like the legs of a running
spider; whilst the right arm, with its drip-
ping wrist, followed closely behind. Upwards
and downwards along that monstrous ke.\ -
board moved the two hands—the one throb-
bing with life and strength, the other long
dead and invisible.
The sapper near the door stared for a
time in amazement at this dreadful recital,
and then watched with horror.
When the end came, Fahlberg twirled the
chair from beneath him with his left hand
and walked across to the doorway. He stood
there for a moment, enraptured., by the
terrific applause of bursting melinite. Then
he boAved to fhe audience of Death but in the
open, and fell on his face Avith a bullet
through his brain.
